I mean-- Course the folks there are the best\non earth, but, gee whiz, those Main Street burgs are slow, and you\nfellows can\xe2\x80\x99t hardly appreciate what it means to be here with a bunch of\nlive ones!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou bet!\xe2\x80\x9d exulted Orville Jones. \xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re the best folks on earth,\nthose small-town folks, but, oh, mama! what conversation! Why, say, they\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t talk about anything but the weather and the ne-oo Ford, by\nheckalorum!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s right. They all talk about just the same things,\xe2\x80\x9d said Eddie\nSwanson.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t they, though! They just say the same things over and over,\xe2\x80\x9d said\nVergil Gunch.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it\xe2\x80\x99s really remarkable. They seem to lack all power of looking at\nthings impersonally. They simply go over and over the same talk about\nFords and the weather and so on,\xe2\x80\x9d said Howard Littlefield.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cStill, at that, you can\xe2\x80\x99t blame \xe2\x80\x99em. They haven\xe2\x80\x99t got any intellectual\nstimulus such as you get up here in the city,\xe2\x80\x9d said Chum Frink.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGosh, that\xe2\x80\x99s right,\xe2\x80\x9d said Babbitt. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want you highbrows to get\nstuck on yourselves but I must say it keeps a fellow right up on his\ntoes to sit in with a poet and with Howard, the guy that put the con in\neconomics! But these small-town boobs, with nobody but each other to\ntalk to, no wonder they get so sloppy and uncultured in their speech,\nand so balled-up in their thinking!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nOrville Jones commented, \xe2\x80\x9cAnd, then take our other advantages--the\nmovies, frinstance. These Yapville sports think they\xe2\x80\x99re all-get-out if\nthey have one change of bill a week, where here in the city you got your\nchoice of a dozen diff\xe2\x80\x99rent movies any evening you want to name!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSure, and the inspiration we get from rubbing up against high-class\nhustlers every day and getting jam full of ginger,\xe2\x80\x9d said Eddie Swanson.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSame time,\xe2\x80\x9d said Babbitt, \xe2\x80\x9cno sense excusing these rube burgs too easy.\nFellow\xe2\x80\x99s own fault if he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t show the initiative to up and beat it\nto the city, like we done--did. And, just speaking in confidence among\nfriends, they\xe2\x80\x99re jealous as the devil of a city man. Every time I go up\nto Catawba I have to go around apologizing to the fellows I was brought\nup with because I\xe2\x80\x99ve more or less succeeded and they haven\xe2\x80\x99t. And if you\ntalk natural to \xe2\x80\x99em, way we do here, and show finesse and what you might\ncall a broad point of view, why, they think you\xe2\x80\x99re putting on side.\nThere\xe2\x80\x99s my own half-brother Martin--runs the little ole general store my\nDad used to keep. Say, I\xe2\x80\x99ll bet he don\xe2\x80\x99t know there is such a thing as a\nTux--as a dinner-jacket. If he was to come in here now, he\xe2\x80\x99d think we\nwere a bunch of--of-- Why, gosh, I swear, he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t know what to think!\nYes, sir, they\xe2\x80\x99re jealous!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nChum Frink agreed, \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s so. But what I mind is their lack of culture\nand appreciation of the Beautiful--if you\xe2\x80\x99ll excuse me for being\nhighbrow. Now, I like to give a high-class lecture, and read some of my\nbest poetry--not the newspaper stuff but the magazine things. But say,\nwhen I get out in the tall grass, there\xe2\x80\x99s nothing will take but a lot of\ncheesy old stories and slang and junk that if any of us were to indulge\nin it here, he\xe2\x80\x99d get the gate so fast it would make his head swim.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nVergil Gunch summed it up: \xe2\x80\x9cFact is, we\xe2\x80\x99re mighty lucky to be living\namong a bunch of city-folks, that recognize artistic things and\nbusiness-punch equally. We\xe2\x80\x99d feel pretty glum if we got stuck in some\nMain Street burg and tried to wise up the old codgers to the kind of\nlife we\xe2\x80\x99re used to here. But, by golly, there\xe2\x80\x99s this you got to say for\n\xe2\x80\x99em: Every small American town is trying to get population and modern\nideals. And darn if a lot of \xe2\x80\x99em don\xe2\x80\x99t put it across! Somebody starts\npanning a rube crossroads, telling how he was there in 1900 and it\nconsisted of one muddy street, count \xe2\x80\x99em, one, and nine hundred human\nclams. Well, you go back there in 1920, and you find pavements and a\nswell little hotel and a first-class ladies\xe2\x80\x99 ready-to-wear shop--real\nperfection, in fact! You don\xe2\x80\x99t want to just look at what these small\ntowns are, you want to look at what they\xe2\x80\x99re aiming to become, and they\nall got an ambition that in the long run is going to make \xe2\x80\x99em the finest\nspots on earth--they all want to be just like Zenith!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\nIII\n\nHowever intimate they might be with T. Cholmondeley Frink as a neighbor,\nas a borrower of lawn-mowers and monkey-wrenches, they knew that he was\nalso a Famous Poet and a distinguished advertising-agent; that behind\nhis easiness were sultry literary mysteries which they could not\npenetrate. But to-night, in the gin-evolved confidence, he admitted them\nto the arcanum:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve got a literary problem that\xe2\x80\x99s worrying me to death. I\xe2\x80\x99m doing a\nseries of ads for the Zeeco Car and I want to make each of \xe2\x80\x99em a real\nlittle gem--reg\xe2\x80\x99lar stylistic stuff. I\xe2\x80\x99m all for this theory that\nperfection is the stunt, or nothing at all, and these are as tough\nthings as I ever tackled. You might think it\xe2\x80\x99d be harder to do my\npoems--all these Heart Topics: home and fireside and happiness--but\nthey\xe2\x80\x99re cinches. You can\xe2\x80\x99t go wrong on \xe2\x80\x99em; you know what sentiments any\ndecent go-ahead fellow must have if he plays the game, and you stick\nright to \xe2\x80\x99em. But the poetry of industrialism, now there\xe2\x80\x99s a literary\nline where you got to open up new territory. Do you know the fellow\nwho\xe2\x80\x99s really _the_ American genius? The fellow who you don\xe2\x80\x99t know his\nname and I don\xe2\x80\x99t either, but his work ought to be preserved so\xe2\x80\x99s future\ngenerations can judge our American thought and originality to-day? Why,\nthe fellow that writes the Prince Albert Tobacco ads! Just listen to\nthis:\n\n     It\xe2\x80\x99s P.A. that jams such joy in jimmy pipes. Say--bet you\xe2\x80\x99ve often\n     bent-an-ear to that spill-of-speech about hopping from five to\n     f-i-f-t-y p-e-r by \xe2\x80\x9cstepping on her a bit!\xe2\x80\x9d Guess that\xe2\x80\x99s going\n     some, all right--BUT--just among ourselves, you better start a\n     rapidwhiz system to keep tabs as to how fast you\xe2\x80\x99ll buzz from low\n     smoke spirits to _tip-top-high_--once you line up behind a jimmy\n     pipe that\xe2\x80\x99s all aglow with that peach-of-a-pal, Prince Albert.\n\n     Prince Albert is john-on-the-job--always joy\xe2\x80\x99usly more-_ish_ in\n     flavor; always delightfully cool and fragrant! For a fact, you\n     never hooked such double-decked, copper-riveted, two-fisted smoke\n     enjoyment!\n\n     Go to a pipe--speed-o-quick like you light on a good thing!\n     Why--packed with Prince Albert you can play a joy\xe2\x80\x99us jimmy straight\n     across the boards! _And you know what that means!_\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow that,\xe2\x80\x9d caroled the motor agent, Eddie Swanson, \xe2\x80\x9cthat\xe2\x80\x99s what I call\nhe-literature! That Prince Albert fellow--though, gosh, there can\xe2\x80\x99t be\njust one fellow that writes \xe2\x80\x99em; must be a big board of classy\nink-slingers in conference, but anyway: now, him, he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t write for\nlong-haired pikers, he writes for Regular Guys, he writes for _me_, and\nI tip my benny to him! The only thing is: I wonder if it sells the\ngoods? Course, like all these poets, this Prince Albert fellow lets his\nidea run away with him. It makes elegant reading, but it don\xe2\x80\x99t say\nnothing. I\xe2\x80\x99d never go out and buy Prince Albert Tobacco after reading\nit, because it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t tell me anything about the stuff. It\xe2\x80\x99s just a\nbunch of fluff.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nFrink faced him: \xe2\x80\x9cOh, you\xe2\x80\x99re crazy! Have I got to sell you the idea of\nStyle? Anyway, that\xe2\x80\x99s the kind of stuff I\xe2\x80\x99d like to do for the Zeeco.\nBut I simply can\xe2\x80\x99t. So I decided to stick to the straight poetic, and I\ntook a shot at a highbrow ad for the Zeeco. How do you like this:\n\n     The long white trail is calling--calling--and it\xe2\x80\x99s over the hills\n     and far away for every man or woman that has red blood in his veins\n     and on his lips the ancient song of the buccaneers. It\xe2\x80\x99s away with\n     dull drudging, and a fig for care. Speed--glorious Speed--it\xe2\x80\x99s more\n     than just a moment\xe2\x80\x99s exhilaration--it\xe2\x80\x99s Life for you and me! This\n     great new truth the makers of the Zeeco Car have considered as much\n     as price and style. It\xe2\x80\x99s fleet as the antelope, smooth as the glide\n     of a swallow, yet powerful as the charge of a bull-elephant. Class\n     breathes in every line. Listen, brother! You\xe2\x80\x99ll never know what the\n     high art of hiking is till you TRY LIFE\xe2\x80\x99S ZIPPINGEST ZEST--THE\n     ZEECO!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d Frink mused, \xe2\x80\x9cthat\xe2\x80\x99s got an elegant color to it, if I do say so,\nbut it ain\xe2\x80\x99t got the originality of \xe2\x80\x98spill-of-speech!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThe whole company sighed with sympathy and admiration.\n\n\n\n\nCHAPTER IX\n\n\nI\n\nBabbitt was fond of his friends, he loved the importance of being host\nand shouting, \xe2\x80\x9cCertainly, you\xe2\x80\x99re going to have smore chicken--the idea!\xe2\x80\x9d\nand he appreciated the genius of T. Cholmondeley Frink, but the vigor of\nthe cocktails was gone, and the more he ate the less joyful he felt.\nThen the amity of the dinner was destroyed by the nagging of the\nSwansons.\n\nIn Floral Heights and the other prosperous sections of Zenith,\nespecially in the \xe2\x80\x9cyoung married set,\xe2\x80\x9d there were many women who had\nnothing to do. Though they had few servants, yet with gas stoves,\nelectric ranges and dish-washers and vacuum cleaners, and tiled kitchen\nwalls, their houses were so convenient that they had little housework,\nand much of their food came from bakeries and delicatessens. They had\nbut two, one, or no children; and despite the myth that the Great War\nhad made work respectable, their husbands objected to their \xe2\x80\x9cwasting\ntime and getting a lot of crank ideas\xe2\x80\x9d in unpaid social work, and still\nmore to their causing a rumor, by earning money, that they were not\nadequately supported. They worked perhaps two hours a day, and the rest\nof the time they ate chocolates, went to the motion-pictures, went\nwindow-shopping, went in gossiping twos and threes to card-parties, read\nmagazines, thought timorously of the lovers who never appeared, and\naccumulated a splendid restlessness which they got rid of by nagging\ntheir husbands. The husbands nagged back.\n\nOf these naggers the Swansons were perfect specimens.\n\nThroughout the dinner Eddie Swanson had been complaining, publicly,\nabout his wife\xe2\x80\x99s new frock. It was, he submitted, too short, too low,\ntoo immodestly thin, and much too expensive. He appealed to Babbitt:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHonest, George, what do you think of that rag Louetta went and bought?\nDon\xe2\x80\x99t you think it\xe2\x80\x99s the limit?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s eating you, Eddie? I call it a swell little dress.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it is, Mr. Swanson. It\xe2\x80\x99s a sweet frock,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Babbitt protested.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere now, do you see, smarty! You\xe2\x80\x99re such an authority on clothes!\xe2\x80\x9d\nLouetta raged, while the guests ruminated and peeped at her shoulders.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s all right now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Swanson. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m authority enough so I know it\nwas a waste of money, and it makes me tired to see you not wearing out a\nwhole closetful of clothes you got already. I\xe2\x80\x99ve expressed my idea about\nthis before, and you know good and well you didn\xe2\x80\x99t pay the least bit of\nattention. I have to camp on your trail to get you to do anything--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThere was much more of it, and they all assisted, all but Babbitt.\nEverything about him was dim except his stomach, and that was a bright\nscarlet disturbance. \xe2\x80\x9cHad too much grub; oughtn\xe2\x80\x99t to eat this stuff,\xe2\x80\x9d he\ngroaned--while he went on eating, while he gulped down a chill and\nglutinous slice of the ice-cream brick, and cocoanut cake as oozy as\nshaving-cream. He felt as though he had been stuffed with clay; his body\nwas bursting, his throat was bursting, his brain was hot mud; and only\nwith agony did he continue to smile and shout as became a host on Floral\nHeights.\n\nHe would, except for his guests, have fled outdoors and walked off the\nintoxication of food, but in the haze which filled the room they sat\nforever, talking, talking, while he agonized, \xe2\x80\x9cDarn fool to be eating\nall this--not \xe2\x80\x99nother mouthful,\xe2\x80\x9d and discovered that he was again\ntasting the sickly welter of melted ice cream on his plate. There was no\nmagic in his friends; he was not uplifted when Howard Littlefield\nproduced from his treasure-house of scholarship the information that\nthe chemical symbol for raw rubber is C\xe2\x82\x81\xe2\x82\x80H\xe2\x82\x81\xe2\x82\x86, which turns into\nisoprene, or 2C\xe2\x82\x85H\xe2\x82\x88. Suddenly, without precedent, Babbitt was not merely\nbored but admitting that he was bored. It was ecstasy to escape from the\ntable, from the torture of a straight chair, and loll on the davenport\nin the living-room.\n\nThe others, from their fitful unconvincing talk, their expressions of\nbeing slowly and painfully smothered, seemed to be suffering from the\ntoil of social life and the horror of good food as much as himself. All\nof them accepted with relief the suggestion of bridge.\n\nBabbitt recovered from the feeling of being boiled. He won at bridge. He\nwas again able to endure Vergil Gunch\xe2\x80\x99s inexorable heartiness. But he\npictured loafing with Paul Riesling beside a lake in Maine. It was as\noverpowering and imaginative as homesickness. He had never seen Maine,\nyet he beheld the shrouded mountains, the tranquil lake of evening.\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat boy Paul\xe2\x80\x99s worth all these ballyhooing highbrows put together,\xe2\x80\x9d he\nmuttered; and, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d like to get away from--everything.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nEven Louetta Swanson did not rouse him.\n\nMrs. Swanson was pretty and pliant. Babbitt was not an analyst of women,\nexcept as to their tastes in Furnished Houses to Rent. He divided them\ninto Real Ladies, Working Women, Old Cranks, and Fly Chickens. He mooned\nover their charms but he was of opinion that all of them (save the women\nof his own family) were \xe2\x80\x9cdifferent\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cmysterious.\xe2\x80\x9d Yet he had known\nby instinct that Louetta Swanson could be approached. Her eyes and lips\nwere moist. Her face tapered from a broad forehead to a pointed chin,\nher mouth was thin but strong and avid, and between her brows were two\noutcurving and passionate wrinkles. She was thirty, perhaps, or younger.\nGossip had never touched her, but every man naturally and instantly rose\nto flirtatiousness when he spoke to her, and every woman watched her\nwith stilled blankness.\n\nBetween games, sitting on the davenport, Babbitt spoke to her with the\nrequisite gallantry, that sonorous Floral Heights gallantry which is\nnot flirtation but a terrified flight from it:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re looking like a new soda-fountain to-night, Louetta.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAm I?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOle Eddie kind of on the rampage.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. I get so sick of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, when you get tired of hubby, you can run off with Uncle George.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I ran away-- Oh, well--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnybody ever tell you your hands are awful pretty?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nShe looked down at them, she pulled the lace of her sleeves over them,\nbut otherwise she did not heed him. She was lost in unexpressed\nimaginings.\n\nBabbitt was too languid this evening to pursue his duty of being a\ncaptivating (though strictly moral) male. He ambled back to the\nbridge-tables. He was not much thrilled when Mrs. Frink, a small\ntwittering woman, proposed that they \xe2\x80\x9ctry and do some spiritualism and\ntable-tipping--you know Chum can make the spirits come--honest, he just\nscares me!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThe ladies of the party had not emerged all evening, but now, as the sex\ngiven to things of the spirit while the men warred against base things\nmaterial, they took command and cried, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, let\xe2\x80\x99s!\xe2\x80\x9d In the dimness the\nmen were rather solemn and foolish, but the goodwives quivered and\nadored as they sat about the table. They laughed, \xe2\x80\x9cNow, you be good or\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell!\xe2\x80\x9d when the men took their hands in the circle.\n\nBabbitt tingled with a slight return of interest in life as Louetta\nSwanson\xe2\x80\x99s hand closed on his with quiet firmness.\n\nAll of them hunched over, intent. They startled as some one drew a\nstrained breath. In the dusty light from the hall they looked unreal,\nthey felt disembodied. Mrs. Gunch squeaked, and they jumped with\nunnatural jocularity, but at Frink\xe2\x80\x99s hiss they sank into subdued awe.\nSuddenly, incredibly, they heard a knocking. They stared at Frink\xe2\x80\x99s\nhalf-revealed hands and found them lying still. They wriggled, and\npretended not to be impressed.\n\nFrink spoke with gravity: \xe2\x80\x9cIs some one there?\xe2\x80\x9d A thud. \xe2\x80\x9cIs one knock to\nbe the sign for \xe2\x80\x98yes\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d A thud. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd two for \xe2\x80\x98no\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d A thud.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, ladies and gentlemen, shall we ask the guide to put us into\ncommunication with the spirit of some great one passed over?\xe2\x80\x9d Frink\nmumbled.\n\nMrs. Orville Jones begged, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, let\xe2\x80\x99s talk to Dante! We studied him at\nthe Reading Circle. You know who he was, Orvy.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly I know who he was! The Wop poet. Where do you think I was\nraised?\xe2\x80\x9d from her insulted husband.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSure--the fellow that took the Cook\xe2\x80\x99s Tour to Hell. I\xe2\x80\x99ve never waded\nthrough his po\xe2\x80\x99try, but we learned about him in the U.,\xe2\x80\x9d said Babbitt.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cPage Mr. Dannnnnty!\xe2\x80\x9d intoned Eddie Swanson.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou ought to get him easy, Mr. Frink, you and he being fellow-poets,\xe2\x80\x9d\nsaid Louetta Swanson.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cFellow-poets, rats! Where d\xe2\x80\x99 you get that stuff?\xe2\x80\x9d protested Vergil\nGunch. \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose Dante showed a lot of speed for an old-timer--not that\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve actually read him, of course--but to come right down to hard facts,\nhe wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t stand one-two-three if he had to buckle down to practical\nliterature and turn out a poem for the newspaper-syndicate every day,\nlike Chum does!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s so,\xe2\x80\x9d from Eddie Swanson. \xe2\x80\x9cThose old birds could take their time.\nJudas Priest, I could write poetry myself if I had a whole year for it,\nand just wrote about that old-fashioned junk like Dante wrote about.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nFrink demanded, \xe2\x80\x9cHush, now! I\xe2\x80\x99ll call him.... O, Laughing Eyes, emerge\nforth into the, uh, the ultimates and bring hither the spirit of Dante,\nthat we mortals may list to his words of wisdom.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou forgot to give um the address: 1658 Brimstone Avenue, Fiery\nHeights, Hell,\xe2\x80\x9d Gunch chuckled, but the others felt that this was\nirreligious. And besides--\xe2\x80\x9cprobably it was just Chum making the knocks,\nbut still, if there did happen to be something to all this, be exciting\nto talk to an old fellow belonging to--way back in early times--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nA thud. The spirit of Dante had come to the parlor of George F. Babbitt.\n\nHe was, it seemed, quite ready to answer their questions. He was \xe2\x80\x9cglad\nto be with them, this evening.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nFrink spelled out the messages by running through the alphabet till the\nspirit interpreter knocked at the right letter.\n\nLittlefield asked, in a learned tone, \xe2\x80\x9cDo you like it in the Paradiso,\nMessire?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are very happy on the higher plane, Signor. We are glad that you are\nstudying this great truth of spiritualism,\xe2\x80\x9d Dante replied.\n\nThe circle moved with an awed creaking of stays and shirtfronts.\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuppose--suppose there were something to this?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBabbitt had a different worry. \xe2\x80\x9cSuppose Chum Frink was really one of\nthese spiritualists! Chum had, for a literary fellow, always seemed to\nbe a Regular Guy; he belonged to the Chatham Road Presbyterian Church\nand went to the Boosters\xe2\x80\x99 lunches and liked cigars and motors and racy\nstories. But suppose that secretly-- After all, you never could tell\nabout these darn highbrows; and to be an out-and-out spiritualist would\nbe almost like being a socialist!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nNo one could long be serious in the presence of Vergil Gunch. \xe2\x80\x9cAsk Dant\xe2\x80\x99\nhow Jack Shakespeare and old Verg\xe2\x80\x99--the guy they named after me--are\ngettin\xe2\x80\x99 along, and don\xe2\x80\x99t they wish they could get into the movie game!\xe2\x80\x9d\nhe blared, and instantly all was mirth. Mrs. Jones shrieked, and Eddie\nSwanson desired to know whether Dante didn\xe2\x80\x99t catch cold with nothing on\nbut his wreath.\n\nThe pleased Dante made humble answer.\n\nBut Babbitt--the curst discontent was torturing him again, and heavily,\nin the impersonal darkness, he pondered, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t-- We\xe2\x80\x99re all so flip\nand think we\xe2\x80\x99re so smart. There\xe2\x80\x99d be-- A fellow like Dante-- I wish I\xe2\x80\x99d\nread some of his pieces. I don\xe2\x80\x99t suppose I ever will, now.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe had, without explanation, the impression of a slaggy cliff and on it,\nin silhouette against menacing clouds, a lone and austere figure. He was\ndismayed by a sudden contempt for his surest friends. He grasped Louetta\nSwanson\xe2\x80\x99s hand, and found the comfort of human warmth. Habit came, a\nveteran warrior; and he shook himself. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat the deuce is the matter\nwith me, this evening?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe patted Louetta\xe2\x80\x99s hand, to indicate that he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t meant anything\nimproper by squeezing it, and demanded of Frink, \xe2\x80\x9cSay, see if you can\nget old Dant\xe2\x80\x99 to spiel us some of his poetry. Talk up to him. Tell him,\n\xe2\x80\x98_Buena giorna, se\xc3\xb1or, com sa va, wie geht\xe2\x80\x99s? Keskersaykersa_ a little\npome, _se\xc3\xb1or_?\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\nII\n\nThe lights were switched on; the women sat on the fronts of their chairs\nin that determined suspense whereby a wife indicates that as soon as the\npresent speaker has finished, she is going to remark brightly to her\nhusband, \xe2\x80\x9cWell, dear, I think per-_haps_ it\xe2\x80\x99s about time for us to be\nsaying good-night.\xe2\x80\x9d For once Babbitt did not break out in blustering\nefforts to keep the party going. He had--there was something he wished\nto think out-- But the psychical research had started them off again.\n(\xe2\x80\x9cWhy didn\xe2\x80\x99t they go home! Why didn\xe2\x80\x99t they go home!\xe2\x80\x9d) Though he was\nimpressed by the profundity of the statement, he was only\nhalf-enthusiastic when Howard Littlefield lectured, \xe2\x80\x9cThe United States\nis the only nation in which the government is a Moral Ideal and not just\na social arrangement.\xe2\x80\x9d (\xe2\x80\x9cTrue--true--weren\xe2\x80\x99t they _ever_ going home?\xe2\x80\x9d)\nHe was usually delighted to have an \xe2\x80\x9cinside view\xe2\x80\x9d of the momentous world\nof motors but to-night he scarcely listened to Eddie Swanson\xe2\x80\x99s\nrevelation: \xe2\x80\x9cIf you want to go above the Javelin class, the Zeeco is a\nmighty good buy. Couple weeks ago, and mind you, this was a fair,\nsquare test, they took a Zeeco stock touring-car and they slid up the\nTonawanda hill on high, and fellow told me--\xe2\x80\x9d (\xe2\x80\x9cZeeco--good boat\nbut-- Were they planning to stay all night?\xe2\x80\x9d)\n\nThey really were going, with a flutter of \xe2\x80\x9cWe did have the best time!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nMost aggressively friendly of all was Babbitt, yet as he burbled he was\nreflecting, \xe2\x80\x9cI got through it, but for a while there I didn\xe2\x80\x99t hardly\nthink I\xe2\x80\x99d last out.\xe2\x80\x9d He prepared to taste that most delicate pleasure of\nthe host: making fun of his guests in the relaxation of midnight. As the\ndoor closed he yawned voluptuously, chest out, shoulders wriggling, and\nturned cynically to his wife.\n\nShe was beaming. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, it was nice, wasn\xe2\x80\x99t it! I know they enjoyed every\nminute of it. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you think so?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe couldn\xe2\x80\x99t do it. He couldn\xe2\x80\x99t mock. It would have been like sneering at\na happy child. He lied ponderously: \xe2\x80\x9cYou bet! Best party this year, by a\nlong shot.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWasn\xe2\x80\x99t the dinner good! And honestly I thought the fried chicken was\ndelicious!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou bet! Fried to the Queen\xe2\x80\x99s taste. Best fried chicken I\xe2\x80\x99ve tasted for\na coon\xe2\x80\x99s age.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDidn\xe2\x80\x99t Matilda fry it beautifully! And don\xe2\x80\x99t you think the soup was\nsimply delicious?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt certainly was! It was corking! Best soup I\xe2\x80\x99ve tasted since Heck was\na pup!\xe2\x80\x9d But his voice was seeping away. They stood in the hall, under\nthe electric light in its square box-like shade of red glass bound with\nnickel. She stared at him.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, George, you don\xe2\x80\x99t sound--you sound as if you hadn\xe2\x80\x99t really enjoyed\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSure I did! Course I did!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGeorge! What is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99m kind of tired, I guess. Been pounding pretty hard at the\noffice. Need to get away and rest up a little.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, we\xe2\x80\x99re going to Maine in just a few weeks now, dear.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYuh--\xe2\x80\x9d Then he was pouring it out nakedly, robbed of reticence. \xe2\x80\x9cMyra:\nI think it\xe2\x80\x99d be a good thing for me to get up there early.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you have this man you have to meet in New York about business.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat man? Oh, sure. Him. Oh, that\xe2\x80\x99s all off. But I want to hit Maine\nearly--get in a little fishing, catch me a big trout, by golly!\xe2\x80\x9d A\nnervous, artificial laugh.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, why don\xe2\x80\x99t we do it? Verona and Matilda can run the house between\nthem, and you and I can go any time, if you think we can afford it.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut that\xe2\x80\x99s-- I\xe2\x80\x99ve been feeling so jumpy lately, I thought maybe it might\nbe a good thing if I kind of got off by myself and sweat it out of me.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGeorge! Don\xe2\x80\x99t you _want_ me to go along?\xe2\x80\x9d She was too wretchedly in\nearnest to be tragic, or gloriously insulted, or anything save dumpy and\ndefenseless and flushed to the red steaminess of a boiled beet.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course I do! I just meant--\xe2\x80\x9d Remembering that Paul Riesling had\npredicted this, he was as desperate as she. \xe2\x80\x9cI mean, sometimes it\xe2\x80\x99s a\ngood thing for an old grouch like me to go off and get it out of his\nsystem.\xe2\x80\x9d He tried to sound paternal. \xe2\x80\x9cThen when you and the kids\narrive-- I figured maybe I might skip up to Maine just a few days ahead\nof you-- I\xe2\x80\x99d be ready for a real bat, see how I mean?\xe2\x80\x9d He coaxed her with\nlarge booming sounds, with affable smiles, like a popular preacher\nblessing an Easter congregation, like a humorous lecturer completing his\nstint of eloquence, like all perpetrators of masculine wiles.\n\nShe stared at him, the joy of festival drained from her face. \xe2\x80\x9cDo I\nbother you when we go on vacations? Don\xe2\x80\x99t I add anything to your fun?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe broke. Suddenly, dreadfully, he was hysterical, he was a yelping\nbaby. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, yes, yes! Hell, yes! But can\xe2\x80\x99t you understand I\xe2\x80\x99m shot to\npieces? I\xe2\x80\x99m all in! I got to take care of myself! I tell you, I got\nto-- I\xe2\x80\x99m sick of everything and everybody! I got to--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nIt was she who was mature and protective now. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, of course! You shall\nrun off by yourself! Why don\xe2\x80\x99t you get Paul to go along, and you boys\njust fish and have a good time?\xe2\x80\x9d She patted his shoulder--reaching up to\nit--while he shook with palsied helplessness, and in that moment was not\nmerely by habit fond of her but clung to her strength.\n\nShe cried cheerily, \xe2\x80\x9cNow up-stairs you go, and pop into bed. We\xe2\x80\x99ll fix\nit all up. I\xe2\x80\x99ll see to the doors. Now skip!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nFor many minutes, for many hours, for a bleak eternity, he lay awake,\nshivering, reduced to primitive terror, comprehending that he had won\nfreedom, and wondering what he could do with anything so unknown and so\nembarrassing as freedom.\n\n\n\n\nCHAPTER X\n\n\nI\n\nNo apartment-house in Zenith had more resolutely experimented in\ncondensation than the Revelstoke Arms, in which Paul and Zilla Riesling\nhad a flat. By sliding the beds into low closets the bedrooms were\nconverted into living-rooms. The kitchens were cupboards each containing\nan electric range, a copper sink, a glass refrigerator, and, very\nintermittently, a Balkan maid. Everything about the Arms was excessively\nmodern, and everything was compressed--except the garages.\n\nThe Babbitts were calling on the Rieslings at the Arms. It was a\nspeculative venture to call on the Rieslings; interesting and sometimes\ndisconcerting. Zilla was an active, strident, full-blown, high-bosomed\nblonde. When she condescended to be good-humored she was nervously\namusing. Her comments on people were saltily satiric and penetrative of\naccepted hypocrisies. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s so!\xe2\x80\x9d you said, and looked sheepish. She\ndanced wildly, and called on the world to be merry, but in the midst of\nit she would turn indignant. She was always becoming indignant. Life was\na plot against her, and she exposed it furiously.\n\nShe was affable to-night. She merely hinted that Orville Jones wore a\ntoup\xc3\xa9, that Mrs. T. Cholmondeley Frink\xe2\x80\x99s singing resembled a Ford going\ninto high, and that the Hon. Otis Deeble, mayor of Zenith and candidate\nfor Congress, was a flatulent fool (which was quite true). The Babbitts\nand Rieslings sat doubtfully on stone-hard brocade chairs in the small\nliving-room of the flat, with its mantel unprovided with a fireplace,\nand its strip of heavy gilt fabric upon a glaring new player-piano, till\nMrs. Riesling shrieked, \xe2\x80\x9cCome on! Let\xe2\x80\x99s put some pep in it! Get out\nyour fiddle, Paul, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll try to make Georgie dance decently.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThe Babbitts were in earnest. They were plotting for the escape to\nMaine. But when Mrs. Babbitt hinted with plump smilingness, \xe2\x80\x9cDoes Paul\nget as tired after the winter\xe2\x80\x99s work as Georgie does?\xe2\x80\x9d then Zilla\nremembered an injury; and when Zilla Riesling remembered an injury the\nworld stopped till something had been done about it.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes he get tired? No, he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t get tired, he just goes crazy, that\xe2\x80\x99s\nall! You think Paul is so reasonable, oh, yes, and he loves to make out\nhe\xe2\x80\x99s a little lamb, but he\xe2\x80\x99s stubborn as a mule. Oh, if you had to live\nwith him--! You\xe2\x80\x99d find out how sweet he is! He just pretends to be meek\nso he can have his own way. And me, I get the credit for being a\nterrible old crank, but if I didn\xe2\x80\x99t blow up once in a while and get\nsomething started, we\xe2\x80\x99d die of dry-rot. He never wants to go any place\nand-- Why, last evening, just because the car was out of order--and that\nwas his fault, too, because he ought to have taken it to the\nservice-station and had the battery looked at--and he didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to go\ndown to the movies on the trolley. But we went, and then there was one\nof those impudent conductors, and Paul wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t do a thing.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was standing on the platform waiting for the people to let me into\nthe car, and this beast, this conductor, hollered at me, \xe2\x80\x98Come on, you,\nmove up!\xe2\x80\x99 Why, I\xe2\x80\x99ve never had anybody speak to me that way in all my\nlife! I was so astonished I just turned to him and said-- I thought there\nmust be some mistake, and so I said to him, perfectly pleasant, \xe2\x80\x98Were\nyou speaking to me?\xe2\x80\x99 and he went on and bellowed at me, \xe2\x80\x98Yes, I was!\nYou\xe2\x80\x99re keeping the whole car from starting!\xe2\x80\x99 he said, and then I saw he\nwas one of these dirty ill-bred hogs that kindness is wasted on, and so\nI stopped and looked right at him, and I said, \xe2\x80\x98I--beg--your--pardon, I\nam not doing anything of the kind,\xe2\x80\x99 I said, \xe2\x80\x98it\xe2\x80\x99s the people ahead of\nme, who won\xe2\x80\x99t move up,\xe2\x80\x99 I said, \xe2\x80\x98and furthermore, let me tell you,\nyoung man, that you\xe2\x80\x99re a low-down, foul-mouthed, impertinent skunk,\xe2\x80\x99 I\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x98and you\xe2\x80\x99re no gentleman! I certainly intend to report you, and\nwe\xe2\x80\x99ll see,\xe2\x80\x99 I said, \xe2\x80\x98whether a lady is to be insulted by any drunken bum\nthat chooses to put on a ragged uniform, and I\xe2\x80\x99d thank you,\xe2\x80\x99 I said, \xe2\x80\x98to\nkeep your filthy abuse to yourself.\xe2\x80\x99 And then I waited for Paul to show\nhe was half a man and come to my defense, and he just stood there and\npretended he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t heard a word, and so I said to him, \xe2\x80\x98Well,\xe2\x80\x99 I\nsaid--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, cut it, cut it, Zill!\xe2\x80\x9d Paul groaned. \xe2\x80\x9cWe all know I\xe2\x80\x99m a\nmollycoddle, and you\xe2\x80\x99re a tender bud, and let\xe2\x80\x99s let it go at that.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet it go?\xe2\x80\x9d Zilla\xe2\x80\x99s face was wrinkled like the Medusa, her voice was a\ndagger of corroded brass. She was full of the joy of righteousness and\nbad temper. She was a crusader and, like every crusader, she exulted in\nthe opportunity to be vicious in the name of virtue. \xe2\x80\x9cLet it go? If\npeople knew how many things I\xe2\x80\x99ve let go--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, quit being such a bully.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, a fine figure you\xe2\x80\x99d cut if I didn\xe2\x80\x99t bully you! You\xe2\x80\x99d lie abed till\nnoon and play your idiotic fiddle till midnight! You\xe2\x80\x99re born lazy, and\nyou\xe2\x80\x99re born shiftless, and you\xe2\x80\x99re born cowardly, Paul Riesling--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, now, don\xe2\x80\x99t say that, Zilla; you don\xe2\x80\x99t mean a word of it!\xe2\x80\x9d protested\nMrs. Babbitt.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will say that, and I mean every single last word of it!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, now, Zilla, the idea!\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Babbitt was maternal and fussy. She was\nno older than Zilla, but she seemed so--at first. She was placid and\npuffy and mature, where Zilla, at forty-five, was so bleached and\ntight-corseted that you knew only that she was older than she looked.\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe idea of talking to poor Paul like that!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor Paul is right! We\xe2\x80\x99d both be poor, we\xe2\x80\x99d be in the poorhouse, if I\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t jazz him up!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, now, Zilla, Georgie and I were just saying how hard Paul\xe2\x80\x99s been\nworking all year, and we were thinking it would be lovely if the Boys\ncould run off by themselves. I\xe2\x80\x99ve been coaxing George to go up to Maine\nahead of the rest of us, and get the tired out of his system before we\ncome, and I think it would be lovely if Paul could manage to get away\nand join him.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAt this exposure of his plot to escape, Paul was startled out of\nimpassivity. He rubbed his fingers. His hands twitched.\n\nZilla bayed, \xe2\x80\x9cYes! You\xe2\x80\x99re lucky! You can let George go, and not have to\nwatch him. Fat old Georgie! Never peeps at another woman! Hasn\xe2\x80\x99t got the\nspunk!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe hell I haven\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d Babbitt was fervently defending his priceless\nimmorality when Paul interrupted him--and Paul looked dangerous. He rose\nquickly; he said gently to Zilla:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you imply I have a lot of sweethearts.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I do!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, my dear, since you ask for it-- There hasn\xe2\x80\x99t been a time in\nthe last ten years when I haven\xe2\x80\x99t found some nice little girl to comfort\nme, and as long as you continue your amiability I shall probably\ncontinue to deceive you. It isn\xe2\x80\x99t hard. You\xe2\x80\x99re so stupid.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nZilla gibbered; she howled; words could not be distinguished in her\nslaver of abuse.\n\nThen the bland George F. Babbitt was transformed. If Paul was dangerous,\nif Zilla was a snake-locked fury, if the neat emotions suitable to the\nRevelstoke Arms had been slashed into raw hatreds, it was Babbitt who\nwas the most formidable. He leaped up. He seemed very large. He seized\nZilla\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder. The cautions of the broker were wiped from his face,\nand his voice was cruel:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve had enough of all this damn nonsense! I\xe2\x80\x99ve known you for\ntwenty-five years, Zil, and I never knew you to miss a chance to take\nyour disappointments out on Paul. You\xe2\x80\x99re not wicked. You\xe2\x80\x99re worse.\nYou\xe2\x80\x99re a fool. And let me tell you that Paul is the finest boy God ever\nmade. Every decent person is sick and tired of your taking advantage of\nbeing a woman and springing every mean innuendo you can think of. Who\nthe hell are you that a person like Paul should have to ask your\n_permission_ to go with me? You act like you were a combination of Queen\nVictoria and Cleopatra. You fool, can\xe2\x80\x99t you see how people snicker at\nyou, and sneer at you?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nZilla was sobbing, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve never-- I\xe2\x80\x99ve never--nobody ever talked to me\nlike this in all my life!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, but that\xe2\x80\x99s the way they talk behind your back! Always! They say\nyou\xe2\x80\x99re a scolding old woman. Old, by God!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThat cowardly attack broke her. Her eyes were blank. She wept. But\nBabbitt glared stolidly. He felt that he was the all-powerful official\nin charge; that Paul and Mrs. Babbitt looked on him with awe; that he\nalone could handle this case.\n\nZilla writhed. She begged, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, they don\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey certainly do!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve been a bad woman! I\xe2\x80\x99m terribly sorry! I\xe2\x80\x99ll kill myself! I\xe2\x80\x99ll do\nanything. Oh, I\xe2\x80\x99ll-- What do you want?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nShe abased herself completely. Also, she enjoyed it. To the connoisseur\nof scenes, nothing is more enjoyable than a thorough, melodramatic,\negoistic humility.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want you to let Paul beat it off to Maine with me,\xe2\x80\x9d Babbitt demanded.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can I help his going? You\xe2\x80\x99ve just said I was an idiot and nobody\npaid any attention to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you can help it, all right, all right! What you got to do is to cut\nout hinting that the minute he gets out of your sight, he\xe2\x80\x99ll go chasing\nafter some petticoat. Matter fact, that\xe2\x80\x99s the way you start the boy off\nwrong. You ought to have more sense--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I will, honestly, I will, George. I know I was bad. Oh, forgive me,\nall of you, forgive me--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nShe enjoyed it.\n\nSo did Babbitt. He condemned magnificently and forgave piously, and as\nhe went parading out with his wife he was grandly explanatory to her:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cKind of a shame to bully Zilla, but course it was the only way to\nhandle her. Gosh, I certainly did have her crawling!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nShe said calmly, \xe2\x80\x9cYes. You were horrid. You were showing off. You were\nhaving a lovely time thinking what a great fine person you were!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, by golly! Can you beat it! Of course I might of expected you to\nnot stand by me! I might of expected you\xe2\x80\x99d stick up for your own sex!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Poor Zilla, she\xe2\x80\x99s so unhappy. She takes it out on Paul. She hasn\xe2\x80\x99t\na single thing to do, in that little flat. And she broods too much. And\nshe used to be so pretty and gay, and she resents losing it. And you\nwere just as nasty and mean as you could be. I\xe2\x80\x99m not a bit proud of\nyou--or of Paul, boasting about his horrid love-affairs!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe was sulkily silent; he maintained his bad temper at a high level of\noutraged nobility all the four blocks home. At the door he left her, in\nself-approving haughtiness, and tramped the lawn.\n\nWith a shock it was revealed to him: \xe2\x80\x9cGosh, I wonder if she was\nright--if she was partly right?\xe2\x80\x9d Overwork must have flayed him to\nabnormal sensitiveness; it was one of the few times in his life when he\nhad queried his eternal excellence; and he perceived the summer night,\nsmelled the wet grass. Then: \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care! I\xe2\x80\x99ve pulled it off. We\xe2\x80\x99re\ngoing to have our spree. And for Paul, I\xe2\x80\x99d do anything.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\nII\n\nThey were buying their Maine tackle at Ijams Brothers\xe2\x80\x99, the Sporting\nGoods Mart, with the help of Willis Ijams, fellow member of the\nBoosters\xe2\x80\x99 Club. Babbitt was completely mad. He trumpeted and danced. He\nmuttered to Paul, \xe2\x80\x9cSay, this is pretty good, eh? To be buying the stuff,\neh? And good old Willis Ijams himself coming down on the floor to wait\non us! Say, if those fellows that are getting their kit for the North\nLakes knew we were going clear up to Maine, they\xe2\x80\x99d have a fit, eh?...\nWell, come on, Brother Ijams--Willis, I mean. Here\xe2\x80\x99s your chance! We\xe2\x80\x99re\na couple of easy marks! Whee! Let me at it! I\xe2\x80\x99m going to buy out the\nstore!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe gloated on fly-rods and gorgeous rubber hip-boots, on tents with\ncelluloid windows and folding chairs and ice-boxes. He simple-heartedly\nwanted to buy all of them. It was the Paul whom he was always vaguely\nprotecting who kept him from his drunken desires.\n\nBut even Paul lightened when Willis Ijams, a salesman with poetry and\ndiplomacy, discussed flies. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, of course, you boys know,\xe2\x80\x9d he said,\n\xe2\x80\x9cthe great scrap is between dry flies and wet flies. Personally, I\xe2\x80\x99m for\ndry flies. More sporting.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s so. Lots more sporting,\xe2\x80\x9d fulminated Babbitt, who knew very\nlittle about flies either wet or dry.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow if you\xe2\x80\x99ll take my advice, Georgie, you\xe2\x80\x99ll stock up well on these\npale evening dims, and silver sedges, and red ants. Oh, boy, there\xe2\x80\x99s a\nfly, that red ant!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou bet! That\xe2\x80\x99s what it is--a fly!\xe2\x80\x9d rejoiced Babbitt.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir, that red ant,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ijams, \xe2\x80\x9cis a real honest-to-God _fly_!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I guess ole Mr. Trout won\xe2\x80\x99t come a-hustling when I drop one of\nthose red ants on the water!\xe2\x80\x9d asserted Babbitt, and his thick wrists\nmade a rapturous motion of casting.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and the landlocked salmon will take it, too,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ijams, who had\nnever seen a landlocked salmon.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSalmon! Trout! Say, Paul, can you see Uncle George with his khaki pants\non haulin\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99em in, some morning \xe2\x80\x99bout seven? Whee!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\nIII\n\nThey were on the New York express, incredibly bound for Maine,\nincredibly without their families. They were free, in a man\xe2\x80\x99s world, in\nthe smoking-compartment of the Pullman.\n\nOutside the car window was a glaze of darkness stippled with the gold of\ninfrequent mysterious lights. Babbitt was immensely conscious, in the\nsway and authoritative clatter of the train, of going, of going on.\nLeaning toward Paul he grunted, \xe2\x80\x9cGosh, pretty nice to be hiking, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThe small room, with its walls of ocher-colored steel, was filled mostly\nwith the sort of men he classified as the Best Fellows You\xe2\x80\x99ll Ever\nMeet--Real Good Mixers. There were four of them on the long seat; a fat\nman with a shrewd fat face, a knife-edged man in a green velour hat, a\nvery young young man with an imitation amber cigarette-holder, and\nBabbitt. Facing them, on two movable leather chairs, were Paul and a\nlanky, old-fashioned man, very cunning, with wrinkles bracketing his\nmouth. They all read newspapers or trade journals, boot-and-shoe\njournals, crockery journals, and waited for the joys of conversation. It\nwas the very young man, now making his first journey by Pullman, who\nbegan it.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay, gee, I had a wild old time in Zenith!\xe2\x80\x9d he gloried. \xe2\x80\x9cSay, if a\nfellow knows the ropes there he can have as wild a time as he can in New\nYork!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYuh, I bet you simply raised the old Ned. I figured you were a bad man\nwhen I saw you get on the train!\xe2\x80\x9d chuckled the fat one.\n\nThe others delightedly laid down their papers.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, that\xe2\x80\x99s all right now! I guess I seen some things in the Arbor you\nnever seen!\xe2\x80\x9d complained the boy.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99ll bet you did! I bet you lapped up the malted milk like a\nreg\xe2\x80\x99lar little devil!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThen, the boy having served as introduction, they ignored him and\ncharged into real talk. Only Paul, sitting by himself, reading at a\nserial story in a newspaper, failed to join them, and all but Babbitt\nregarded him as a snob, an eccentric, a person of no spirit.\n\nWhich of them said which has never been determined, and does not matter,\nsince they all had the same ideas and expressed them always with the\nsame ponderous and brassy assurance. If it was not Babbitt who was\ndelivering any given verdict, at least he was beaming on the chancellor\nwho did deliver it.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt that, though,\xe2\x80\x9d announced the first, \xe2\x80\x9cthey\xe2\x80\x99re selling quite some booze\nin Zenith. Guess they are everywhere. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know how you fellows feel\nabout prohibition, but the way it strikes me is that it\xe2\x80\x99s a mighty\nbeneficial thing for the poor zob that hasn\xe2\x80\x99t got any will-power but for\nfellows like us, it\xe2\x80\x99s an infringement of personal liberty.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a fact. Congress has got no right to interfere with a fellow\xe2\x80\x99s\npersonal liberty,\xe2\x80\x9d contended the second.\n\nA man came in from the car, but as all the seats were full he stood up\nwhile he smoked his cigarette. He was an Outsider; he was not one of the\nOld Families of the smoking-compartment. They looked upon him bleakly\nand, after trying to appear at ease by examining his chin in the mirror,\nhe gave it up and went out in silence.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust been making a trip through the South. Business conditions not very\ngood down there,\xe2\x80\x9d said one of the council.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that a fact! Not very good, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, didn\xe2\x80\x99t strike me they were up to normal.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot up to normal, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t hardly say they were.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThe whole council nodded sagely and decided, \xe2\x80\x9cYump, not hardly up to\nsnuff.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, business conditions ain\xe2\x80\x99t what they ought to be out West,\nneither, not by a long shot.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a fact. And I guess the hotel business feels it. That\xe2\x80\x99s one good\nthing, though: these hotels that\xe2\x80\x99ve been charging five bucks a day--yes,\nand maybe six-seven!--for a rotten room are going to be darn glad to get\nfour, and maybe give you a little service.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a fact. Say, uh, speaknubout hotels, I hit the St. Francis at\nSan Francisco for the first time, the other day, and, say, it certainly\nis a first-class place.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re right, brother! The St. Francis is a swell place--absolutely\nA1.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a fact. I\xe2\x80\x99m right with you. It\xe2\x80\x99s a first-class place.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYuh, but say, any of you fellows ever stay at the Rippleton, in\nChicago? I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to knock-- I believe in boosting wherever you\ncan--but say, of all the rotten dumps that pass \xe2\x80\x99emselves off as\nfirst-class hotels, that\xe2\x80\x99s the worst. I\xe2\x80\x99m going to _get_ those guys, one\nof these days, and I told \xe2\x80\x99em so. You know how I am--well, maybe you\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know, but I\xe2\x80\x99m accustomed to first-class accommodations, and I\xe2\x80\x99m\nperfectly willing to pay a reasonable price. I got into Chicago late the\nother night, and the Rippleton\xe2\x80\x99s near the station-- I\xe2\x80\x99d never been there\nbefore, but I says to the taxi-driver-- I always believe in taking a taxi\nwhen you get in late; may cost a little more money, but, gosh, it\xe2\x80\x99s\nworth it when you got to be up early next morning and out selling a lot\nof crabs--and I said to him, \xe2\x80\x98Oh, just drive me over to the Rippleton.\xe2\x80\x99\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, we got there, and I breezed up to the desk and said to the clerk,\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, brother, got a nice room with bath for Cousin Bill?\xe2\x80\x99 Saaaay!\nYou\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 thought I\xe2\x80\x99d sold him a second, or asked him to work on Yom\nKippur! He hands me the cold-boiled stare and yaps, \xe2\x80\x98I dunno, friend,\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll see,\xe2\x80\x99 and he ducks behind the rigamajig they keep track of the\nrooms on. Well, I guess he called up the Credit Association and the\nAmerican Security League to see if I was all right--he certainly took\nlong enough--or maybe he just went to sleep; but finally he comes out\nand looks at me like it hurts him, and croaks, \xe2\x80\x98I think I can let you\nhave a room with bath.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98Well, that\xe2\x80\x99s awful nice of you--sorry to\ntrouble you--how much\xe2\x80\x99ll it set me back?\xe2\x80\x99 I says, real sweet. \xe2\x80\x98It\xe2\x80\x99ll\ncost you seven bucks a day, friend,\xe2\x80\x99 he says.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it was late, and anyway, it went down on my\nexpense-account--gosh, if I\xe2\x80\x99d been paying it instead of the firm, I\xe2\x80\x99d\n\xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 tramped the streets all night before I\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 let any hick tavern\nstick me seven great big round dollars, believe me! So I lets it go at\nthat. Well, the clerk wakes a nice young bellhop--fine lad--not a day\nover seventy-nine years old--fought at the Battle of Gettysburg and\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t know it\xe2\x80\x99s over yet--thought I was one of the Confederates, I\nguess, from the way he looked at me--and Rip van Winkle took me up to\nsomething-- I found out afterwards they called it a room, but first I\nthought there\xe2\x80\x99d been some mistake-- I thought they were putting me in the\nSalvation Army collection-box! At seven _per_ each and every _diem_!\nGosh!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYuh, I\xe2\x80\x99ve heard the Rippleton was pretty cheesy. Now, when I go to\nChicago I always stay at the Blackstone or the La Salle--first-class\nplaces.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay, any of you fellows ever stay at the Birchdale at Terre Haute? How\nis it?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, the Birchdale is a first-class hotel.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n(Twelve minutes of conference on the state of hotels in South Bend,\nFlint, Dayton, Tulsa, Wichita, Fort Worth, Winona, Erie, Fargo, and\nMoose Jaw.)\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpeaknubout prices,\xe2\x80\x9d the man in the velour hat observed, fingering the\nelk-tooth on his heavy watch-chain, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d like to know where they get\nthis stuff about clothes coming down. Now, you take this suit I got on.\xe2\x80\x9d\nHe pinched his trousers-leg. \xe2\x80\x9cFour years ago I paid forty-two fifty for\nit, and it was real sure-\xe2\x80\x99nough value. Well, here the other day I went\ninto a store back home and asked to see a suit, and the fellow yanks out\nsome hand-me-downs that, honest, I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t put on a hired man. Just out\nof curiosity I asks him, \xe2\x80\x98What you charging for that junk?\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98Junk,\xe2\x80\x99 he\nsays, \xe2\x80\x98what d\xe2\x80\x99 you mean junk? That\xe2\x80\x99s a swell piece of goods, all wool--\xe2\x80\x99\nLike hell! It was nice vegetable wool, right off the Ole Plantation!\n\xe2\x80\x98It\xe2\x80\x99s all wool,\xe2\x80\x99 he says, \xe2\x80\x98and we get sixty-seven ninety for it.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98Oh,\nyou do, do you!\xe2\x80\x99 I says. \xe2\x80\x98Not from me you don\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x99 I says, and I walks\nright out on him. You bet! I says to the wife, \xe2\x80\x98Well,\xe2\x80\x99 I said, \xe2\x80\x98as long\nas your strength holds out and you can go on putting a few more patches\non papa\xe2\x80\x99s pants, we\xe2\x80\x99ll just pass up buying clothes.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s right, brother. And just look at collars, frinstance--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHey! Wait!\xe2\x80\x9d the fat man protested. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter with collars? I\xe2\x80\x99m\nselling collars! D\xe2\x80\x99you realize the cost of labor on collars is still two\nhundred and seven per cent. above--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThey voted that if their old friend the fat man sold collars, then the\nprice of collars was exactly what it should be; but all other clothing\nwas tragically too expensive. They admired and loved one another now.\nThey went profoundly into the science of business, and indicated that\nthe purpose of manufacturing a plow or a brick was so that it might be\nsold. To them, the Romantic Hero was no longer the knight, the wandering\npoet, the cowpuncher, the aviator, nor the brave young district\nattorney, but the great sales-manager, who had an Analysis of\nMerchandizing Problems on his glass-topped desk, whose title of nobility\nwas \xe2\x80\x9cGo-getter,\xe2\x80\x9d and who devoted himself and all his young samurai to\nthe cosmic purpose of Selling--not of selling anything in particular,\nfor or to anybody in particular, but pure Selling.\n\nThe shop-talk roused Paul Riesling. Though he was a player of violins\nand an interestingly unhappy husband, he was also a very able salesman\nof tar-roofing. He listened to the fat man\xe2\x80\x99s remarks on \xe2\x80\x9cthe value of\nhouse-organs and bulletins as a method of jazzing-up the Boys out on the\nroad;\xe2\x80\x9d and he himself offered one or two excellent thoughts on the use\nof two-cent stamps on circulars. Then he committed an offense against\nthe holy law of the Clan of Good Fellows. He became highbrow.\n\nThey were entering a city. On the outskirts they passed a steel-mill\nwhich flared in scarlet and orange flame that licked at the cadaverous\nstacks, at the iron-sheathed walls and sullen converters.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy Lord, look at that--beautiful!\xe2\x80\x9d said Paul.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou bet it\xe2\x80\x99s beautiful, friend. That\xe2\x80\x99s the Shelling-Horton Steel Plant,\nand they tell me old John Shelling made a good three million bones out\nof munitions during the war!\xe2\x80\x9d the man with the velour hat said\nreverently.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean-- I mean it\xe2\x80\x99s lovely the way the light pulls that\npicturesque yard, all littered with junk, right out of the darkness,\xe2\x80\x9d\nsaid Paul.\n\nThey stared at him, while Babbitt crowed, \xe2\x80\x9cPaul there has certainly got\none great little eye for picturesque places and quaint sights and all\nthat stuff. \xe2\x80\x99D of been an author or something if he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t gone into the\nroofing line.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nPaul looked annoyed. (Babbitt sometimes wondered if Paul appreciated his\nloyal boosting.) The man in the velour hat grunted, \xe2\x80\x9cWell, personally, I\nthink Shelling-Horton keep their works awful dirty. Bum routing. But I\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t suppose there\xe2\x80\x99s any law against calling \xe2\x80\x99em \xe2\x80\x98picturesque\xe2\x80\x99 if it\ngets you that way!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nPaul sulkily returned to his newspaper and the conversation logically\nmoved on to trains.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat time do we get into Pittsburg?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Babbitt.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cPittsburg? I think we get in at--no, that was last year\xe2\x80\x99s\nschedule--wait a minute--let\xe2\x80\x99s see--got a time-table right here.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder if we\xe2\x80\x99re on time?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYuh, sure, we must be just about on time.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, we aren\xe2\x80\x99t--we were seven minutes late, last station.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWere we? Straight? Why, gosh, I thought we were right on time.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, we\xe2\x80\x99re about seven minutes late.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYuh, that\xe2\x80\x99s right; seven minutes late.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThe porter entered--a negro in white jacket with brass buttons.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow late are we, George?\xe2\x80\x9d growled the fat man.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Deed, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know, sir. I think we\xe2\x80\x99re about on time,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\nporter, folding towels and deftly tossing them up on the rack above the\nwashbowls. The council stared at him gloomily and when he was gone they\nwailed:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what\xe2\x80\x99s come over these niggers, nowadays. They never give\nyou a civil answer.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a fact. They\xe2\x80\x99re getting so they don\xe2\x80\x99t have a single bit of\nrespect for you. The old-fashioned coon was a fine old cuss--he knew his\nplace--but these young dinges don\xe2\x80\x99t want to be porters or\ncotton-pickers. Oh, no! They got to be lawyers and professors and Lord\nknows what all! I tell you, it\xe2\x80\x99s becoming a pretty serious problem. We\nought to get together and show the black man, yes, and the yellow man,\nhis place. Now, I haven\xe2\x80\x99t got one particle of race-prejudice. I\xe2\x80\x99m the\nfirst to be glad when a nigger succeeds--so long as he stays where he\nbelongs and doesn\xe2\x80\x99t try to usurp the rightful authority and business\nability of the white man.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the i.! And another thing we got to do,\xe2\x80\x9d said the man with the\nvelour hat (whose name was Koplinsky), \xe2\x80\x9cis to keep these damn foreigners\nout of the country. Thank the Lord, we\xe2\x80\x99re putting a limit on\nimmigration. These Dagoes and Hunkies have got to learn that this is a\nwhite man\xe2\x80\x99s country, and they ain\xe2\x80\x99t wanted here. When we\xe2\x80\x99ve assimilated\nthe foreigners we got here now and learned \xe2\x80\x99em the principles of\nAmericanism and turned \xe2\x80\x99em into regular folks, why then maybe we\xe2\x80\x99ll let\nin a few more.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou bet. That\xe2\x80\x99s a fact,\xe2\x80\x9d they observed, and passed on to lighter\ntopics. They rapidly reviewed motor-car prices, tire-mileage,\noil-stocks, fishing, and the prospects for the wheat-crop in Dakota.\n\nBut the fat man was impatient at this waste of time. He was a veteran\ntraveler and free of illusions. Already he had asserted that he was \xe2\x80\x9can\nold he-one.\xe2\x80\x9d He leaned forward, gathered in their attention by his\nexpression of sly humor, and grumbled, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, hell, boys, let\xe2\x80\x99s cut out\nthe formality and get down to the stories!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThey became very lively and intimate.\n\nPaul and the boy vanished. The others slid forward on the long seat,\nunbuttoned their vests, thrust their feet up on the chairs, pulled the\nstately brass cuspidors nearer, and ran the green window-shade down on\nits little trolley, to shut them in from the uncomfortable strangeness\nof night. After each bark of laughter they cried, \xe2\x80\x9cSay, jever hear the\none about--\xe2\x80\x9d Babbitt was expansive and virile. When the train stopped at\nan important station, the four men walked up and down the cement\nplatform, under the vast smoky train-shed roof, like a stormy sky, under\nthe elevated footways, beside crates of ducks and sides of beef, in the\nmystery of an unknown city. They strolled abreast, old friends and well\ncontent. At the long-drawn \xe2\x80\x9cAlllll aboarrrrrd\xe2\x80\x9d--like a mountain call at\ndusk--they hastened back into the smoking-compartment, and till two of\nthe morning continued the droll tales, their eyes damp with cigar-smoke\nand laughter. When they parted they shook hands, and chuckled, \xe2\x80\x9cWell,\nsir, it\xe2\x80\x99s been a great session. Sorry to bust it up. Mighty glad to met\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBabbitt lay awake in the close hot tomb of his Pullman berth, shaking\nwith remembrance of the fat man\xe2\x80\x99s limerick about the lady who wished to\nbe wild. He raised the shade; he lay with a puffy arm tucked between his\nhead and the skimpy pillow, looking out on the sliding silhouettes of\ntrees, and village lamps like exclamation-points. He was very happy.\n\n\n\n\nCHAPTER XI\n\n\nI\n\nThey had four hours in New York between trains. The one thing Babbitt\nwished to see was the Pennsylvania Hotel, which had been built since his\nlast visit. He stared up at it, muttering, \xe2\x80\x9cTwenty-two hundred rooms and\ntwenty-two hundred baths! That\xe2\x80\x99s got everything in the world beat. Lord,\ntheir turnover must be--well, suppose price of rooms is four to eight\ndollars a day, and I suppose maybe some ten and--four times twenty-two\nhundred--say six times twenty-two hundred--well, anyway, with\nrestaurants and everything, say summers between eight and fifteen\nthousand a day. Every day! I never thought I\xe2\x80\x99d see a thing like that!\nSome town! Of course the average fellow in Zenith has got more\nIndividual Initiative than the fourflushers here, but I got to hand it\nto New York. Yes, sir, town, you\xe2\x80\x99re all right--some ways. Well, old\nPaulski, I guess we\xe2\x80\x99ve seen everything that\xe2\x80\x99s worth while. How\xe2\x80\x99ll we\nkill the rest of the time? Movie?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBut Paul desired to see a liner. \xe2\x80\x9cAlways wanted to go to Europe--and, by\nthunder, I will, too, some day before I pass out,\xe2\x80\x9d he sighed.\n\nFrom a rough wharf on the North River they stared at the stern of the\n_Aquitania_ and her stacks and wireless antenn\xc3\xa6 lifted above the\ndock-house which shut her in.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy golly,\xe2\x80\x9d Babbitt droned, \xe2\x80\x9cwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be so bad to go over to the Old\nCountry and take a squint at all these ruins, and the place where\nShakespeare was born. And think of being able to order a drink whenever\nyou wanted one! Just range up to a bar and holler out loud, \xe2\x80\x98Gimme a\ncocktail, and darn the police!\xe2\x80\x99 Not bad at all. What juh like to see,\nover there, Paulibus?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nPaul did not answer. Babbitt turned. Paul was standing with clenched\nfists, head drooping, staring at the liner as in terror. His thin body,\nseen against the summer-glaring planks of the wharf, was childishly\nmeager.\n\nAgain, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat would you hit for on the other side, Paul?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nScowling at the steamer, his breast heaving, Paul whispered, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, my\nGod!\xe2\x80\x9d While Babbitt watched him anxiously he snapped, \xe2\x80\x9cCome on, let\xe2\x80\x99s\nget out of this,\xe2\x80\x9d and hastened down the wharf, not looking back.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s funny,\xe2\x80\x9d considered Babbitt. \xe2\x80\x9cThe boy didn\xe2\x80\x99t care for seeing the\nocean boats after all. I thought he\xe2\x80\x99d be interested in \xe2\x80\x99em.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\nII\n\nThough he exulted, and made sage speculations about locomotive\nhorse-power, as their train climbed the Maine mountain-ridge and from\nthe summit he looked down the shining way among the pines; though he\nremarked, \xe2\x80\x9cWell, by golly!\xe2\x80\x9d when he discovered that the station at\nKatadumcook, the end of the line, was an aged freight-car; Babbitt\xe2\x80\x99s\nmoment of impassioned release came when they sat on a tiny wharf on Lake\nSunasquam, awaiting the launch from the hotel. A raft had floated down\nthe lake; between the logs and the shore, the water was transparent,\nthin-looking, flashing with minnows. A guide in black felt hat with\ntrout-flies in the band, and flannel shirt of a peculiarly daring blue,\nsat on a log and whittled and was silent. A dog, a good country dog,\nblack and woolly gray, a dog rich in leisure and in meditation,\nscratched and grunted and slept. The thick sunlight was lavish on the\nbright water, on the rim of gold-green balsam boughs, the silver birches\nand tropic ferns, and across the lake it burned on the sturdy shoulders\nof the mountains. Over everything was a holy peace.\n\nSilent, they loafed on the edge of the wharf, swinging their legs above\nthe water. The immense tenderness of the place sank into Babbitt, and\nhe murmured, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d just like to sit here--the rest of my life--and\nwhittle--and sit. And never hear a typewriter. Or Stan Graff fussing in\nthe \xe2\x80\x99phone. Or Rone and Ted scrapping. Just sit. Gosh!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe patted Paul\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder. \xe2\x80\x9cHow does it strike you, old snoozer?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it\xe2\x80\x99s darn good, Georgie. There\xe2\x80\x99s something sort of eternal about\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nFor once, Babbitt understood him.\n\n\nIII\n\nTheir launch rounded the bend; at the head of the lake, under a mountain\nslope, they saw the little central dining-shack of their hotel and the\ncrescent of squat log cottages which served as bedrooms. They landed,\nand endured the critical examination of the habitu\xc3\xa9s who had been at the\nhotel for a whole week. In their cottage, with its high stone fireplace,\nthey hastened, as Babbitt expressed it, to \xe2\x80\x9cget into some regular\nhe-togs.\xe2\x80\x9d They came out; Paul in an old gray suit and soft white shirt;\nBabbitt in khaki shirt and vast and flapping khaki trousers. It was\nexcessively new khaki; his rimless spectacles belonged to a city office;\nand his face was not tanned but a city pink. He made a discordant noise\nin the place. But with infinite satisfaction he slapped his legs and\ncrowed, \xe2\x80\x9cSay, this is getting back home, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThey stood on the wharf before the hotel. He winked at Paul and drew\nfrom his back pocket a plug of chewing-tobacco, a vulgarism forbidden in\nthe Babbitt home. He took a chew, beaming and wagging his head as he\ntugged at it. \xe2\x80\x9cUm! Um! Maybe I haven\xe2\x80\x99t been hungry for a wad of\neating-tobacco! Have some?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThey looked at each other in a grin of understanding. Paul took the\nplug, gnawed at it. They stood quiet, their jaws working. They solemnly\nspat, one after the other, into the placid water. They stretched\nvoluptuously, with lifted arms and arched backs. From beyond the\nmountains came the shuffling sound of a far-off train. A trout leaped,\nand fell back in a silver circle. They sighed together.\n\n\nIV\n\nThey had a week before their families came. Each evening they planned to\nget up early and fish before breakfast. Each morning they lay abed till\nthe breakfast-bell, pleasantly conscious that there were no efficient\nwives to rouse them. The mornings were cold; the fire was kindly as they\ndressed.\n\nPaul was distressingly clean, but Babbitt reveled in a good sound\ndirtiness, in not having to shave till his spirit was moved to it. He\ntreasured every grease spot and fish-scale on his new khaki trousers.\n\nAll morning they fished unenergetically, or tramped the dim and\naqueous-lighted trails among rank ferns and moss sprinkled with crimson\nbells. They slept all afternoon, and till midnight played stud-poker\nwith the guides. Poker was a serious business to the guides. They did\nnot gossip; they shuffled the thick greasy cards with a deft ferocity\nmenacing to the \xe2\x80\x9csports;\xe2\x80\x9d and Joe Paradise, king of guides, was\nsarcastic to loiterers who halted the game even to scratch.\n\nAt midnight, as Paul and he blundered to their cottage over the pungent\nwet grass, and pine-roots confusing in the darkness, Babbitt rejoiced\nthat he did not have to explain to his wife where he had been all\nevening.\n\nThey did not talk much. The nervous loquacity and opinionation of the\nZenith Athletic Club dropped from them. But when they did talk they\nslipped into the na\xc3\xafve intimacy of college days. Once they drew their\ncanoe up to the bank of Sunasquam Water, a stream walled in by the dense\ngreen of the hardhack. The sun roared on the green jungle but in the\nshade was sleepy peace, and the water was golden and rippling. Babbitt\ndrew his hand through the cool flood, and mused:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe never thought we\xe2\x80\x99d come to Maine together!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. We\xe2\x80\x99ve never done anything the way we thought we would. I expected\nto live in Germany with my granddad\xe2\x80\x99s people, and study the fiddle.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s so. And remember how I wanted to be a lawyer and go into\npolitics? I still think I might have made a go of it. I\xe2\x80\x99ve kind of got\nthe gift of the gab--anyway, I can think on my feet, and make some kind\nof a spiel on most anything, and of course that\xe2\x80\x99s the thing you need in\npolitics. By golly, Ted\xe2\x80\x99s going to law-school, even if I didn\xe2\x80\x99t! Well-- I\nguess it\xe2\x80\x99s worked out all right. Myra\xe2\x80\x99s been a fine wife. And Zilla\nmeans well, Paulibus.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Up here, I figure out all sorts of plans to keep her amused. I\nkind of feel life is going to be different, now that we\xe2\x80\x99re getting a\ngood rest and can go back and start over again.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope so, old boy.\xe2\x80\x9d Shyly: \xe2\x80\x9cSay, gosh, it\xe2\x80\x99s been awful nice to sit\naround and loaf and gamble and act regular, with you along, you old\nhorse-thief!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you know what it means to me, Georgie. Saved my life.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThe shame of emotion overpowered them; they cursed a little, to prove\nthey were good rough fellows; and in a mellow silence, Babbitt whistling\nwhile Paul hummed, they paddled back to the hotel.\n\n\nV\n\nThough it was Paul who had seemed overwrought, Babbitt who had been the\nprotecting big brother, Paul became clear-eyed and merry, while Babbitt\nsank into irritability. He uncovered layer on layer of hidden weariness.\nAt first he had played nimble jester to Paul and for him sought\namusements; by the end of the week Paul was nurse, and Babbitt accepted\nfavors with the condescension one always shows a patient nurse.\n\nThe day before their families arrived, the women guests at the hotel\nbubbled, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it nice! You must be so excited;\xe2\x80\x9d and the\nproprieties compelled Babbitt and Paul to look excited. But they went to\nbed early and grumpy.\n\nWhen Myra appeared she said at once, \xe2\x80\x9cNow, we want you boys to go on\nplaying around just as if we weren\xe2\x80\x99t here.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThe first evening, he stayed out for poker with the guides, and she said\nin placid merriment, \xe2\x80\x9cMy! You\xe2\x80\x99re a regular bad one!\xe2\x80\x9d The second evening,\nshe groaned sleepily, \xe2\x80\x9cGood heavens, are you going to be out every\nsingle night?\xe2\x80\x9d The third evening, he didn\xe2\x80\x99t play poker.\n\nHe was tired now in every cell. \xe2\x80\x9cFunny! Vacation doesn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to have\ndone me a bit of good,\xe2\x80\x9d he lamented. \xe2\x80\x9cPaul\xe2\x80\x99s frisky as a colt, but I\nswear, I\xe2\x80\x99m crankier and nervouser than when I came up here.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe had three weeks of Maine. At the end of the second week he began to\nfeel calm, and interested in life. He planned an expedition to climb\nSachem Mountain, and wanted to camp overnight at Box Car Pond. He was\ncuriously weak, yet cheerful, as though he had cleansed his veins of\npoisonous energy and was filling them with wholesome blood.\n\nHe ceased to be irritated by Ted\xe2\x80\x99s infatuation with a waitress (his\nseventh tragic affair this year); he played catch with Ted, and with\npride taught him to cast a fly in the pine-shadowed silence of Skowtuit\nPond.\n\nAt the end he sighed, \xe2\x80\x9cHang it, I\xe2\x80\x99m just beginning to enjoy my vacation.\nBut, well, I feel a lot better. And it\xe2\x80\x99s going to be one great year!\nMaybe the Real Estate Board will elect me president, instead of some\nfuzzy old-fashioned faker like Chan Mott.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nOn the way home, whenever he went into the smoking-compartment he felt\nguilty at deserting his wife and angry at being expected to feel guilty,\nbut each time he triumphed, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, this is going to be a great year, a\ngreat old year!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\n\nCHAPTER XII\n\n\nI\n\nAll the way home from Maine, Babbitt was certain that he was a changed\nman. He was converted to serenity. He was going to cease worrying about\nbusiness. He was going to have more \xe2\x80\x9cinterests\xe2\x80\x9d--theaters, public\naffairs, reading. And suddenly, as he finished an especially heavy\ncigar, he was going to stop smoking.\n\nHe invented a new and perfect method. He would buy no tobacco; he would\ndepend on borrowing it; and, of course, he would be ashamed to borrow\noften. In a spasm of righteousness he flung his cigar-case out of the\nsmoking-compartment window. He went back and was kind to his wife about\nnothing in particular; he admired his own purity, and decided,\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbsolutely simple. Just a matter of will-power.\xe2\x80\x9d He started a magazine\nserial about a scientific detective. Ten miles on, he was conscious that\nhe desired to smoke. He ducked his head, like a turtle going into its\nshell; he appeared uneasy; he skipped two pages in his story and didn\xe2\x80\x99t\nknow it. Five miles later, he leaped up and sought the porter. \xe2\x80\x9cSay, uh,\nGeorge, have you got a--\xe2\x80\x9d The porter looked patient. \xe2\x80\x9cHave you got a\ntime-table?\xe2\x80\x9d Babbitt finished. At the next stop he went out and bought a\ncigar. Since it was to be his last before he reached Zenith, he finished\nit down to an inch stub.\n\nFour days later he again remembered that he had stopped smoking, but he\nwas too busy catching up with his office-work to keep it remembered.\n\n\nII\n\nBaseball, he determined, would be an excellent hobby. \xe2\x80\x9cNo sense a man\xe2\x80\x99s\nworking his fool head off. I\xe2\x80\x99m going out to the Game three times a\nweek. Besides, fellow ought to support the home team.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe did go and support the team, and enhance the glory of Zenith, by\nyelling \xe2\x80\x9cAttaboy!\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cRotten!\xe2\x80\x9d He performed the rite scrupulously. He\nwore a cotton handkerchief about his collar; he became sweaty; he opened\nhis mouth in a wide loose grin; and drank lemon soda out of a bottle. He\nwent to the Game three times a week, for one week. Then he compromised\non watching the _Advocate-Times_ bulletin-board. He stood in the\nthickest and steamiest of the crowd, and as the boy up on the lofty\nplatform recorded the achievements of Big Bill Bostwick, the pitcher,\nBabbitt remarked to complete strangers, \xe2\x80\x9cPretty nice! Good work!\xe2\x80\x9d and\nhastened back to the office.\n\nHe honestly believed that he loved baseball. It is true that he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t,\nin twenty-five years, himself played any baseball except back-lot catch\nwith Ted--very gentle, and strictly limited to ten minutes. But the game\nwas a custom of his clan, and it gave outlet for the homicidal and\nsides-taking instincts which Babbitt called \xe2\x80\x9cpatriotism\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9clove of\nsport.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAs he approached the office he walked faster and faster, muttering,\n\xe2\x80\x9cGuess better hustle.\xe2\x80\x9d All about him the city was hustling, for\nhustling\xe2\x80\x99s sake. Men in motors were hustling to pass one another in the\nhustling traffic. Men were hustling to catch trolleys, with another\ntrolley a minute behind, and to leap from the trolleys, to gallop across\nthe sidewalk, to hurl themselves into buildings, into hustling express\nelevators. Men in dairy lunches were hustling to gulp down the food\nwhich cooks had hustled to fry. Men in barber shops were snapping, \xe2\x80\x9cJus\xe2\x80\x99\nshave me once over. Gotta hustle.\xe2\x80\x9d Men were feverishly getting rid of\nvisitors in offices adorned with the signs, \xe2\x80\x9cThis Is My Busy Day\xe2\x80\x9d and\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Lord Created the World in Six Days--You Can Spiel All You Got to\nSay in Six Minutes.\xe2\x80\x9d Men who had made five thousand, year before last,\nand ten thousand last year, were urging on nerve-yelping bodies and\nparched brains so that they might make twenty thousand this year; and\nthe men who had broken down immediately after making their twenty\nthousand dollars were hustling to catch trains, to hustle through the\nvacations which the hustling doctors had ordered.\n\nAmong them Babbitt hustled back to his office, to sit down with nothing\nmuch to do except see that the staff looked as though they were\nhustling.\n\n\nIII\n\nEvery Saturday afternoon he hustled out to his country club and hustled\nthrough nine holes of golf as a rest after the week\xe2\x80\x99s hustle.\n\nIn Zenith it was as necessary for a Successful Man to belong to a\ncountry club as it was to wear a linen collar.
