Babbitt had not been able to prove\nthese suspicions, and though he had rather planned to discharge Graff he\nhad never quite found time for it.\n\nNow into Babbitt\xe2\x80\x99s private room charged a red-faced man, panting, \xe2\x80\x9cLook\nhere! I\xe2\x80\x99ve come to raise particular merry hell, and unless you have that\nfellow pinched, I will!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s-- Calm down, o\xe2\x80\x99 man. What\xe2\x80\x99s trouble?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrouble! Huh! Here\xe2\x80\x99s the trouble--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSit down and take it easy! They can hear you all over the building!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis fellow Graff you got working for you, he leases me a house. I was\nin yesterday and signs the lease, all O.K., and he was to get the\nowner\xe2\x80\x99s signature and mail me the lease last night. Well, and he did.\nThis morning I comes down to breakfast and the girl says a fellow had\ncome to the house right after the early delivery and told her he wanted\nan envelope that had been mailed by mistake, big long envelope with\n\xe2\x80\x98Babbitt-Thompson\xe2\x80\x99 in the corner of it. Sure enough, there it was, so\nshe lets him have it. And she describes the fellow to me, and it was\nthis Graff. So I \xe2\x80\x99phones to him and he, the poor fool, he admits it! He\nsays after my lease was all signed he got a better offer from another\nfellow and he wanted my lease back. Now what you going to do about it?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour name is--?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWilliam Varney--W. K. Varney.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes. That was the Garrison house.\xe2\x80\x9d Babbitt sounded the buzzer. When\nMiss McGoun came in, he demanded, \xe2\x80\x9cGraff gone out?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you look through his desk and see if there is a lease made out to\nMr. Varney on the Garrison house?\xe2\x80\x9d To Varney: \xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t tell you how sorry\nI am this happened. Needless to say, I\xe2\x80\x99ll fire Graff the minute he comes\nin. And of course your lease stands. But there\xe2\x80\x99s one other thing I\xe2\x80\x99d\nlike to do. I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell the owner not to pay us the commission but apply\nit to your rent. No! Straight! I want to. To be frank, this thing shakes\nme up bad. I suppose I\xe2\x80\x99ve always been a Practical Business Man. Probably\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve told one or two fairy stories in my time, when the occasion called\nfor it--you know: sometimes you have to lay things on thick, to impress\nboneheads. But this is the first time I\xe2\x80\x99ve ever had to accuse one of my\nown employees of anything more dishonest than pinching a few stamps.\nHonest, it would hurt me if we profited by it. So you\xe2\x80\x99ll let me hand you\nthe commission? Good!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\nII\n\nHe walked through the February city, where trucks flung up a spattering\nof slush and the sky was dark above dark brick cornices. He came back\nmiserable. He, who respected the law, had broken it by concealing the\nFederal crime of interception of the mails. But he could not see Graff\ngo to jail and his wife suffer. Worse, he had to discharge Graff, and\nthis was a part of office routine which he feared. He liked people so\nmuch, he so much wanted them to like him, that he could not bear\ninsulting them.\n\nMiss McGoun dashed in to whisper, with the excitement of an approaching\nscene, \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s here!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Graff? Ask him to come in.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe tried to make himself heavy and calm in his chair, and to keep his\neyes expressionless. Graff stalked in--a man of thirty-five, dapper,\neye-glassed, with a foppish mustache.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWant me?\xe2\x80\x9d said Graff.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Sit down.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nGraff continued to stand, grunting, \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose that old nut Varney has\nbeen in to see you. Let me explain about him. He\xe2\x80\x99s a regular tightwad,\nand he sticks out for every cent, and he practically lied to me about\nhis ability to pay the rent-- I found that out just after we signed up.\nAnd then another fellow comes along with a better offer for the house,\nand I felt it was my duty to the firm to get rid of Varney, and I was so\nworried about it I skun up there and got back the lease. Honest, Mr.\nBabbitt, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t intend to pull anything crooked. I just wanted the\nfirm to have all the commis--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait now, Stan. This may all be true, but I\xe2\x80\x99ve been having a lot of\ncomplaints about you. Now I don\xe2\x80\x99t s\xe2\x80\x99pose you ever mean to do wrong, and\nI think if you just get a good lesson that\xe2\x80\x99ll jog you up a little,\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ll turn out a first-class realtor yet. But I don\xe2\x80\x99t see how I can\nkeep you on.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nGraff leaned against the filing-cabinet, his hands in his pockets, and\nlaughed. \xe2\x80\x9cSo I\xe2\x80\x99m fired! Well, old Vision and Ethics, I\xe2\x80\x99m tickled to\ndeath! But I don\xe2\x80\x99t want you to think you can get away with any\nholier-than-thou stuff. Sure I\xe2\x80\x99ve pulled some raw stuff--a little of\nit--but how could I help it, in this office?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, by God, young man--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cTut, tut! Keep the naughty temper down, and don\xe2\x80\x99t holler, because\neverybody in the outside office will hear you. They\xe2\x80\x99re probably\nlistening right now. Babbitt, old dear, you\xe2\x80\x99re crooked in the first\nplace and a damn skinflint in the second. If you paid me a decent salary\nI wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have to steal pennies off a blind man to keep my wife from\nstarving. Us married just five months, and her the nicest girl living,\nand you keeping us flat broke all the time, you damned old thief, so you\ncan put money away for your saphead of a son and your wishywashy fool of\na daughter! Wait, now! You\xe2\x80\x99ll by God take it, or I\xe2\x80\x99ll bellow so the\nwhole office will hear it! And crooked-- Say, if I told the prosecuting\nattorney what I know about this last Street Traction option steal, both\nyou and me would go to jail, along with some nice, clean, pious, high-up\ntraction guns!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Stan, looks like we were coming down to cases. That deal-- There\nwas nothing crooked about it. The only way you can get progress is for\nthe broad-gauged men to get things done; and they got to be rewarded--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, for Pete\xe2\x80\x99s sake, don\xe2\x80\x99t get virtuous on me! As I gather it, I\xe2\x80\x99m\nfired. All right. It\xe2\x80\x99s a good thing for me. And if I catch you knocking\nme to any other firm, I\xe2\x80\x99ll squeal all I know about you and Henry T. and\nthe dirty little lickspittle deals that you corporals of industry pull\noff for the bigger and brainier crooks, and you\xe2\x80\x99ll get chased out of\ntown. And me--you\xe2\x80\x99re right, Babbitt, I\xe2\x80\x99ve been going crooked, but now\nI\xe2\x80\x99m going straight, and the first step will be to get a job in some\noffice where the boss doesn\xe2\x80\x99t talk about Ideals. Bad luck, old dear, and\nyou can stick your job up the sewer!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBabbitt sat for a long time, alternately raging, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll have him\narrested,\xe2\x80\x9d and yearning \xe2\x80\x9cI wonder-- No, I\xe2\x80\x99ve never done anything that\nwasn\xe2\x80\x99t necessary to keep the Wheels of Progress moving.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nNext day he hired in Graff\xe2\x80\x99s place Fritz Weilinger, the salesman of his\nmost injurious rival, the East Side Homes and Development Company, and\nthus at once annoyed his competitor and acquired an excellent man. Young\nFritz was a curly-headed, merry, tennis-playing youngster. He made\ncustomers welcome to the office. Babbitt thought of him as a son, and in\nhim had much comfort.\n\n\nIII\n\nAn abandoned race-track on the outskirts of Chicago, a plot excellent\nfor factory sites, was to be sold, and Jake Offut asked Babbitt to bid\non it for him. The strain of the Street Traction deal and his\ndisappointment in Stanley Graff had so shaken Babbitt that he found it\nhard to sit at his desk and concentrate. He proposed to his family,\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here, folks! Do you know who\xe2\x80\x99s going to trot up to Chicago for a\ncouple of days--just week-end; won\xe2\x80\x99t lose but one day of school--know\nwho\xe2\x80\x99s going with that celebrated business-ambassador, George F. Babbitt?\nWhy, Mr. Theodore Roosevelt Babbitt!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHurray!\xe2\x80\x9d Ted shouted, and \xe2\x80\x9cOh, maybe the Babbitt men won\xe2\x80\x99t paint that\nlil ole town red!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAnd, once away from the familiar implications of home, they were two men\ntogether. They\xe2\x80\x99re slow. They haven\xe2\x80\x99t either one of \xe2\x80\x99em\ngot our pep.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s right. They\xe2\x80\x99re slow. He\xe2\x80\x99s one of these highbrows. He had once called at Babbitt\xe2\x80\x99s office, to ask\nabout houses. \xe2\x80\x9cH\xe2\x80\x99 are you, Mr. Babbitt! We going to have you with us to\nChicago? Babbitt\nordered Blue Point oysters with cocktail sauce, a tremendous steak with\na tremendous platter of French fried potatoes, two pots of coffee, apple\npie with ice cream for both of them and, for Ted, an extra piece of\nmince pie.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHot stuff! Some feed, young fella!\xe2\x80\x9d Ted admired.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuh! You stick around with me, old man, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll show you a good time!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThey went to a musical comedy and nudged each other at the matrimonial\njokes and the prohibition jokes; they paraded the lobby, arm in arm,\nbetween acts, and in the glee of his first release from the shame which\ndissevers fathers and sons Ted chuckled, \xe2\x80\x9cDad, did you ever hear the one\nabout the three milliners and the judge?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nWhen Ted had returned to Zenith, Babbitt was lonely. As he was trying to\nmake alliance between Offutt and certain Milwaukee interests which\nwanted the race-track plot, most of his time was taken up in waiting for\ntelephone calls.... Sitting on the edge of his bed, holding the portable\ntelephone, asking wearily, \xe2\x80\x9cMr. Sagen not in yet? Didn\xe2\x80\x99 he leave any\nmessage for me? All right, I\xe2\x80\x99ll hold the wire.\xe2\x80\x9d Staring at a stain on\nthe wall, reflecting that it resembled a shoe, and being bored by this\ntwentieth discovery that it resembled a shoe. Lighting a cigarette;\nthen, bound to the telephone with no ash-tray in reach, wondering what\nto do with this burning menace and anxiously trying to toss it into the\ntiled bathroom. At last, on the telephone, \xe2\x80\x9cNo message, eh? All right,\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll call up again.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nOne afternoon he wandered through snow-rutted streets of which he had\nnever heard, streets of small tenements and two-family houses and\nmarooned cottages. It came to him that he had nothing to do, that there\nwas nothing he wanted to do. He was bleakly lonely in the evening, when\nhe dined by himself at the Regency Hotel. He sat in the lobby afterward,\nin a plush chair bedecked with the Saxe-Coburg arms, lighting a cigar\nand looking for some one who would come and play with him and save him\nfrom thinking. In the chair next to him (showing the arms of Lithuania)\nwas a half-familiar man, a large red-faced man with pop eyes and a\ndeficient yellow mustache. He seemed kind and insignificant, and as\nlonely as Babbitt himself. He wore a tweed suit and a reluctant orange\ntie.\n\nIt came to Babbitt with a pyrotechnic crash. The melancholy stranger was\nSir Gerald Doak.\n\nInstinctively Babbitt rose, bumbling, \xe2\x80\x9cHow\xe2\x80\x99re you, Sir Gerald? \xe2\x80\x99Member\nwe met in Zenith, at Charley McKelvey\xe2\x80\x99s? It\xe2\x80\x99s just as\nGerald Doak says to me in Chicago--oh, yes, Jerry\xe2\x80\x99s an old friend of\nmine--the wife and I are thinking of running over to England to stay\nwith Jerry in his castle, next year--and he said to me, \xe2\x80\x98Georgie, old\nbean, I like Lucile first-rate, but you and me, George, we got to make\nher get over this highty-tighty hooptediddle way she\xe2\x80\x99s got.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBut that evening a thing happened which wrecked his pride.\n\n\nV\n\nAt the Regency Hotel cigar-counter he fell to talking with a salesman of\npianos, and they dined together. Babbitt was filled with friendliness\nand well-being. He enjoyed the gorgeousness of the dining-room: the\nchandeliers, the looped brocade curtains, the portraits of French kings\nagainst panels of gilded oak. He enjoyed the crowd: pretty women, good\nsolid fellows who were \xe2\x80\x9cliberal spenders.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe gasped. He stared, and turned away, and stared again. Three tables\noff, with a doubtful sort of woman, a woman at once coy and withered,\nwas Paul Riesling, and Paul was supposed to be in Akron, selling\ntar-roofing. The woman was tapping his hand, mooning at him and\ngiggling. Babbitt felt that he had encountered something involved and\nharmful. Paul was talking with the rapt eagerness of a man who is\ntelling his troubles. He was concentrated on the woman\xe2\x80\x99s faded eyes.\nOnce he held her hand and once, blind to the other guests, he puckered\nhis lips as though he was pretending to kiss her. Babbitt had so strong\nan impulse to go to Paul that he could feel his body uncoiling, his\nshoulders moving, but he felt, desperately, that he must be diplomatic,\nand not till he saw Paul paying the check did he bluster to the\npiano-salesman, \xe2\x80\x9cBy golly--friend of mine over there--\xe2\x80\x99scuse me\nsecond--just say hello to him.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe touched Paul\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder, and cried, \xe2\x80\x9cWell, when did you hit town?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nPaul glared up at him, face hardening. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, hello, George. Thought you\xe2\x80\x99d\ngone back to Zenith.\xe2\x80\x9d He did not introduce his companion. Babbitt peeped\nat her. She was a flabbily pretty, weakly flirtatious woman of forty-two\nor three, in an atrocious flowery hat. Her rouging was thorough but\nunskilful.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere you staying, Paulibus?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThe woman turned, yawned, examined her nails. She seemed accustomed to\nnot being introduced.\n\nPaul grumbled, \xe2\x80\x9cCampbell Inn, on the South Side.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlone?\xe2\x80\x9d It sounded insinuating.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes! Unfortunately!\xe2\x80\x9d Furiously Paul turned toward the woman, smiling\nwith a fondness sickening to Babbitt. \xe2\x80\x9cMay! Want to introduce you. Mrs.\nArnold, this is my old--acquaintance, George Babbitt.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cPleasmeech,\xe2\x80\x9d growled Babbitt, while she gurgled, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99m very pleased\nto meet any friend of Mr. Riesling\xe2\x80\x99s, I\xe2\x80\x99m sure.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBabbitt demanded, \xe2\x80\x9cBe back there later this evening, Paul? I\xe2\x80\x99ll go down to your\nhotel, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll wait for you!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\n\nCHAPTER XX\n\n\nI\n\nHe sat smoking with the piano-salesman, clinging to the warm refuge of\ngossip, afraid to venture into thoughts of Paul. He was the more affable\non the surface as secretly he became more apprehensive, felt more\nhollow. He was certain that Paul was in Chicago without Zilla\xe2\x80\x99s\nknowledge, and that he was doing things not at all moral and secure.\nWhen the salesman yawned that he had to write up his orders, Babbitt\nleft him, left the hotel, in leisurely calm. But savagely he said\n\xe2\x80\x9cCampbell Inn!\xe2\x80\x9d to the taxi-driver. He sat agitated on the slippery\nleather seat, in that chill dimness which smelled of dust and perfume\nand Turkish cigarettes. He did not heed the snowy lake-front, the dark\nspaces and sudden bright corners in the unknown land south of the Loop.\n\nThe office of the Campbell Inn was hard, bright, new; the night clerk\nharder and brighter. \xe2\x80\x9cYep?\xe2\x80\x9d he said to Babbitt.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Paul Riesling registered here?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYep.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs he in now?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNope.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen if you\xe2\x80\x99ll give me his key, I\xe2\x80\x99ll wait for him.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t do that, brother. Wait down here if you wanna.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBabbitt had spoken with the deference which all the Clan of Good Fellows\ngive to hotel clerks. Now he said with snarling abruptness:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI may have to wait some time. I\xe2\x80\x99m Riesling\xe2\x80\x99s brother-in-law. I\xe2\x80\x99ll go up\nto his room. D\xe2\x80\x99 I look like a sneak-thief?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHis voice was low and not pleasant. With considerable haste the clerk\ntook down the key, protesting, \xe2\x80\x9cI never said you looked like a\nsneak-thief. Just rules of the hotel. But if you want to--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nOn his way up in the elevator Babbitt wondered why he was here. Why\nshouldn\xe2\x80\x99t Paul be dining with a respectable married woman? Why had he\nlied to the clerk about being Paul\xe2\x80\x99s brother-in-law? He had acted like a\nchild. He must be careful not to say foolish dramatic things to Paul. As\nhe settled down he tried to look pompous and placid. Then the\nthought-- Suicide. He\xe2\x80\x99d been dreading that, without knowing it. Paul\nwould be just the person to do something like that. He must be out of\nhis head or he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be confiding in that--that dried-up hag.\n\nZilla (oh, damn Zilla! how gladly he\xe2\x80\x99d throttle that nagging fiend of a\nwoman!)--she\xe2\x80\x99d probably succeeded at last, and driven Paul crazy.\n\nSuicide. Out there in the lake, way out, beyond the piled ice along the\nshore. It would be ghastly cold to drop into the water to-night.\n\nOr--throat cut--in the bathroom--\n\nBabbitt flung into Paul\xe2\x80\x99s bathroom. It was empty. He smiled, feebly.\n\nHe pulled at his choking collar, looked at his watch, opened the window\nto stare down at the street, looked at his watch, tried to read the\nevening paper lying on the glass-topped bureau, looked again at his\nwatch. Three minutes had gone by since he had first looked at it.\n\nAnd he waited for three hours.\n\nHe was sitting fixed, chilled, when the doorknob turned. Paul came in\nglowering.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHello,\xe2\x80\x9d Paul said. \xe2\x80\x9cBeen waiting?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYuh, little while.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell what? Just thought I\xe2\x80\x99d drop in to see how you made out in Akron.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did all right. What difference does it make?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, gosh, Paul, what are you sore about?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you butting into my affairs for?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Paul, that\xe2\x80\x99s no way to talk! I\xe2\x80\x99m not butting into nothing. I was\nso glad to see your ugly old phiz that I just dropped in to say howdy.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99m not going to have anybody following me around and trying to\nboss me. I\xe2\x80\x99ve had all of that I\xe2\x80\x99m going to stand!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, gosh, I\xe2\x80\x99m not--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t like the way you looked at May Arnold, or the snooty way you\ntalked.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, all right then! If you think I\xe2\x80\x99m a buttinsky, then I\xe2\x80\x99ll just butt\nin! I don\xe2\x80\x99t know who your May Arnold is, but I know doggone good and\nwell that you and her weren\xe2\x80\x99t talking about tar-roofing, no, nor about\nplaying the violin, neither! If you haven\xe2\x80\x99t got any moral consideration\nfor yourself, you ought to have some for your position in the community.\nThe idea of your going around places gawping into a female\xe2\x80\x99s eyes like a\nlove-sick pup! I can understand a fellow slipping once, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t\npropose to see a fellow that\xe2\x80\x99s been as chummy with me as you have\ngetting started on the downward path and sneaking off from his wife,\neven as cranky a one as Zilla, to go woman-chasing--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you\xe2\x80\x99re a perfectly moral little husband!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am, by God! I\xe2\x80\x99ve never looked at any woman except Myra since I\xe2\x80\x99ve\nbeen married--practically--and I never will! I tell you there\xe2\x80\x99s nothing\nto immorality. It don\xe2\x80\x99t pay. Can\xe2\x80\x99t you see, old man, it just makes Zilla\nstill crankier?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nSlight of resolution as he was of body, Paul threw his snow-beaded\novercoat on the floor and crouched on a flimsy cane chair. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, you\xe2\x80\x99re\nan old blowhard, and you know less about morality than Tinka, but you\xe2\x80\x99re\nall right, Georgie. But you can\xe2\x80\x99t understand that-- I\xe2\x80\x99m through. I can\xe2\x80\x99t\ngo Zilla\xe2\x80\x99s hammering any longer. She\xe2\x80\x99s made up her mind that I\xe2\x80\x99m a\ndevil, and-- Reg\xe2\x80\x99lar Inquisition. Torture. She enjoys it. It\xe2\x80\x99s a game to\nsee how sore she can make me. And me, either it\xe2\x80\x99s find a little\ncomfort, any comfort, anywhere, or else do something a lot worse. Now\nthis Mrs. Arnold, she\xe2\x80\x99s not so young, but she\xe2\x80\x99s a fine woman and she\nunderstands a fellow, and she\xe2\x80\x99s had her own troubles.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYea! I suppose she\xe2\x80\x99s one of these hens whose husband \xe2\x80\x98doesn\xe2\x80\x99t\nunderstand her\xe2\x80\x99!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know. Maybe. He was killed in the war.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBabbitt lumbered up, stood beside Paul patting his shoulder, making soft\napologetic noises.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHonest, George, she\xe2\x80\x99s a fine woman, and she\xe2\x80\x99s had one hell of a time.\nWe manage to jolly each other up a lot. We tell each other we\xe2\x80\x99re the\ndandiest pair on earth. Maybe we don\xe2\x80\x99t believe it, but it helps a lot to\nhave somebody with whom you can be perfectly simple, and not all this\ndiscussing--explaining--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd that\xe2\x80\x99s as far as you go?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not! Go on! Say it!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don\xe2\x80\x99t-- I can\xe2\x80\x99t say I like it, but--\xe2\x80\x9d With a burst which left\nhim feeling large and shining with generosity, \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s none of my darn\nbusiness! I\xe2\x80\x99ll do anything I can for you, if there\xe2\x80\x99s anything I can do.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere might be. I judge from Zilla\xe2\x80\x99s letters that\xe2\x80\x99ve been forwarded\nfrom Akron that she\xe2\x80\x99s getting suspicious about my staying away so long.\nShe\xe2\x80\x99d be perfectly capable of having me shadowed, and of coming to\nChicago and busting into a hotel dining-room and bawling me out before\neverybody.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll take care of Zilla. I\xe2\x80\x99ll hand her a good fairy-story when I get\nback to Zenith.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know-- I don\xe2\x80\x99t think you better try it. You\xe2\x80\x99re a good fellow,\nbut I don\xe2\x80\x99t know that diplomacy is your strong point.\xe2\x80\x9d Babbitt looked\nhurt, then irritated. \xe2\x80\x9cI mean with women! With women, I mean. Course\nthey got to go some to beat you in business diplomacy, but I just mean\nwith women. Zilla may do a lot of rough talking, but she\xe2\x80\x99s pretty\nshrewd. She\xe2\x80\x99d have the story out of you in no time.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, all right, but--\xe2\x80\x9d Babbitt was still pathetic at not being allowed\nto play Secret Agent. Paul soothed:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cCourse maybe you might tell her you\xe2\x80\x99d been in Akron and seen me there.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, sure, you bet! Don\xe2\x80\x99t I have to go look at that candy-store\nproperty in Akron? Don\xe2\x80\x99t I? Ain\xe2\x80\x99t it a shame I have to stop off there\nwhen I\xe2\x80\x99m so anxious to get home? Ain\xe2\x80\x99t it a regular shame? I\xe2\x80\x99ll say it\nis! I\xe2\x80\x99ll say it\xe2\x80\x99s a doggone shame!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cFine. But for glory hallelujah\xe2\x80\x99s sake don\xe2\x80\x99t go putting any fancy\nfixings on the story. When men lie they always try to make it too\nartistic, and that\xe2\x80\x99s why women get suspicious. And-- Let\xe2\x80\x99s have a drink,\nGeorgie. I\xe2\x80\x99ve got some gin and a little vermouth.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThe Paul who normally refused a second cocktail took a second now, and a\nthird. He became red-eyed and thick-tongued. He was embarrassingly\njocular and salacious.\n\nIn the taxicab Babbitt incredulously found tears crowding into his eyes.\n\n\nII\n\nHe had not told Paul of his plan but he did stop at Akron, between\ntrains, for the one purpose of sending to Zilla a post-card with \xe2\x80\x9cHad to\ncome here for the day, ran into Paul.\xe2\x80\x9d In Zenith he called on her. If\nfor public appearances Zilla was over-coiffed, over-painted, and\nresolutely corseted, for private misery she wore a filthy blue\ndressing-gown and torn stockings thrust into streaky pink satin mules.\nHer face was sunken. She seemed to have but half as much hair as Babbitt\nremembered, and that half was stringy. She sat in a rocker amid a debris\nof candy-boxes and cheap magazines, and she sounded dolorous when she\ndid not sound derisive. But Babbitt was exceedingly breezy:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, well, Zil, old dear, having a good loaf while hubby\xe2\x80\x99s away?\nThat\xe2\x80\x99s the idea! I\xe2\x80\x99ll bet a hat Myra never got up till ten, while I was\nin Chicago. Say, could I borrow your thermos--just dropped in to see if\nI could borrow your thermos bottle. We\xe2\x80\x99re going to have a toboggan\nparty--want to take some coffee _mit_. Oh, did you get my card from\nAkron, saying I\xe2\x80\x99d run into Paul?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. What was he doing?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d He unbuttoned his overcoat, sat tentatively on the\narm of a chair.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know how I mean!\xe2\x80\x9d She slapped the pages of a magazine with an\nirritable clatter. \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose he was trying to make love to some hotel\nwaitress or manicure girl or somebody.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHang it, you\xe2\x80\x99re always letting on that Paul goes round chasing skirts.\nHe doesn\xe2\x80\x99t, in the first place, and if he did, it would prob\xe2\x80\x99ly be\nbecause you keep hinting at him and dinging at him so much. I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t\nmeant to, Zilla, but since Paul is away, in Akron--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe really is in Akron? I know he has some horrible woman that he writes\nto in Chicago.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDidn\xe2\x80\x99t I tell you I saw him in Akron? What \xe2\x80\x99re you trying to do? Make\nme out a liar?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, but I just-- I get so worried.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, there you are! That\xe2\x80\x99s what gets me! Here you love Paul, and yet\nyou plague him and cuss him out as if you hated him. I simply can\xe2\x80\x99t\nunderstand why it is that the more some folks love people, the harder\nthey try to make \xe2\x80\x99em miserable.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou love Ted and Rone-- I suppose--and yet you nag them.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh. Well. That. That\xe2\x80\x99s different. Besides, I don\xe2\x80\x99t nag \xe2\x80\x99em. Not what\nyou\xe2\x80\x99d call nagging. But zize saying: Now, here\xe2\x80\x99s Paul, the nicest, most\nsensitive critter on God\xe2\x80\x99s green earth. You ought to be ashamed of\nyourself the way you pan him. Why, you talk to him like a washerwoman.\nI\xe2\x80\x99m surprised you can act so doggone common, Zilla!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nShe brooded over her linked fingers. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, I know. I do go and get mean\nsometimes, and I\xe2\x80\x99m sorry afterwards. But, oh, Georgie, Paul is so\naggravating! Honestly, I\xe2\x80\x99ve tried awfully hard, these last few years, to\nbe nice to him, but just because I used to be spiteful--or I seemed so;\nI wasn\xe2\x80\x99t, really, but I used to speak up and say anything that came into\nmy head--and so he made up his mind that everything was my fault.\nEverything can\xe2\x80\x99t always be my fault, can it? And now if I get to\nfussing, he just turns silent, oh, so dreadfully silent, and he won\xe2\x80\x99t\nlook at me--he just ignores me. He simply isn\xe2\x80\x99t human! And he\ndeliberately keeps it up till I bust out and say a lot of things I don\xe2\x80\x99t\nmean. So silent-- Oh, you righteous men! How wicked you are! How rotten\nwicked!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThey thrashed things over and over for half an hour. At the end, weeping\ndrably, Zilla promised to restrain herself.\n\nPaul returned four days later, and the Babbitts and Rieslings went\nfestively to the movies and had chop suey at a Chinese restaurant. As\nthey walked to the restaurant through a street of tailor shops and\nbarber shops, the two wives in front, chattering about cooks, Babbitt\nmurmured to Paul, \xe2\x80\x9cZil seems a lot nicer now.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, she has been, except once or twice. But it\xe2\x80\x99s too late now. I\njust-- I\xe2\x80\x99m not going to discuss it, but I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid of her. There\xe2\x80\x99s\nnothing left. I don\xe2\x80\x99t ever want to see her. Some day I\xe2\x80\x99m going to break\naway from her. Somehow.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\n\nCHAPTER XXI\n\n\nThe International Organization of Boosters\xe2\x80\x99 Clubs has become a\nworld-force for optimism, manly pleasantry, and good business. Chapters\nare to be found now in thirty countries. Nine hundred and twenty of the\nthousand chapters, however, are in the United States.\n\nNone of these is more ardent than the Zenith Boosters\xe2\x80\x99 Club.\n\nThe second March lunch of the Zenith Boosters was the most important of\nthe year, as it was to be followed by the annual election of officers.\nThere was agitation abroad. The lunch was held in the ballroom of the\nO\xe2\x80\x99Hearn House. As each of the four hundred Boosters entered he took from\na wallboard a huge celluloid button announcing his name, his nickname,\nand his business. There was a fine of ten cents for calling a Fellow\nBooster by anything but his nickname at a lunch, and as Babbitt jovially\nchecked his hat the air was radiant with shouts of \xe2\x80\x9cHello, Chet!\xe2\x80\x9d and\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow\xe2\x80\x99re you, Shorty!\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cTop o\xe2\x80\x99 the mornin\xe2\x80\x99, Mac!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThey sat at friendly tables for eight, choosing places by lot. Babbitt\nwas with Albert Boos the merchant tailor, Hector Seybolt of the Little\nSweetheart Condensed Milk Company, Emil Wengert the jeweler, Professor\nPumphrey of the Riteway Business College, Dr. Walter Gorbutt, Roy\nTeegarten the photographer, and Ben Berkey the photo-engraver. One of\nthe merits of the Boosters\xe2\x80\x99 Club was that only two persons from each\ndepartment of business were permitted to join, so that you at once\nencountered the Ideals of other occupations, and realized the\nmetaphysical oneness of all occupations--plumbing and portrait-painting,\nmedicine and the manufacture of chewing-gum.\n\nBabbitt\xe2\x80\x99s table was particularly happy to-day, because Professor\nPumphrey had just had a birthday, and was therefore open to teasing.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s pump Pump about how old he is!\xe2\x80\x9d said Emil Wengert.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, let\xe2\x80\x99s paddle him with a dancing-pump!\xe2\x80\x9d said Ben Berkey.\n\nBut it was Babbitt who had the applause, with \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t talk about pumps to\nthat guy! The only pump he knows is a bottle! Honest, they tell me he\xe2\x80\x99s\nstarting a class in home-brewing at the ole college!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAt each place was the Boosters\xe2\x80\x99 Club booklet, listing the members.\nThough the object of the club was good-fellowship, yet they never lost\nsight of the importance of doing a little more business. After each name\nwas the member\xe2\x80\x99s occupation. There were scores of advertisements in the\nbooklet, and on one page the admonition: \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s no rule that you have\nto trade with your Fellow Boosters, but get wise, boy--what\xe2\x80\x99s the use of\nletting all this good money get outside of our happy fambly?\xe2\x80\x9d And at\neach place, to-day, there was a present; a card printed in artistic red\nand black:\n\n                        SERVICE AND BOOSTERISM\n\n     Service finds its finest opportunity and development only in its\n     broadest and deepest application and the consideration of its\n     perpetual action upon reaction. I believe the highest type of\n     Service, like the most progressive tenets of ethics, senses\n     unceasingly and is motived by active adherence and loyalty to that\n     which is the essential principle of Boosterism--Good Citizenship in\n     all its factors and aspects.\n\n                                                          DAD PETERSEN.\n\n           Compliments of Dadbury Petersen Advertising Corp.\n\n                       \xe2\x80\x9cAds, not Fads, at Dad\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nThe Boosters all read Mr. Peterson\xe2\x80\x99s aphorism and said they understood\nit perfectly.\n\nThe meeting opened with the regular weekly \xe2\x80\x9cstunts.\xe2\x80\x9d Retiring President\nVergil Gunch was in the chair, his stiff hair like a hedge, his voice\nlike a brazen gong of festival. Members who had brought guests\nintroduced them publicly. \xe2\x80\x9cThis tall red-headed piece of misinformation\nis the sporting editor of the _Press_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Willis Ijams; and H. H.\nHazen, the druggist, chanted, \xe2\x80\x9cBoys, when you\xe2\x80\x99re on a long motor tour\nand finally get to a romantic spot or scene and draw up and remark to\nthe wife, \xe2\x80\x98This is certainly a romantic place,\xe2\x80\x99 it sends a glow right up\nand down your vertebr\xc3\xa6. Well, my guest to-day is from such a place,\nHarper\xe2\x80\x99s Ferry, Virginia, in the beautiful Southland, with memories of\ngood old General Robert E. Lee and of that brave soul, John Brown who,\nlike every good Booster, goes marching on--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThere were two especially distinguished guests: the leading man of the\n\xe2\x80\x9cBird of Paradise\xe2\x80\x9d company, playing this week at the Dodsworth Theater,\nand the mayor of Zenith, the Hon. Lucas Prout.\n\nVergil Gunch thundered, \xe2\x80\x9cWhen we manage to grab this celebrated Thespian\noff his lovely aggregation of beautiful actresses--and I got to admit I\nbutted right into his dressing-room and told him how the Boosters\nappreciated the high-class artistic performance he\xe2\x80\x99s giving us--and\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t forget that the treasurer of the Dodsworth is a Booster and will\nappreciate our patronage--and when on top of that we yank Hizzonor out\nof his multifarious duties at City Hall, then I feel we\xe2\x80\x99ve done\nourselves proud, and Mr. Prout will now say a few words about the\nproblems and duties--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBy rising vote the Boosters decided which was the handsomest and which\nthe ugliest guest, and to each of them was given a bunch of carnations,\ndonated, President Gunch noted, by Brother Booster H. G. Yeager, the\nJennifer Avenue florist.\n\nEach week, in rotation, four Boosters were privileged to obtain the\npleasures of generosity and of publicity by donating goods or services\nto four fellow-members, chosen by lot. There was laughter, this week,\nwhen it was announced that one of the contributors was Barnabas Joy, the\nundertaker. Everybody whispered, \xe2\x80\x9cI can think of a coupla good guys to\nbe buried if his donation is a free funeral!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThrough all these diversions the Boosters were lunching on chicken\ncroquettes, peas, fried potatoes, coffee, apple pie, and American\ncheese. Gunch did not lump the speeches. Presently he called on the\nvisiting secretary of the Zenith Rotary Club, a rival organization. The\nsecretary had the distinction of possessing State Motor Car License\nNumber 5.\n\nThe Rotary secretary laughingly admitted that wherever he drove in the\nstate so low a number created a sensation, and \xe2\x80\x9cthough it was pretty\nnice to have the honor, yet traffic cops remembered it only too darn\nwell, and sometimes he didn\xe2\x80\x99t know but what he\xe2\x80\x99d almost as soon have\njust plain B56,876 or something like that. Only let any doggone Booster\ntry to get Number 5 away from a live Rotarian next year, and watch the\nfur fly! And if they\xe2\x80\x99d permit him, he\xe2\x80\x99d wind up by calling for a cheer\nfor the Boosters and Rotarians and the Kiwanis all together!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBabbitt sighed to Professor Pumphrey, \xe2\x80\x9cBe pretty nice to have as low a\nnumber as that! Everybody\xe2\x80\x99d say, \xe2\x80\x98He must be an important guy!\xe2\x80\x99 Wonder\nhow he got it? I\xe2\x80\x99ll bet he wined and dined the superintendent of the\nMotor License Bureau to a fare-you-well!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThen Chum Frink addressed them:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSome of you may feel that it\xe2\x80\x99s out of place here to talk on a strictly\nhighbrow and artistic subject, but I want to come out flatfooted and ask\nyou boys to O.K. the proposition of a Symphony Orchestra for Zenith.\nNow, where a lot of you make your mistake is in assuming that if you\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t like classical music and all that junk, you ought to oppose it.\nNow, I want to confess that, though I\xe2\x80\x99m a literary guy by profession, I\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t care a rap for all this long-haired music. I\xe2\x80\x99d rather listen to a\ngood jazz band any time than to some piece by Beethoven that hasn\xe2\x80\x99t any\nmore tune to it than a bunch of fighting cats, and you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t whistle\nit to save your life! But that isn\xe2\x80\x99t the point. Culture has become as\nnecessary an adornment and advertisement for a city to-day as pavements\nor bank-clearances. It\xe2\x80\x99s Culture, in theaters and art-galleries and so\non, that brings thousands of visitors to New York every year and, to be\nfrank, for all our splendid attainments we haven\xe2\x80\x99t yet got the Culture\nof a New York or Chicago or Boston--or at least we don\xe2\x80\x99t get the credit\nfor it. The thing to do then, as a live bunch of go-getters, is to\n_capitalize Culture_; to go right out and grab it.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cPictures and books are fine for those that have the time to study \xe2\x80\x99em,\nbut they don\xe2\x80\x99t shoot out on the road and holler \xe2\x80\x98This is what little old\nZenith can put up in the way of Culture.\xe2\x80\x99 That\xe2\x80\x99s precisely what a\nSymphony Orchestra does do. Look at the credit Minneapolis and\nCincinnati get. An orchestra with first-class musickers and a swell\nconductor--and I believe we ought to do the thing up brown and get one\nof the highest-paid conductors on the market, providing he ain\xe2\x80\x99t a\nHun--it goes right into Beantown and New York and Washington; it plays\nat the best theaters to the most cultured and moneyed people; it gives\nsuch class-advertising as a town can get in no other way; and the guy\nwho is so short-sighted as to crab this orchestra proposition is passing\nup the chance to impress the glorious name of Zenith on some big New\nYork millionaire that might--that might establish a branch factory here!\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI could also go into the fact that for our daughters who show an\ninterest in highbrow music and may want to teach it, having an A1 local\norganization is of great benefit, but let\xe2\x80\x99s keep this on a practical\nbasis, and I call on you good brothers to whoop it up for Culture and a\nWorld-beating Symphony Orchestra!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThey applauded.\n\nTo a rustle of excitement President Gunch proclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cGentlemen, we\nwill now proceed to the annual election of officers.\xe2\x80\x9d For each of the\nsix offices, three candidates had been chosen by a committee. The second\nname among the candidates for vice-president was Babbitt\xe2\x80\x99s.\n\nHe was surprised. He looked self-conscious. His heart pounded. He was\nstill more agitated when the ballots were counted and Gunch said, \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s\na pleasure to announce that Georgie Babbitt will be the next assistant\ngavel-wielder. I know of no man who stands more stanchly for common\nsense and enterprise than good old George. Come on, let\xe2\x80\x99s give him our\nbest long yell!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAs they adjourned, a hundred men crushed in to slap his back. He had\nnever known a higher moment. He drove away in a blur of wonder. He\nlunged into his office, chuckling to Miss McGoun, \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I guess you\nbetter congratulate your boss! Been elected vice-president of the\nBoosters!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe was disappointed. She answered only, \xe2\x80\x9cYes-- Oh, Mrs. Babbitt\xe2\x80\x99s been\ntrying to get you on the \xe2\x80\x99phone.\xe2\x80\x9d But the new salesman, Fritz Weilinger,\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cBy golly, chief, say, that\xe2\x80\x99s great, that\xe2\x80\x99s perfectly great! I\xe2\x80\x99m\ntickled to death! Congratulations!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBabbitt called the house, and crowed to his wife, \xe2\x80\x9cHeard you were trying\nto get me, Myra. Say, you got to hand it to little Georgie, this time!\nBetter talk careful! You are now addressing the vice-president of the\nBoosters\xe2\x80\x99 Club!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Georgie--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cPretty nice, huh? Willis Ijams is the new president, but when he\xe2\x80\x99s\naway, little ole Georgie takes the gavel and whoops \xe2\x80\x99em up and\nintroduces the speakers--no matter if they\xe2\x80\x99re the governor\nhimself--and--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGeorge! Listen!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9c--it puts him in solid with big men like Doc Dilling and--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGeorge! Paul Riesling--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sure, I\xe2\x80\x99ll \xe2\x80\x99phone Paul and let him know about it right away.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGeorgie! _Listen!_ Paul\xe2\x80\x99s in jail. He shot his wife, he shot Zilla,\nthis noon. She may not live.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\n\nCHAPTER XXII\n\n\nI\n\nHe drove to the City Prison, not blindly, but with unusual fussy care at\ncorners, the fussiness of an old woman potting plants. It kept him from\nfacing the obscenity of fate.\n\nThe attendant said, \xe2\x80\x9cNaw, you can\xe2\x80\x99t see any of the prisoners till\nthree-thirty--visiting-hour.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nIt was three. For half an hour Babbitt sat looking at a calendar and a\nclock on a whitewashed wall. The chair was hard and mean and creaky.\nPeople went through the office and, he thought, stared at him. He felt a\nbelligerent defiance which broke into a wincing fear of this machine\nwhich was grinding Paul--Paul--\n\nExactly at half-past three he sent in his name.\n\nThe attendant returned with \xe2\x80\x9cRiesling says he don\xe2\x80\x99t want to see you.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re crazy! You didn\xe2\x80\x99t give him my name! Tell him it\xe2\x80\x99s George wants\nto see him, George Babbitt.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYuh, I told him, all right, all right! He said he didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to see\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen take me in anyway.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing doing. If you ain\xe2\x80\x99t his lawyer, if he don\xe2\x80\x99t want to see you,\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s all there is to it.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, my _God_-- Say, let me see the warden.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s busy. Come on, now, you--\xe2\x80\x9d Babbitt reared over him. The attendant\nhastily changed to a coaxing \xe2\x80\x9cYou can come back and try to-morrow.\nProbably the poor guy is off his nut.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBabbitt drove, not at all carefully or fussily, sliding viciously past\ntrucks, ignoring the truckmen\xe2\x80\x99s curses, to the City Hall; he stopped\nwith a grind of wheels against the curb, and ran up the marble steps to\nthe office of the Hon. Mr. Lucas Prout, the mayor. He bribed the mayor\xe2\x80\x99s\ndoorman with a dollar; he was instantly inside, demanding, \xe2\x80\x9cYou remember\nme, Mr. Prout? Babbitt--vice-president of the Boosters--campaigned for\nyou? Say, have you heard about poor Riesling? Well, I want an order on\nthe warden or whatever you call um of the City Prison to take me back\nand see him. Good. Thanks.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nIn fifteen minutes he was pounding down the prison corridor to a cage\nwhere Paul Riesling sat on a cot, twisted like an old beggar, legs\ncrossed, arms in a knot, biting at his clenched fist.\n\nPaul looked up blankly as the keeper unlocked the cell, admitted\nBabbitt, and left them together. He spoke slowly: \xe2\x80\x9cGo on! Be moral!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBabbitt plumped on the couch beside him. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not going to be moral! I\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t care what happened! I just want to do anything I can. I\xe2\x80\x99m glad\nZilla got what was coming to her.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nPaul said argumentatively, \xe2\x80\x9cNow, don\xe2\x80\x99t go jumping on Zilla. I\xe2\x80\x99ve been\nthinking; maybe she hasn\xe2\x80\x99t had any too easy a time. Just after I shot\nher-- I didn\xe2\x80\x99t hardly mean to, but she got to deviling me so I went\ncrazy, just for a second, and pulled out that old revolver you and I\nused to shoot rabbits with, and took a crack at her. Didn\xe2\x80\x99t hardly mean\nto-- After that, when I was trying to stop the blood-- It was terrible\nwhat it did to her shoulder, and she had beautiful skin-- Maybe she won\xe2\x80\x99t\ndie. I hope it won\xe2\x80\x99t leave her skin all scarred. But just afterward,\nwhen I was hunting through the bathroom for some cotton to stop the\nblood, I ran onto a little fuzzy yellow duck we hung on the tree one\nChristmas, and I remembered she and I\xe2\x80\x99d been awfully happy then-- Hell. I\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t hardly believe it\xe2\x80\x99s me here.\xe2\x80\x9d As Babbitt\xe2\x80\x99s arm tightened about his\nshoulder, Paul sighed, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m glad you came. But I thought maybe you\xe2\x80\x99d\nlecture me, and when you\xe2\x80\x99ve committed a murder, and been brought here\nand everything--there was a big crowd outside the apartment house, all\nstaring, and the cops took me through it-- Oh, I\xe2\x80\x99m not going to talk\nabout it any more.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBut he went on, in a monotonous, terrified insane mumble. To divert him\nBabbitt said, \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you got a scar on your cheek.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. That\xe2\x80\x99s where the cop hit me. I suppose cops get a lot of fun out\nof lecturing murderers, too. He was a big fellow. And they wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t let\nme help carry Zilla down to the ambulance.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cPaul! Quit it! Listen: she won\xe2\x80\x99t die, and when it\xe2\x80\x99s all over you and\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll go off to Maine again. And maybe we can get that May Arnold to go\nalong. I\xe2\x80\x99ll go up to Chicago and ask her. Good woman, by golly. And\nafterwards I\xe2\x80\x99ll see that you get started in business out West somewhere,\nmaybe Seattle--they say that\xe2\x80\x99s a lovely city.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nPaul was half smiling. It was Babbitt who rambled now. He could not tell\nwhether Paul was heeding, but he droned on till the coming of Paul\xe2\x80\x99s\nlawyer, P. J. Maxwell, a thin, busy, unfriendly man who nodded at\nBabbitt and hinted, \xe2\x80\x9cIf Riesling and I could be alone for a moment--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBabbitt wrung Paul\xe2\x80\x99s hands, and waited in the office till Maxwell came\npattering out. \xe2\x80\x9cLook, old man, what can I do?\xe2\x80\x9d he begged.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing. Not a thing. Not just now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Maxwell. \xe2\x80\x9cSorry. Got to\nhurry. And don\xe2\x80\x99t try to see him. I\xe2\x80\x99ve had the doctor give him a shot of\nmorphine, so he\xe2\x80\x99ll sleep.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nIt seemed somehow wicked to return to the office. Babbitt felt as though\nhe had just come from a funeral. He drifted out to the City Hospital to\ninquire about Zilla. She was not likely to die, he learned. The bullet\nfrom Paul\xe2\x80\x99s huge old .44 army revolver had smashed her shoulder and torn\nupward and out.\n\nHe wandered home and found his wife radiant with the horrified interest\nwe have in the tragedies of our friends. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course Paul isn\xe2\x80\x99t\naltogether to blame, but this is what comes of his chasing after other\nwomen instead of bearing his cross in a Christian way,\xe2\x80\x9d she exulted.\n\nHe was too languid to respond as he desired. He said what was to be said\nabout the Christian bearing of crosses, and went out to clean the car.\nDully, patiently, he scraped linty grease from the drip-pan, gouged at\nthe mud caked on the wheels. He used up many minutes in washing his\nhands; scoured them with gritty kitchen soap; rejoiced in hurting his\nplump knuckles. \xe2\x80\x9cDamn soft hands--like a woman\xe2\x80\x99s. Aah!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAt dinner, when his wife began the inevitable, he bellowed, \xe2\x80\x9cI forbid\nany of you to say a word about Paul! I\xe2\x80\x99ll \xe2\x80\x99tend to all the talking about\nthis that\xe2\x80\x99s necessary, hear me? There\xe2\x80\x99s going to be one house in this\nscandal-mongering town to-night that isn\xe2\x80\x99t going to spring the\nholier-than-thou. And throw those filthy evening papers out of the\nhouse!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBut he himself read the papers, after dinner.\n\nBefore nine he set out for the house of Lawyer Maxwell. He was received\nwithout cordiality. \xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d said Maxwell.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want to offer my services in the trial. I\xe2\x80\x99ve got an idea. Why\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t I go on the stand and swear I was there, and she pulled the gun\nfirst and he wrestled with her and the gun went off accidentally?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd perjure yourself?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuh? Yes, I suppose it would be perjury. Oh-- Would it help?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, my dear fellow! Perjury!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t be a fool! Excuse me, Maxwell; I didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean to get your\ngoat. I just mean: I\xe2\x80\x99ve known and you\xe2\x80\x99ve known many and many a case of\nperjury, just to annex some rotten little piece of real estate, and here\nwhere it\xe2\x80\x99s a case of saving Paul from going to prison, I\xe2\x80\x99d perjure\nmyself black in the face.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. Aside from the ethics of the matter, I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid it isn\xe2\x80\x99t\npracticable. The prosecutor would tear your testimony to pieces. It\xe2\x80\x99s\nknown that only Riesling and his wife were there at the time.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, look here! Let me go on the stand and swear--and this would be\nthe God\xe2\x80\x99s truth--that she pestered him till he kind of went crazy.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. Sorry. Riesling absolutely refuses to have any testimony reflecting\non his wife. He insists on pleading guilty.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen let me get up and testify something--whatever you say. Let me do\n_something_!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sorry, Babbitt, but the best thing you can do-- I hate to say it,\nbut you could help us most by keeping strictly out of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBabbitt, revolving his hat like a defaulting poor tenant, winced so\nvisibly that Maxwell condescended:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like to hurt your feelings, but you see we both want to do our\nbest for Riesling, and we mustn\xe2\x80\x99t consider any other factor. The trouble\nwith you, Babbitt, is that you\xe2\x80\x99re one of these fellows who talk too\nreadily. You like to hear your own voice. If there were anything for\nwhich I could put you in the witness-box, you\xe2\x80\x99d get going and give the\nwhole show away. Sorry. Now I must look over some papers-- So sorry.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\nII\n\nHe spent most of the next morning nerving himself to face the garrulous\nworld of the Athletic Club. They would talk about Paul; they would be\nlip-licking and rotten. But at the Roughnecks\xe2\x80\x99 Table they did not\nmention Paul. They spoke with zeal of the coming baseball season. He\nloved them as he never had before.\n\n\nIII\n\nHe had, doubtless from some story-book, pictured Paul\xe2\x80\x99s trial as a long\nstruggle, with bitter arguments, a taut crowd, and sudden and\noverwhelming new testimony. Actually, the trial occupied less than\nfifteen minutes, largely filled with the evidence of doctors that Zilla\nwould recover and that Paul must have been temporarily insane. Next day\nPaul was sentenced to three years in the State Penitentiary and taken\noff--quite undramatically, not handcuffed, merely plodding in a tired\nway beside a cheerful deputy sheriff--and after saying good-by to him at\nthe station Babbitt returned to his office to realize that he faced a\nworld which, without Paul, was meaningless.\n\n\n\n\nCHAPTER XXIII\n\n\nI\n\nHe was busy, from March to June. He kept himself from the bewilderment\nof thinking. His wife and the neighbors were generous. Every evening he\nplayed bridge or attended the movies, and the days were blank of face\nand silent.\n\nIn June, Mrs. Babbitt and Tinka went East, to stay with relatives, and\nBabbitt was free to do--he was not quite sure what.\n\nAll day long after their departure he thought of the emancipated house\nin which he could, if he desired, go mad and curse the gods without\nhaving to keep up a husbandly front. He considered, \xe2\x80\x9cI could have a\nreg\xe2\x80\x99lar party to-night; stay out till two and not do any explaining\nafterwards. Cheers!\xe2\x80\x9d He telephoned to Vergil Gunch, to Eddie Swanson.\nBoth of them were engaged for the evening, and suddenly he was bored by\nhaving to take so much trouble to be riotous.\n\nHe was silent at dinner, unusually kindly to Ted and Verona, hesitating\nbut not disapproving when Verona stated her opinion of Kenneth Escott\xe2\x80\x99s\nopinion of Dr. John Jennison Drew\xe2\x80\x99s opinion of the opinions of the\nevolutionists. Ted was working in a garage through the summer vacation,\nand he related his daily triumphs: how he had found a cracked ball-race,\nwhat he had said to the Old Grouch, what he had said to the foreman\nabout the future of wireless telephony.\n\nTed and Verona went to a dance after dinner. Even the maid was out.\nRarely had Babbitt been alone in the house for an entire evening. He was\nrestless. He vaguely wanted something more diverting than the newspaper\ncomic strips to read. He ambled up to Verona\xe2\x80\x99s room, sat on her\nmaidenly blue and white bed, humming and grunting in a solid-citizen\nmanner as he examined her books: Conrad\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cRescue,\xe2\x80\x9d a volume strangely\nnamed \xe2\x80\x9cFigures of Earth,\xe2\x80\x9d poetry (quite irregular poetry, Babbitt\nthought) by Vachel Lindsay, and essays by H. L. Mencken--highly improper\nessays, making fun of the church and all the decencies. He liked none of\nthe books. In them he felt a spirit of rebellion against niceness and\nsolid-citizenship. These authors--and he supposed they were famous ones,\ntoo--did not seem to care about telling a good story which would enable\na fellow to forget his troubles. He sighed. He noted a book, \xe2\x80\x9cThe Three\nBlack Pennies,\xe2\x80\x9d by Joseph Hergesheimer. Ah, that was something like it!\nIt would be an adventure story, maybe about counterfeiting--detectives\nsneaking up on the old house at night. He tucked the book under his arm,\nhe clumped down-stairs and solemnly began to read, under the piano-lamp:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cA twilight like blue dust sifted into the shallow fold of the thickly\nwooded hills. It was early October, but a crisping frost had already\nstamped the maple trees with gold, the Spanish oaks were hung with\npatches of wine red, the sumach was brilliant in the darkening\nunderbrush. A pattern of wild geese, flying low and unconcerned above\nthe hills, wavered against the serene ashen evening. Howat Penny,\nstanding in the comparative clearing of a road, decided that the\nshifting regular flight would not come close enough for a shot.... He\nhad no intention of hunting the geese. With the drooping of day his\nkeenness had evaporated; an habitual indifference strengthened,\npermeating him....\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThere it was again: discontent with the good common ways. Babbitt laid\ndown the book and listened to the stillness. The inner doors of the\nhouse were open. He heard from the kitchen the steady drip of the\nrefrigerator, a rhythm demanding and disquieting. He roamed to the\nwindow. The summer evening was foggy and, seen through the wire screen,\nthe street lamps were crosses of pale fire. The whole world was\nabnormal. While he brooded, Verona and Ted came in and went up to bed.\nSilence thickened in the sleeping house. He put on his hat, his\nrespectable derby, lighted a cigar, and walked up and down before the\nhouse, a portly, worthy, unimaginative figure, humming \xe2\x80\x9cSilver Threads\namong the Gold.\xe2\x80\x9d He casually considered, \xe2\x80\x9cMight call up Paul.\xe2\x80\x9d Then he\nremembered. He saw Paul in a jailbird\xe2\x80\x99s uniform, but while he agonized\nhe didn\xe2\x80\x99t believe the tale. It was part of the unreality of this\nfog-enchanted evening.\n\nIf she were here Myra would be hinting, \xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t it late, Georgie?\xe2\x80\x9d He\ntramped in forlorn and unwanted freedom. Fog hid the house now. The\nworld was uncreated, a chaos without turmoil or desire.\n\nThrough the mist came a man at so feverish a pace that he seemed to\ndance with fury as he entered the orb of glow from a street-lamp. At\neach step he brandished his stick and brought it down with a crash. His\nglasses on their broad pretentious ribbon banged against his stomach.\nBabbitt incredulously saw that it was Chum Frink.\n\nFrink stopped, focused his vision, and spoke with gravity:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s another fool. George Babbitt. Lives for renting\nhowshes--houses. Know who I am? I\xe2\x80\x99m traitor to poetry. I\xe2\x80\x99m drunk. I\xe2\x80\x99m\ntalking too much. I don\xe2\x80\x99t care. Know what I could\xe2\x80\x99ve been? I could\xe2\x80\x99ve\nbeen a Gene Field or a James Whitcomb Riley. Maybe a Stevenson. I\ncould\xe2\x80\x99ve. Whimsies. \xe2\x80\x99Magination. Lissen. Lissen to this. Just made it\nup:\n\n    Glittering summery meadowy noise\n    Of beetles and bums and respectable boys.\n\nHear that? Whimzh--whimsy. I made that up. _I_ don\xe2\x80\x99t know what it means!\nBeginning good verse. Chile\xe2\x80\x99s Garden Verses. And whadi write? Tripe!\nCheer-up poems. All tripe! Could have written-- Too late!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe darted on with an alarming plunge, seeming always to pitch forward\nyet never quite falling. Babbitt would have been no more astonished and\nno less had a ghost skipped out of the fog carrying his head. He\naccepted Frink with vast apathy; he grunted, \xe2\x80\x9cPoor boob!\xe2\x80\x9d and\nstraightway forgot him.\n\nHe plodded into the house, deliberately went to the refrigerator and\nrifled it. When Mrs. Babbitt was at home, this was one of the major\nhousehold crimes. He stood before the covered laundry tubs, eating a\nchicken leg and half a saucer of raspberry jelly, and grumbling over a\nclammy cold boiled potato. He was thinking. It was coming to him that\nperhaps all life as he knew it and vigorously practised it was futile;\nthat heaven as portrayed by the Reverend Dr. John Jennison Drew was\nneither probable nor very interesting; that he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t much pleasure out\nof making money; that it was of doubtful worth to rear children merely\nthat they might rear children who would rear children. What was it all\nabout? What did he want?\n\nHe blundered into the living-room, lay on the davenport, hands behind\nhis head.\n\nWhat did he want? Wealth? Social position? Travel? Servants? Yes, but\nonly incidentally.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI give it up,\xe2\x80\x9d he sighed.\n\nBut he did know that he wanted the presence of Paul Riesling; and from\nthat he stumbled into the admission that he wanted the fairy girl--in\nthe flesh. If there had been a woman whom he loved, he would have fled\nto her, humbled his forehead on her knees.\n\nHe thought of his stenographer, Miss McGoun. He thought of the prettiest\nof the manicure girls at the Hotel Thornleigh barber shop. As he fell\nasleep on the davenport he felt that he had found something in life, and\nthat he had made a terrifying, thrilling break with everything that was\ndecent and normal.\n\n\nII\n\nHe had forgotten, next morning, that he was a conscious rebel, but he\nwas irritable in the office and at the eleven o\xe2\x80\x99clock drive of telephone\ncalls and visitors he did something he had often desired and never\ndared: he left the office without excuses to those slave-drivers his\nemployees, and went to the movies. He enjoyed the right to be alone. He\ncame out with a vicious determination to do what he pleased.\n\nAs he approached the Roughnecks\xe2\x80\x99 Table at the club, everybody laughed.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, here\xe2\x80\x99s the millionaire!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sidney Finkelstein.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I saw him in his Locomobile!\xe2\x80\x9d said Professor Pumphrey.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGosh, it must be great to be a smart guy like Georgie!\xe2\x80\x9d moaned Vergil\nGunch. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s probably stolen all of Dorchester. I\xe2\x80\x99d hate to leave a poor\nlittle defenseless piece of property lying around where he could get his\nhooks on it!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThey had, Babbitt perceived, \xe2\x80\x9csomething on him.\xe2\x80\x9d Also, they \xe2\x80\x9chad their\nkidding clothes on.\xe2\x80\x9d Ordinarily he would have been delighted at the\nhonor implied in being chaffed, but he was suddenly touchy. He grunted,\n\xe2\x80\x9cYuh, sure; maybe I\xe2\x80\x99ll take you guys on as office boys!\xe2\x80\x9d He was\nimpatient as the jest elaborately rolled on to its d\xc3\xa9nouement.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course he may have been meeting a girl,\xe2\x80\x9d they said, and \xe2\x80\x9cNo, I think\nhe was waiting for his old roommate, Sir Jerusalem Doak.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe exploded, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, spring it, spring it, you boneheads! What\xe2\x80\x99s the great\njoke?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHurray! George is peeved!\xe2\x80\x9d snickered Sidney Finkelstein, while a grin\nwent round the table. Gunch revealed the shocking truth: He had seen\nBabbitt coming out of a motion-picture theater--at noon!\n\nThey kept it up. With a hundred variations, a hundred guffaws, they\nsaid that he had gone to the movies during business-hours. He didn\xe2\x80\x99t so\nmuch mind Gunch, but he was annoyed by Sidney Finkelstein, that brisk,\nlean, red-headed explainer of jokes. He was bothered, too, by the lump\nof ice in his glass of water. It was too large; it spun round and burned\nhis nose when he tried to drink. He raged that Finkelstein was like that\nlump of ice. But he won through; he kept up his banter till they grew\ntired of the superlative jest and turned to the great problems of the\nday.\n\nHe reflected, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter with me to-day? Seems like I\xe2\x80\x99ve got an\nawful grouch. Only they talk so darn much. But I better steer careful\nand keep my mouth shut.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAs they lighted their cigars he mumbled, \xe2\x80\x9cGot to get back,\xe2\x80\x9d and on a\nchorus of \xe2\x80\x9cIf you _will_ go spending your mornings with lady ushers at\nthe movies!\xe2\x80\x9d he escaped. He heard them giggling. He was embarrassed.\nWhile he was most bombastically agreeing with the coat-man that the\nweather was warm, he was conscious that he was longing to run childishly\nwith his troubles to the comfort of the fairy child.\n\n\nIII\n\nHe kept Miss McGoun after he had finished dictating. He searched for a\ntopic which would warm her office impersonality into friendliness.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere you going on your vacation?\xe2\x80\x9d he purred.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I\xe2\x80\x99ll go up-state to a farm do you want me to have the Siddons\nlease copied this afternoon?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no hurry about it.... I suppose you have a great time when you get\naway from us cranks in the office.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nShe rose and gathered her pencils. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, nobody\xe2\x80\x99s cranky here I think I\ncan get it copied after I do the letters.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nShe was gone. Babbitt utterly repudiated the view that he had been\ntrying to discover how approachable was Miss McGoun. \xe2\x80\x9cCourse! knew there\nwas nothing doing!\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\n\n\nIV\n\nEddie Swanson, the motor-car agent who lived across the street from\nBabbitt, was giving a Sunday supper. His wife Louetta, young Louetta who\nloved jazz in music and in clothes and laughter, was at her wildest. She\ncried, \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll have a real party!\xe2\x80\x9d as she received the guests. Babbitt\nhad uneasily felt that to many men she might be alluring; now he\nadmitted that to himself she was overwhelmingly alluring. Mrs. Babbitt\nhad never quite approved of Louetta; Babbitt was glad that she was not\nhere this evening.\n\nHe insisted on helping Louetta in the kitchen: taking the chicken\ncroquettes from the warming-oven, the lettuce sandwiches from the\nice-box. He held her hand, once, and she depressingly didn\xe2\x80\x99t notice it.\nShe caroled, \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re a good little mother\xe2\x80\x99s-helper, Georgie. Now trot in\nwith the tray and leave it on the side-table.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe wished that Eddie Swanson would give them cocktails; that Louetta\nwould have one. He wanted-- Oh, he wanted to be one of these Bohemians\nyou read about. Studio parties. Wild lovely girls who were independent.\nNot necessarily bad. Certainly not! But not tame, like Floral Heights.\nHow he\xe2\x80\x99d ever stood it all these years--\n\nEddie did not give them cocktails. True, they supped with mirth, and\nwith several repetitions by Orville Jones of \xe2\x80\x9cAny time Louetta wants to\ncome sit on my lap I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell this sandwich to beat it!\xe2\x80\x9d but they were\nrespectable, as befitted Sunday evening. Babbitt had discreetly\npre\xc3\xabmpted a place beside Louetta on the piano bench. While he talked\nabout motors, while he listened with a fixed smile to her account of the\nfilm she had seen last Wednesday, while he hoped that she would hurry up\nand finish her description of the plot, the beauty of the leading man,\nand the luxury of the setting, he studied her. Slim waist girdled with\nraw silk, strong brows, ardent eyes, hair parted above a broad\nforehead--she meant youth to him and a charm which saddened. He thought\nof how valiant a companion she would be on a long motor tour, exploring\nmountains, picnicking in a pine grove high above a valley. Her frailness\ntouched him; he was angry at Eddie Swanson for the incessant family\nbickering. All at once he identified Louetta with the fairy girl. He was\nstartled by the conviction that they had always had a romantic\nattraction for each other.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you\xe2\x80\x99re leading a simply terrible life, now you\xe2\x80\x99re a widower,\xe2\x80\x9d\nshe said.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou bet! I\xe2\x80\x99m a bad little fellow and proud of it. Some evening you slip\nEddie some dope in his coffee and sneak across the road and I\xe2\x80\x99ll show\nyou how to mix a cocktail,\xe2\x80\x9d he roared.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, now, I might do it! You never can tell!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, whenever you\xe2\x80\x99re ready, you just hang a towel out of the attic\nwindow and I\xe2\x80\x99ll jump for the gin!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nEvery one giggled at this naughtiness. In a pleased way Eddie Swanson\nstated that he would have a physician analyze his coffee daily. The\nothers were diverted to a discussion of the more agreeable recent\nmurders, but Babbitt drew Louetta back to personal things:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the prettiest dress I ever saw in my life.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you honestly like it?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cLike it? Why, say, I\xe2\x80\x99m going to have Kenneth Escott put a piece in the\npaper saying that the swellest dressed woman in the U. S. is MrS.E.\nLouetta Swanson.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, you stop teasing me!\xe2\x80\x9d But she beamed. \xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s dance a little.\nGeorge, you\xe2\x80\x99ve got to dance with me.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nEven as he protested, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, you know what a rotten dancer I am!\xe2\x80\x9d he was\nlumbering to his feet.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll teach you. I can teach anybody.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHer eyes were moist, her voice was jagged with excitement. He was\nconvinced that he had won her. He clasped her, conscious of her smooth\nwarmth, and solemnly he circled in a heavy version of the one-step. He\nbumped into only one or two people. \xe2\x80\x9cGosh, I\xe2\x80\x99m not doing so bad;\nhittin\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99em up like a regular stage dancer!\xe2\x80\x9d he gloated; and she\nanswered busily, \xe2\x80\x9cYes--yes-- I told you I could teach anybody--_don\xe2\x80\x99t\ntake such long steps_!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nFor a moment he was robbed of confidence; with fearful concentration he\nsought to keep time to the music. But he was enveloped again by her\nenchantment. \xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s got to like me; I\xe2\x80\x99ll make her!\xe2\x80\x9d he vowed. He tried\nto kiss the lock beside her ear. She mechanically moved her head to\navoid it, and mechanically she murmured, \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nFor a moment he hated her, but after the moment he was as urgent as\never. He danced with Mrs. Orville Jones, but he watched Louetta swooping\ndown the length of the room with her husband. \xe2\x80\x9cCareful! You\xe2\x80\x99re getting\nfoolish!\xe2\x80\x9d he cautioned himself, the while he hopped and bent his solid\nknees in dalliance with Mrs. Jones, and to that worthy lady rumbled,\n\xe2\x80\x9cGee, it\xe2\x80\x99s hot!\xe2\x80\x9d Without reason, he thought of Paul in that shadowy\nplace where men never dance. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m crazy to-night; better go home,\xe2\x80\x9d he\nworried, but he left Mrs. Jones and dashed to Louetta\xe2\x80\x99s lovely side,\ndemanding, \xe2\x80\x9cThe next is mine.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99m so hot; I\xe2\x80\x99m not going to dance this one.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen,\xe2\x80\x9d boldly, \xe2\x80\x9ccome out and sit on the porch and get all nice and\ncool.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nIn the tender darkness, with the clamor in the house behind them, he\nresolutely took her hand. She squeezed his once, then relaxed.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cLouetta! I think you\xe2\x80\x99re the nicest thing I know!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I think you\xe2\x80\x99re very nice.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you? You got to like me! I\xe2\x80\x99m so lonely!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you\xe2\x80\x99ll be all right when your wife comes home.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I\xe2\x80\x99m always lonely.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nShe clasped her hands under her chin, so that he dared not touch her. He\nsighed:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I feel punk and--\xe2\x80\x9d He was about to bring in the tragedy of Paul,\nbut that was too sacred even for the diplomacy of love. \xe2\x80\x9c--when I get\ntired out at the office and everything, I like to look across the street\nand think of you. Do you know I dreamed of you, one time!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas it a nice dream?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cLovely!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, well, they say dreams go by opposites! Now I must run in.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nShe was on her feet.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t go in yet! Please, Louetta!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I must. Have to look out for my guests.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet \xe2\x80\x99em look out for \xe2\x80\x99emselves!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t do that.\xe2\x80\x9d She carelessly tapped his shoulder and slipped\naway.\n\nBut after two minutes of shamed and childish longing to sneak home he\nwas snorting, \xe2\x80\x9cCertainly I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t trying to get chummy with her! Knew\nthere was nothing doing, all the time!\xe2\x80\x9d and he ambled in to dance with\nMrs. Orville Jones, and to avoid Louetta, virtuously and conspicuously.\n\n\n\n\nCHAPTER XXIV\n\n\nI\n\nHis visit to Paul was as unreal as his night of fog and questioning.\nUnseeing he went through prison corridors stinking of carbolic acid to a\nroom lined with pale yellow settees pierced in rosettes, like the\nshoe-store benches he had known as a boy. The guard led in Paul. Above\nhis uniform of linty gray, Paul\xe2\x80\x99s face was pale and without expression.\nHe moved timorously in response to the guard\xe2\x80\x99s commands; he meekly\npushed Babbitt\xe2\x80\x99s gifts of tobacco and magazines across the table to the\nguard for examination. He had nothing to say but \xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99m getting used\nto it\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m working in the tailor shop; the stuff hurts my fingers.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBabbitt knew that in this place of death Paul was already dead. And as\nhe pondered on the train home something in his own self seemed to have\ndied: a loyal and vigorous faith in the goodness of the world, a fear of\npublic disfavor, a pride in success. He was glad that his wife was away.\nHe admitted it without justifying it. He did not care.\n\n\nII\n\nHer card read \xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Daniel Judique.\xe2\x80\x9d Babbitt knew of her as the widow of\na wholesale paper-dealer. She must have been forty or forty-two but he\nthought her younger when he saw her in the office, that afternoon. She\nhad come to inquire about renting an apartment, and he took her away\nfrom the unskilled girl accountant. He was nervously attracted by her\nsmartness. She was a slender woman, in a black Swiss frock dotted with\nwhite, a cool-looking graceful frock. A broad black hat shaded her\nface. Her eyes were lustrous, her soft chin of an agreeable plumpness,\nand her cheeks an even rose. Babbitt wondered afterward if she was made\nup, but no man living knew less of such arts.\n\nShe sat revolving her violet parasol. Her voice was appealing without\nbeing coy. \xe2\x80\x9cI wonder if you can help me?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe delighted.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve looked everywhere and-- I want a little flat, just a bedroom, or\nperhaps two, and sitting-room and kitchenette and bath, but I want one\nthat really has some charm to it, not these dingy places or these new\nones with terrible gaudy chandeliers. And I can\xe2\x80\x99t pay so dreadfully\nmuch. My name\xe2\x80\x99s Tanis Judique.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think maybe I\xe2\x80\x99ve got just the thing for you. Would you like to chase\naround and look at it now?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. I have a couple of hours.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nIn the new Cavendish Apartments, Babbitt had a flat which he had been\nholding for Sidney Finkelstein, but at the thought of driving beside\nthis agreeable woman he threw over his friend Finkelstein, and with a\nnote of gallantry he proclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll let you see what I can do!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe dusted the seat of the car for her, and twice he risked death in\nshowing off his driving.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou do know how to handle a car!\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\n\nHe liked her voice. There was, he thought, music in it and a hint of\nculture, not a bouncing giggle like Louetta Swanson\xe2\x80\x99s.\n\nHe boasted, \xe2\x80\x9cYou know, there\xe2\x80\x99s a lot of these fellows that are so scared\nand drive so slow that they get in everybody\xe2\x80\x99s way. The safest driver is\na fellow that knows how to handle his machine and yet isn\xe2\x80\x99t scared to\nspeed up when it\xe2\x80\x99s necessary, don\xe2\x80\x99t you think so?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI bet you drive like a wiz.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no-- I mean--not really. Of course, we had a car-- I mean, before my\nhusband passed on--and I used to make believe drive it, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t\nthink any woman ever learns to drive like a man.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, now, there\xe2\x80\x99s some mighty good woman drivers.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, of course, these women that try to imitate men, and play golf and\neverything, and ruin their complexions and spoil their hands!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s so. I never did like these mannish females.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean--of course, I admire them, dreadfully, and I feel so weak and\nuseless beside them.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, rats now! She caught the glance, snuggled her hands together with a\nkittenish curving of slim white fingers which delighted him, and\nyearned:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do love to play-- I mean-- I like to drum on the piano, but I haven\xe2\x80\x99t\nhad any real training. Mr. Judique used to say I would\xe2\x80\x99ve been a good\npianist if I\xe2\x80\x99d had any training, but then, I guess he was just\nflattering me.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll bet he wasn\xe2\x80\x99t! I\xe2\x80\x99ll bet you\xe2\x80\x99ve got temperament.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh-- Do you like music, Mr. Babbitt?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou bet I do!
