Don\xe2\x80\x99t go making a fool of\nyourself again!\xe2\x80\x9d and with loftiness he discoursed:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish I could dance like some of these young fellows, but I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell\nyou: I feel it\xe2\x80\x99s a man\xe2\x80\x99s place to take a full, you might say, a creative\nshare in the world\xe2\x80\x99s work and mold conditions and have something to show\nfor his life, don\xe2\x80\x99t you think so?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I do!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd so I have to sacrifice some of the things I might like to tackle,\nthough I do, by golly, play about as good a game of golf as the next\nfellow!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99m sure you do.... Are you married?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cUh--yes.... And, uh, of course official duties-- I\xe2\x80\x99m the vice-president\nof the Boosters\xe2\x80\x99 Club, and I\xe2\x80\x99m running one of the committees of the\nState Association of Real Estate Boards, and that means a lot of work\nand responsibility--and practically no gratitude for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I know! Public men never do get proper credit.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThey looked at each other with a high degree of mutual respect, and at\nthe Cavendish Apartments he helped her out in a courtly manner, waved\nhis hand at the house as though he were presenting it to her, and\nponderously ordered the elevator boy to \xe2\x80\x9chustle and get the keys.\xe2\x80\x9d She\nstood close to him in the elevator, and he was stirred but cautious.\n\nIt was a pretty flat, of white woodwork and soft blue walls. Mrs.\nJudique gushed with pleasure as she agreed to take it, and as they\nwalked down the hall to the elevator she touched his sleeve, caroling,\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99m so glad I went to you! It\xe2\x80\x99s such a privilege to meet a man who\nreally Understands. Oh! The flats _some_ people have showed me!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe had a sharp instinctive belief that he could put his arm around her,\nbut he rebuked himself and with excessive politeness he saw her to the\ncar, drove her home. All the way back to his office he raged:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGlad I had some sense for once.... Curse it, I wish I\xe2\x80\x99d tried. She\xe2\x80\x99s a\ndarling! A corker! A reg\xe2\x80\x99lar charmer! Lovely eyes and darling lips and\nthat trim waist--never get sloppy, like some women.... No, no, no! She\xe2\x80\x99s\na real cultured lady. One of the brightest little women I\xe2\x80\x99ve met these\nmany moons. Understands about Public Topics and-- But, darn it, why\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t I try?... Tanis!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\nIII\n\nHe was harassed and puzzled by it, but he found that he was turning\ntoward youth, as youth. The girl who especially disturbed him--though he\nhad never spoken to her--was the last manicure girl on the right in the\nPompeian Barber Shop. She was small, swift, black-haired, smiling. She\nwas nineteen, perhaps, or twenty. She wore thin salmon-colored blouses\nwhich exhibited her shoulders and her black-ribboned camisoles.\n\nHe went to the Pompeian for his fortnightly hair-trim. As always, he\nfelt disloyal at deserting his neighbor, the Reeves Building Barber\nShop. Then, for the first time, he overthrew his sense of guilt.\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoggone it, I don\xe2\x80\x99t have to go here if I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to! I don\xe2\x80\x99t own the\nReeves Building! These barbers got nothing on me! I\xe2\x80\x99ll doggone well get\nmy hair cut where I doggone well want to! Don\xe2\x80\x99t want to hear anything\nmore about it! I\xe2\x80\x99m through standing by people--unless I want to. It\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t get you anywhere. I\xe2\x80\x99m through!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThe Pompeian Barber Shop was in the basement of the Hotel Thornleigh,\nlargest and most dynamically modern hotel in Zenith. Curving marble\nsteps with a rail of polished brass led from the hotel-lobby down to the\nbarber shop. The interior was of black and white and crimson tiles,\nwith a sensational ceiling of burnished gold, and a fountain in which a\nmassive nymph forever emptied a scarlet cornucopia. Forty barbers and\nnine manicure girls worked desperately, and at the door six colored\nporters lurked to greet the customers, to care reverently for their hats\nand collars, to lead them to a place of waiting where, on a carpet like\na tropic isle in the stretch of white stone floor, were a dozen leather\nchairs and a table heaped with magazines.\n\nBabbitt\xe2\x80\x99s porter was an obsequious gray-haired negro who did him an\nhonor highly esteemed in the land of Zenith--greeted him by name. Yet\nBabbitt was unhappy. His bright particular manicure girl was engaged.\nShe was doing the nails of an overdressed man and giggling with him.\nBabbitt hated him. He thought of waiting, but to stop the powerful\nsystem of the Pompeian was inconceivable, and he was instantly wafted\ninto a chair.\n\nAbout him was luxury, rich and delicate. One votary was having a\nviolet-ray facial treatment, the next an oil shampoo. Boys wheeled about\nmiraculous electrical massage-machines. The barbers snatched steaming\ntowels from a machine like a howitzer of polished nickel and\ndisdainfully flung them away after a second\xe2\x80\x99s use. On the vast marble\nshelf facing the chairs were hundreds of tonics, amber and ruby and\nemerald. It was flattering to Babbitt to have two personal slaves at\nonce--the barber and the bootblack. He would have been completely happy\nif he could also have had the manicure girl. The barber snipped at his\nhair and asked his opinion of the Havre de Grace races, the baseball\nseason, and Mayor Prout. The young negro bootblack hummed \xe2\x80\x9cThe Camp\nMeeting Blues\xe2\x80\x9d and polished in rhythm to his tune, drawing the shiny\nshoe-rag so taut at each stroke that it snapped like a banjo string. The\nbarber was an excellent salesman. He made Babbitt feel rich and\nimportant by his manner of inquiring, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is your favorite tonic, sir?\nHave you time to-day, sir, for a facial massage? Your scalp is a little\ntight; shall I give you a scalp massage?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBabbitt\xe2\x80\x99s best thrill was in the shampoo. The barber made his hair\ncreamy with thick soap, then (as Babbitt bent over the bowl, muffled in\ntowels) drenched it with hot water which prickled along his scalp, and\nat last ran the water ice-cold. At the shock, the sudden burning cold on\nhis skull, Babbitt\xe2\x80\x99s heart thumped, his chest heaved, and his spine was\nan electric wire. It was a sensation which broke the monotony of life.\nHe looked grandly about the shop as he sat up. The barber obsequiously\nrubbed his wet hair and bound it in a towel as in a turban, so that\nBabbitt resembled a plump pink calif on an ingenious and adjustable\nthrone. The barber begged (in the manner of one who was a good fellow\nyet was overwhelmed by the splendors of the calif), \xe2\x80\x9cHow about a little\nEldorado Oil Rub, sir? Very beneficial to the scalp, sir. Didn\xe2\x80\x99t I give\nyou one the last time?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe hadn\xe2\x80\x99t, but Babbitt agreed, \xe2\x80\x9cWell, all right.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nWith quaking eagerness he saw that his manicure girl was free.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know, I guess I\xe2\x80\x99ll have a manicure after all,\xe2\x80\x9d he droned, and\nexcitedly watched her coming, dark-haired, smiling, tender, little. The\nmanicuring would have to be finished at her table, and he would be able\nto talk to her without the barber listening. He waited contentedly, not\ntrying to peep at her, while she filed his nails and the barber shaved\nhim and smeared on his burning cheeks all the interesting mixtures which\nthe pleasant minds of barbers have devised through the revolving ages.\nWhen the barber was done and he sat opposite the girl at her table, he\nadmired the marble slab of it, admired the sunken set bowl with its tiny\nsilver taps, and admired himself for being able to frequent so costly a\nplace. When she withdrew his wet hand from the bowl, it was so sensitive\nfrom the warm soapy water that he was abnormally aware of the clasp of\nher firm little paw. He delighted in the pinkness and glossiness of her\nnails. Her hands seemed to him more adorable than Mrs. Judique\xe2\x80\x99s thin\nfingers, and more elegant. He had a certain ecstasy in the pain when she\ngnawed at the cuticle of his nails with a sharp knife. He struggled not\nto look at the outline of her young bosom and her shoulders, the more\napparent under a film of pink chiffon. He was conscious of her as an\nexquisite thing, and when he tried to impress his personality on her he\nspoke as awkwardly as a country boy at his first party:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, kinda hot to be working to-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, it is hot. You cut your own nails, last time, didn\xe2\x80\x99t you!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYe-es, guess I must\xe2\x80\x99ve.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou always ought to go to a manicure.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, maybe that\xe2\x80\x99s so. I--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s nothing looks so nice as nails that are looked after good. I\nalways think that\xe2\x80\x99s the best way to spot a real gent. There was an auto\nsalesman in here yesterday that claimed you could always tell a fellow\xe2\x80\x99s\nclass by the car he drove, but I says to him, \xe2\x80\x98Don\xe2\x80\x99t be silly,\xe2\x80\x99 I says;\n\xe2\x80\x98the wisenheimers grab a look at a fellow\xe2\x80\x99s nails when they want to tell\nif he\xe2\x80\x99s a tinhorn or a real gent!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, maybe there\xe2\x80\x99s something to that. Course, that is--with a pretty\nkiddy like you, a man can\xe2\x80\x99t help coming to get his mitts done.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYeh, I may be a kid, but I\xe2\x80\x99m a wise bird, and I know nice folks when I\nsee um-- I can read character at a glance--and I\xe2\x80\x99d never talk so frank\nwith a fellow if I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t see he was a nice fellow.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nShe smiled. Her eyes seemed to him as gentle as April pools. With great\nseriousness he informed himself that \xe2\x80\x9cthere were some roughnecks who\nwould think that just because a girl was a manicure girl and maybe not\nawful well educated, she was no good, but as for him, he was a democrat,\nand understood people,\xe2\x80\x9d and he stood by the assertion that this was a\nfine girl, a good girl--but not too uncomfortably good. He inquired in a\nvoice quick with sympathy:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you have a lot of fellows who try to get fresh with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay, gee, do I! Say, listen, there\xe2\x80\x99s some of these cigar-store sports\nthat think because a girl\xe2\x80\x99s working in a barber shop, they can get away\nwith anything. The things they saaaaaay! But, believe me, I know how to\nhop those birds! I just give um the north and south and ask um, \xe2\x80\x98Say,\nwho do you think you\xe2\x80\x99re talking to?\xe2\x80\x99 and they fade away like love\xe2\x80\x99s\nyoung nightmare and oh, don\xe2\x80\x99t you want a box of nail-paste? It will keep\nthe nails as shiny as when first manicured, harmless to apply and lasts\nfor days.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSure, I\xe2\x80\x99ll try some. Say-- Say, it\xe2\x80\x99s funny; I\xe2\x80\x99ve been coming here ever\nsince the shop opened and--\xe2\x80\x9d With arch surprise. \xe2\x80\x9c-- I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe I\nknow your name!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you? My, that\xe2\x80\x99s funny! I don\xe2\x80\x99t know yours!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow you quit kidding me! What\xe2\x80\x99s the nice little name?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it ain\xe2\x80\x99t so darn nice. I guess it\xe2\x80\x99s kind of kike. But my folks\nain\xe2\x80\x99t kikes. My papa\xe2\x80\x99s papa was a nobleman in Poland, and there was a\ngentleman in here one day, he was kind of a count or something--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cKind of a no-account, I guess you mean!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho\xe2\x80\x99s telling this, smarty? And he said he knew my papa\xe2\x80\x99s papa\xe2\x80\x99s folks\nin Poland and they had a dandy big house. Right on a lake!\xe2\x80\x9d Doubtfully,\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaybe you don\xe2\x80\x99t believe it?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSure. No. Really. Sure I do. Why not? Don\xe2\x80\x99t think I\xe2\x80\x99m kidding you,\nhoney, but every time I\xe2\x80\x99ve noticed you I\xe2\x80\x99ve said to myself, \xe2\x80\x98That kid\nhas Blue Blood in her veins!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you, honest?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHonest I did. Well, well, come on--now we\xe2\x80\x99re friends--what\xe2\x80\x99s the\ndarling little name?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIda Putiak. It ain\xe2\x80\x99t so much-a-much of a name. I always say to Ma, I\nsay, \xe2\x80\x98Ma, why didn\xe2\x80\x99t you name me Dolores, or something with some class\nto it?\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, now, I think it\xe2\x80\x99s a scrumptious name. Ida!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI bet I know _your_ name!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, now, not necessarily. Of course-- Oh, it isn\xe2\x80\x99t so specially well\nknown.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAren\xe2\x80\x99t you Mr. Sondheim that travels for the Krackajack Kitchen Kutlery\nKo.?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not! I\xe2\x80\x99m Mr. Babbitt, the real-estate broker!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, excuse me! Oh, of course. You mean here in Zenith.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYep.\xe2\x80\x9d With the briskness of one whose feelings have been hurt.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, sure. I\xe2\x80\x99ve read your ads. They\xe2\x80\x99re swell.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cUm, well-- You might have read about my speeches.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cCourse I have! I don\xe2\x80\x99t get much time to read but-- I guess you think I\xe2\x80\x99m\nan awfully silly little nit!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you\xe2\x80\x99re a little darling!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell-- There\xe2\x80\x99s one nice thing about this job. It gives a girl a chance\nto meet some awfully nice gentlemen and improve her mind with\nconversation, and you get so you can read a guy\xe2\x80\x99s character at the first\nglance.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here, Ida; please don\xe2\x80\x99t think I\xe2\x80\x99m getting fresh--\xe2\x80\x9d He was hotly\nreflecting that it would be humiliating to be rejected by this child,\nand dangerous to be accepted. If he took her to dinner, if he were seen\nby censorious friends-- But he went on ardently: \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t think I\xe2\x80\x99m getting\nfresh if I suggest it would be nice for us to go out and have a little\ndinner together some evening.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know as I ought to but-- My gentleman-friend\xe2\x80\x99s always wanting to\ntake me out. But maybe I could to-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\nIV\n\nThere was no reason, he assured himself, why he shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have a quiet\ndinner with a poor girl who would benefit by association with an\neducated and mature person like himself. But, lest some one see them\nand not understand, he would take her to Biddlemeier\xe2\x80\x99s Inn, on the\noutskirts of the city. They would have a pleasant drive, this hot lonely\nevening, and he might hold her hand--no, he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t even do that. Ida\nwas complaisant; her bare shoulders showed it only too clearly; but he\xe2\x80\x99d\nbe hanged if he\xe2\x80\x99d make love to her merely because she expected it.\n\nThen his car broke down; something had happened to the ignition. And he\n_had_ to have the car this evening! Furiously he tested the spark-plugs,\nstared at the commutator. His angriest glower did not seem to stir the\nsulky car, and in disgrace it was hauled off to a garage. With a renewed\nthrill he thought of a taxicab. There was something at once wealthy and\ninterestingly wicked about a taxicab.\n\nBut when he met her, on a corner two blocks from the Hotel Thornleigh,\nshe said, \xe2\x80\x9cA taxi? Why, I thought you owned a car!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do. Of course I do! But it\xe2\x80\x99s out of commission to-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d she remarked, as one who had heard that tale before.\n\nAll the way out to Biddlemeier\xe2\x80\x99s Inn he tried to talk as an old friend,\nbut he could not pierce the wall of her words. With interminable\nindignation she narrated her retorts to \xe2\x80\x9cthat fresh head-barber\xe2\x80\x9d and the\ndrastic things she would do to him if he persisted in saying that she\nwas \xe2\x80\x9cbetter at gassing than at hoof-paring.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAt Biddlemeier\xe2\x80\x99s Inn they were unable to get anything to drink. The\nhead-waiter refused to understand who George F. Babbitt was. They sat\nsteaming before a vast mixed grill, and made conversation about\nbaseball. When he tried to hold Ida\xe2\x80\x99s hand she said with bright\nfriendliness, \xe2\x80\x9cCareful! That fresh waiter is rubbering.\xe2\x80\x9d But they came\nout into a treacherous summer night, the air lazy and a little moon\nabove transfigured maples.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s drive some other place, where we can get a drink and dance!\xe2\x80\x9d he\ndemanded.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSure, some other night. But I promised Ma I\xe2\x80\x99d be home early to-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cRats! It\xe2\x80\x99s too nice to go home.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d just love to, but Ma would give me fits.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe was trembling. She was everything that was young and exquisite. He\nput his arm about her. She snuggled against his shoulder, unafraid, and\nhe was triumphant. Then she ran down the steps of the Inn, singing,\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome on, Georgie, we\xe2\x80\x99ll have a nice drive and get cool.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nIt was a night of lovers. All along the highway into Zenith, under the\nlow and gentle moon, motors were parked and dim figures were clasped in\nrevery. He held out hungry hands to Ida, and when she patted them he was\ngrateful. There was no sense of struggle and transition; he kissed her\nand simply she responded to his kiss, they two behind the stolid back of\nthe chauffeur.\n\nHer hat fell off, and she broke from his embrace to reach for it.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, let it be!\xe2\x80\x9d he implored.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuh? My hat? Not a chance!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe waited till she had pinned it on, then his arm sank about her. She\ndrew away from it, and said with maternal soothing, \xe2\x80\x9cNow, don\xe2\x80\x99t be a\nsilly boy! Mustn\xe2\x80\x99t make Ittle Mama scold! Just sit back, dearie, and see\nwhat a swell night it is. If you\xe2\x80\x99re a good boy, maybe I\xe2\x80\x99ll kiss you when\nwe say nighty-night. Now give me a cigarette.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe was solicitous about lighting her cigarette and inquiring as to her\ncomfort. Then he sat as far from her as possible. He was cold with\nfailure. No one could have told Babbitt that he was a fool with more\nvigor, precision, and intelligence than he himself displayed. He\nreflected that from the standpoint of the Rev. Dr. John Jennison Drew he\nwas a wicked man, and from the standpoint of Miss Ida Putiak, an old\nbore who had to be endured as the penalty attached to eating a large\ndinner.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDearie, you aren\xe2\x80\x99t going to go and get peevish, are you?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nShe spoke pertly. He wanted to spank her. He brooded, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t have to\ntake anything off this gutter-pup! Darn immigrant! Well, let\xe2\x80\x99s get it\nover as quick as we can, and sneak home and kick ourselves for the rest\nof the night.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe snorted, \xe2\x80\x9cHuh? Me peevish? Why, you baby, why should I be peevish?\nNow, listen, Ida; listen to Uncle George. I want to put you wise about\nthis scrapping with your head-barber all the time. I\xe2\x80\x99ve had a lot of\nexperience with employees, and let me tell you it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t pay to\nantagonize--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAt the drab wooden house in which she lived he said good-night briefly\nand amiably, but as the taxicab drove off he was praying \xe2\x80\x9cOh, my God!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\n\nCHAPTER XXV\n\n\nI\n\nHe awoke to stretch cheerfully as he listened to the sparrows, then to\nremember that everything was wrong; that he was determined to go astray,\nand not in the least enjoying the process. Why, he wondered, should he\nbe in rebellion? What was it all about? \xe2\x80\x9cWhy not be sensible; stop all\nthis idiotic running around, and enjoy himself with his family, his\nbusiness, the fellows at the club?\xe2\x80\x9d What was he getting out of\nrebellion? Misery and shame--the shame of being treated as an offensive\nsmall boy by a ragamuffin like Ida Putiak! And yet-- Always he came back\nto \xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet.\xe2\x80\x9d Whatever the misery, he could not regain contentment with\na world which, once doubted, became absurd.\n\nOnly, he assured himself, he was \xe2\x80\x9cthrough with this chasing after\ngirls.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBy noontime he was not so sure even of that. If in Miss McGoun, Louetta\nSwanson, and Ida he had failed to find the lady kind and lovely, it did\nnot prove that she did not exist. He was hunted by the ancient thought\nthat somewhere must exist the not impossible she who would understand\nhim, value him, and make him happy.\n\n\nII\n\nMrs. Babbitt returned in August.\n\nOn her previous absences he had missed her reassuring buzz and of her\narrival he had made a f\xc3\xaate. Now, though he dared not hurt her by letting\na hint of it appear in his letters, he was sorry that she was coming\nbefore he had found himself, and he was embarrassed by the need of\nmeeting her and looking joyful.\n\nHe loitered down to the station; he studied the summer-resort posters,\nlest he have to speak to acquaintances and expose his uneasiness. But he\nwas well trained. When the train clanked in he was out on the cement\nplatform, peering into the chair-cars, and as he saw her in the line of\npassengers moving toward the vestibule he waved his hat. At the door he\nembraced her, and announced, \xe2\x80\x9cWell, well, well, well, by golly, you look\nfine, you look fine.\xe2\x80\x9d Then he was aware of Tinka. Here was something,\nthis child with her absurd little nose and lively eyes, that loved him,\nbelieved him great, and as he clasped her, lifted and held her till she\nsquealed, he was for the moment come back to his old steady self.\n\nTinka sat beside him in the car, with one hand on the steering-wheel,\npretending to help him drive, and he shouted back to his wife, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll bet\nthe kid will be the best chuffer in the family! She holds the wheel like\nan old professional!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAll the while he was dreading the moment when he would be alone with his\nwife and she would patiently expect him to be ardent.\n\n\nIII\n\nThere was about the house an unofficial theory that he was to take his\nvacation alone, to spend a week or ten days in Catawba, but he was\nnagged by the memory that a year ago he had been with Paul in Maine. He\nsaw himself returning; finding peace there, and the presence of Paul, in\na life primitive and heroic. Like a shock came the thought that he\nactually could go. Only, he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t, really; he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t leave his\nbusiness, and \xe2\x80\x9cMyra would think it sort of funny, his going way off\nthere alone. Course he\xe2\x80\x99d decided to do whatever he darned pleased, from\nnow on, but still--to go way off to Maine!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe went, after lengthy meditations.\n\nWith his wife, since it was inconceivable to explain that he was going\nto seek Paul\xe2\x80\x99s spirit in the wilderness, he frugally employed the lie\nprepared over a year ago and scarcely used at all. He said that he had\nto see a man in New York on business. He could not have explained even\nto himself why he drew from the bank several hundred dollars more than\nhe needed, nor why he kissed Tinka so tenderly, and cried, \xe2\x80\x9cGod bless\nyou, baby!\xe2\x80\x9d From the train he waved to her till she was but a scarlet\nspot beside the brown bulkier presence of Mrs. Babbitt, at the end of a\nsteel and cement aisle ending in vast barred gates. With melancholy he\nlooked back at the last suburb of Zenith.\n\nAll the way north he pictured the Maine guides: simple and strong and\ndaring, jolly as they played stud-poker in their unceiled shack, wise in\nwoodcraft as they tramped the forest and shot the rapids. He\nparticularly remembered Joe Paradise, half Yankee, half Indian. If he\ncould but take up a backwoods claim with a man like Joe, work hard with\nhis hands, be free and noisy in a flannel shirt, and never come back to\nthis dull decency!\n\nOr, like a trapper in a Northern Canada movie, plunge through the\nforest, make camp in the Rockies, a grim and wordless caveman! Why not?\nHe _could_ do it! There\xe2\x80\x99d be enough money at home for the family to live\non till Verona was married and Ted self-supporting. Old Henry T. would\nlook out for them. Honestly! Why _not_? Really _live_--\n\nHe longed for it, admitted that he longed for it, then almost believed\nthat he was going to do it. Whenever common sense snorted, \xe2\x80\x9cNonsense!\nFolks don\xe2\x80\x99t run away from decent families and partners; just simply\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t do it, that\xe2\x80\x99s all!\xe2\x80\x9d then Babbitt answered pleadingly, \xe2\x80\x9cWell, it\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t take any more nerve than for Paul to go to jail and-- Lord, how\nI\xe2\x80\x99d like to do it! Moccasins--six-gun--frontier town--gamblers--sleep\nunder the stars--be a regular man, with he-men like Joe\nParadise--gosh!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nSo he came to Maine, again stood on the wharf before the camp-hotel,\nagain spat heroically into the delicate and shivering water, while the\npines rustled, the mountains glowed, and a trout leaped and fell in a\nsliding circle. He hurried to the guides\xe2\x80\x99 shack as to his real home, his\nreal friends, long missed. They would be glad to see him. They would\nstand up and shout, \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, here\xe2\x80\x99s Mr. Babbitt! He ain\xe2\x80\x99t one of these\nordinary sports! He\xe2\x80\x99s a real guy!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nIn their boarded and rather littered cabin the guides sat about the\ngreasy table playing stud-poker with greasy cards: half a dozen wrinkled\nmen in old trousers and easy old felt hats. They glanced up and nodded.\nJoe Paradise, the swart aging man with the big mustache, grunted, \xe2\x80\x9cHow\ndo. Back again?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nSilence, except for the clatter of chips.\n\nBabbitt stood beside them, very lonely. He hinted, after a period of\nhighly concentrated playing, \xe2\x80\x9cGuess I might take a hand, Joe.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSure. Sit in. How many chips you want? Let\xe2\x80\x99s see; you were here with\nyour wife, last year, wa\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d said Joe Paradise.\n\nThat was all of Babbitt\xe2\x80\x99s welcome to the old home.\n\nHe played for half an hour before he spoke again. His head was reeking\nwith the smoke of pipes and cheap cigars, and he was weary of pairs and\nfour-flushes, resentful of the way in which they ignored him. He flung\nat Joe:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWorking now?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNope.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cLike to guide me for a few days?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, jus\xe2\x80\x99 soon. I ain\xe2\x80\x99t engaged till next week.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nOnly thus did Joe recognize the friendship Babbitt was offering him.\nBabbitt paid up his losses and left the shack rather childishly. Joe\nraised his head from the coils of smoke like a seal rising from surf,\ngrunted, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll come \xe2\x80\x99round t\xe2\x80\x99morrow,\xe2\x80\x9d and dived down to his three aces.\n\nNeither in his voiceless cabin, fragrant with planks of new-cut pine,\nnor along the lake, nor in the sunset clouds which presently eddied\nbehind the lavender-misted mountains, could Babbitt find the spirit of\nPaul as a reassuring presence. He was so lonely that after supper he\nstopped to talk with an ancient old lady, a gasping and steadily\ndiscoursing old lady, by the stove in the hotel-office. He told her of\nTed\xe2\x80\x99s presumable future triumphs in the State University and of Tinka\xe2\x80\x99s\nremarkable vocabulary till he was homesick for the home he had left\nforever.\n\nThrough the darkness, through that Northern pine-walled silence, he\nblundered down to the lake-front and found a canoe. There were no\npaddles in it but with a board, sitting awkwardly amidships and poking\nat the water rather than paddling, he made his way far out on the lake.\nThe lights of the hotel and the cottages became yellow dots, a cluster\nof glow-worms at the base of Sachem Mountain. Larger and ever more\nimperturbable was the mountain in the star-filtered darkness, and the\nlake a limitless pavement of black marble. He was dwarfed and dumb and a\nlittle awed, but that insignificance freed him from the pomposities of\nbeing Mr. George F. Babbitt of Zenith; saddened and freed his heart. Now\nhe was conscious of the presence of Paul, fancied him (rescued from\nprison, from Zilla and the brisk exactitudes of the tar-roofing\nbusiness) playing his violin at the end of the canoe. He vowed, \xe2\x80\x9cI will\ngo on! I\xe2\x80\x99ll never go back! Now that Paul\xe2\x80\x99s out of it, I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to\nsee any of those damn people again! I was a fool to get sore because Joe\nParadise didn\xe2\x80\x99t jump up and hug me. He\xe2\x80\x99s one of these woodsmen; too wise\nto go yelping and talking your arm off like a cityman. But get him back\nin the mountains, out on the trail--! That\xe2\x80\x99s real living!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\nIV\n\nJoe reported at Babbitt\xe2\x80\x99s cabin at nine the next morning. Babbitt\ngreeted him as a fellow caveman:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Joe, how d\xe2\x80\x99you feel about hitting the trail, and getting away\nfrom these darn soft summerites and these women and all?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, Mr. Babbitt.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you say we go over to Box Car Pond--they tell me the shack\nthere isn\xe2\x80\x99t being used--and camp out?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, all right, Mr. Babbitt, but it\xe2\x80\x99s nearer to Skowtuit Pond, and you\ncan get just about as good fishing there.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I want to get into the real wilds.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, all right.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll put the old packs on our backs and get into the woods and really\nhike.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think maybe it would be easier to go by water, through Lake Chogue.\nWe can go all the way by motor boat--flat-bottom boat with an Evinrude.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir! Bust up the quiet with a chugging motor? Not on your life! You\njust throw a pair of socks in the old pack, and tell \xe2\x80\x99em what you want\nfor eats. I\xe2\x80\x99ll be ready soon\xe2\x80\x99s you are.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMost of the sports go by boat, Mr. Babbitt. It\xe2\x80\x99s a long walk.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here, Joe: are you objecting to walking?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no, I guess I can do it. But I haven\xe2\x80\x99t tramped that far for sixteen\nyears. Most of the sports go by boat. But I can do it if you say so-- I\nguess.\xe2\x80\x9d Joe walked away in sadness.\n\nBabbitt had recovered from his touchy wrath before Joe returned. He\npictured him as warming up and telling the most entertaining stories.\nBut Joe had not yet warmed up when they took the trail. He persistently\nkept behind Babbitt, and however much his shoulders ached from the\npack, however sorely he panted, Babbitt could hear his guide panting\nequally. But the trail was satisfying: a path brown with pine-needles\nand rough with roots, among the balsams, the ferns, the sudden groves of\nwhite birch. He became credulous again, and rejoiced in sweating. When\nhe stopped to rest he chuckled, \xe2\x80\x9cGuess we\xe2\x80\x99re hitting it up pretty good\nfor a couple o\xe2\x80\x99 old birds, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cUh-huh,\xe2\x80\x9d admitted Joe.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is a mighty pretty place. Look, you can see the lake down through\nthe trees. I tell you, Joe, you don\xe2\x80\x99t appreciate how lucky you are to\nlive in woods like this, instead of a city with trolleys grinding and\ntypewriters clacking and people bothering the life out of you all the\ntime! I wish I knew the woods like you do. Say, what\xe2\x80\x99s the name of that\nlittle red flower?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nRubbing his back, Joe regarded the flower resentfully. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, some folks\ncall it one thing and some calls it another I always just call it Pink\nFlower.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBabbitt blessedly ceased thinking as tramping turned into blind\nplodding. He was submerged in weariness. His plump legs seemed to go on\nby themselves, without guidance, and he mechanically wiped away the\nsweat which stung his eyes. He was too tired to be consciously glad as,\nafter a sun-scourged mile of corduroy tote-road through a swamp where\nflies hovered over a hot waste of brush, they reached the cool shore of\nBox Car Pond. When he lifted the pack from his back he staggered from\nthe change in balance, and for a moment could not stand erect. He lay\nbeneath an ample-bosomed maple tree near the guest-shack, and joyously\nfelt sleep running through his veins.\n\nHe awoke toward dusk, to find Joe efficiently cooking bacon and eggs and\nflapjacks for supper, and his admiration of the woodsman returned. He\nsat on a stump and felt virile.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cJoe, what would you do if you had a lot of money? Would you stick to\nguiding, or would you take a claim \xe2\x80\x99way back in the woods and be\nindependent of people?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nFor the first time Joe brightened. He chewed his cud a second, and\nbubbled, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve often thought of that! If I had the money, I\xe2\x80\x99d go down to\nTinker\xe2\x80\x99s Falls and open a swell shoe store.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAfter supper Joe proposed a game of stud-poker but Babbitt refused with\nbrevity, and Joe contentedly went to bed at eight. Babbitt sat on the\nstump, facing the dark pond, slapping mosquitos. Save the snoring guide,\nthere was no other human being within ten miles. He was lonelier than he\nhad ever been in his life. Then he was in Zenith.\n\nHe was worrying as to whether Miss McGoun wasn\xe2\x80\x99t paying too much for\ncarbon paper. He was at once resenting and missing the persistent\nteasing at the Roughnecks\xe2\x80\x99 Table. He was wondering what Zilla Riesling\nwas doing now. He was wondering whether, after the summer\xe2\x80\x99s maturity of\nbeing a garageman, Ted would \xe2\x80\x9cget busy\xe2\x80\x9d in the university. He was\nthinking of his wife. \xe2\x80\x9cIf she would only--if she wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be so darn\nsatisfied with just settling down-- No! I won\xe2\x80\x99t! I won\xe2\x80\x99t go back! I\xe2\x80\x99ll be\nfifty in three years. Sixty in thirteen years. I\xe2\x80\x99m going to have some\nfun before it\xe2\x80\x99s too late. I don\xe2\x80\x99t care! I will!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe thought of Ida Putiak, of Louetta Swanson, of that nice widow--what\nwas her name?--Tanis Judique?--the one for whom he\xe2\x80\x99d found the flat. He\nwas enmeshed in imaginary conversations. Then:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGee, I can\xe2\x80\x99t seem to get away from thinking about folks!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThus it came to him merely to run away was folly, because he could never\nrun away from himself.\n\nThat moment he started for Zenith. In his journey there was no\nappearance of flight, but he was fleeing, and four days afterward he was\non the Zenith train. He knew that he was slinking back not because it\nwas what he longed to do but because it was all he could do. He scanned\nagain his discovery that he could never run away from Zenith and family\nand office, because in his own brain he bore the office and the family\nand every street and disquiet and illusion of Zenith.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I\xe2\x80\x99m going to--oh, I\xe2\x80\x99m going to start something!\xe2\x80\x9d he vowed, and he\ntried to make it valiant.\n\n\n\n\nCHAPTER XXVI\n\n\nI\n\nAs he walked through the train, looking for familiar faces, he saw only\none person whom he knew, and that was Seneca Doane, the lawyer who,\nafter the blessings of being in Babbitt\xe2\x80\x99s own class at college and of\nbecoming a corporation-counsel, had turned crank, had headed\nfarmer-labor tickets and fraternized with admitted socialists. Though he\nwas in rebellion, naturally Babbitt did not care to be seen talking with\nsuch a fanatic, but in all the Pullmans he could find no other\nacquaintance, and reluctantly he halted. Seneca Doane was a slight,\nthin-haired man, rather like Chum Frink except that he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t Frink\xe2\x80\x99s\ngrin. He was reading a book called \xe2\x80\x9cThe Way of All Flesh.\xe2\x80\x9d It looked\nreligious to Babbitt, and he wondered if Doane could possibly have been\nconverted and turned decent and patriotic.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, hello, Doane,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\n\nDoane looked up. His voice was curiously kind. \xe2\x80\x9cOh! How do, Babbitt.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBeen away, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I\xe2\x80\x99ve been in Washington.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWashington, eh? How\xe2\x80\x99s the old Government making out?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s-- Won\xe2\x80\x99t you sit down?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThanks. Don\xe2\x80\x99t care if I do. Well, well! Been quite a while since I\xe2\x80\x99ve\nhad a good chance to talk to you, Doane. I was, uh-- Sorry you didn\xe2\x80\x99t\nturn up at the last class-dinner.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh--thanks.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow\xe2\x80\x99s the unions coming? Going to run for mayor again?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nDoane seemed restless. He was fingering the pages of his book. He said\n\xe2\x80\x9cI might\xe2\x80\x9d as though it didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean anything in particular, and he\nsmiled.\n\nBabbitt liked that smile, and hunted for conversation: \xe2\x80\x9cSaw a bang-up\ncabaret in New York: the \xe2\x80\x98Good-Morning Cutie\xe2\x80\x99 bunch at the Hotel\nMinton.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, they\xe2\x80\x99re pretty girls. I danced there one evening.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh. Like dancing?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNaturally. I like dancing and pretty women and good food better than\nanything else in the world. Most men do.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut gosh, Doane, I thought you fellows wanted to take all the good eats\nand everything away from us.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. Not at all. What I\xe2\x80\x99d like to see is the meetings of the Garment\nWorkers held at the Ritz, with a dance afterward. Isn\xe2\x80\x99t that\nreasonable?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYuh, might be good idea, all right. Well-- Shame I haven\xe2\x80\x99t seen more of\nyou, recent years. Oh, say, hope you haven\xe2\x80\x99t held it against me, my\nbucking you as mayor, going on the stump for Prout. You see, I\xe2\x80\x99m an\norganization Republican, and I kind of felt--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s no reason why you shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t fight me. I have no doubt you\xe2\x80\x99re\ngood for the Organization. I remember--in college you were an unusually\nliberal, sensitive chap. I can still recall your saying to me that you\nwere going to be a lawyer, and take the cases of the poor for nothing,\nand fight the rich. And I remember I said I was going to be one of the\nrich myself, and buy paintings and live at Newport. I\xe2\x80\x99m sure you\ninspired us all.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell.... Well.... I\xe2\x80\x99ve always aimed to be liberal.\xe2\x80\x9d Babbitt was\nenormously shy and proud and self-conscious; he tried to look like the\nboy he had been a quarter-century ago, and he shone upon his old friend\nSeneca Doane as he rumbled, \xe2\x80\x9cTrouble with a lot of these fellows, even\nthe live wires and some of \xe2\x80\x99em that think they\xe2\x80\x99re forward-looking, is\nthey aren\xe2\x80\x99t broad-minded and liberal. Now, I always believe in giving\nthe other fellow a chance, and listening to his ideas.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s fine.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell you how I figure it: A little opposition is good for all of us, so\na fellow, especially if he\xe2\x80\x99s a business man and engaged in doing the\nwork of the world, ought to be liberal.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI always say a fellow ought to have Vision and Ideals. I guess some of\nthe fellows in my business think I\xe2\x80\x99m pretty visionary, but I just let\n\xe2\x80\x99em think what they want to and go right on--same as you do.... By\ngolly, this is nice to have a chance to sit and visit and kind of, you\nmight say, brush up on our ideals.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut of course we visionaries do rather get beaten. Doesn\xe2\x80\x99t it bother\nyou?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a bit! Nobody can dictate to me what I think!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re the man I want to help me. I want you to talk to some of the\nbusiness men and try to make them a little more liberal in their\nattitude toward poor Beecher Ingram.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIngram? But, why, he\xe2\x80\x99s this nut preacher that got kicked out of the\nCongregationalist Church, isn\xe2\x80\x99t he, and preaches free love and\nsedition?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThis, Doane explained, was indeed the general conception of Beecher\nIngram, but he himself saw Beecher Ingram as a priest of the brotherhood\nof man, of which Babbitt was notoriously an upholder. So would Babbitt\nkeep his acquaintances from hounding Ingram and his forlorn little\nchurch?\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou bet! I\xe2\x80\x99ll call down any of the boys I hear getting funny about\nIngram,\xe2\x80\x9d Babbitt said affectionately to his dear friend Doane.\n\nDoane warmed up and became reminiscent. He spoke of student days in\nGermany, of lobbying for single tax in Washington, of international\nlabor conferences. He mentioned his friends, Lord Wycombe, Colonel\nWedgwood, Professor Piccoli. Babbitt had always supposed that Doane\nassociated only with the I. W. W., but now he nodded gravely, as one who\nknew Lord Wycombes by the score, and he got in two references to Sir\nGerald Doak. He felt daring and idealistic and cosmopolitan.\n\nSuddenly, in his new spiritual grandeur, he was sorry for Zilla\nRiesling, and understood her as these ordinary fellows at the Boosters\xe2\x80\x99\nClub never could.\n\n\nII\n\nFive hours after he had arrived in Zenith and told his wife how hot it\nwas in New York, he went to call on Zilla. He was buzzing with ideas and\nforgiveness. He\xe2\x80\x99d get Paul released; he\xe2\x80\x99d do things, vague but highly\nbenevolent things, for Zilla; he\xe2\x80\x99d be as generous as his friend Seneca\nDoane.\n\nHe had not seen Zilla since Paul had shot her, and he still pictured her\nas buxom, high-colored, lively, and a little blowsy. As he drove up to\nher boarding-house, in a depressing back street below the wholesale\ndistrict, he stopped in discomfort. At an upper window, leaning on her\nelbow, was a woman with the features of Zilla, but she was bloodless and\naged, like a yellowed wad of old paper crumpled into wrinkles. Where\nZilla had bounced and jiggled, this woman was dreadfully still.\n\nHe waited half an hour before she came into the boarding-house parlor.\nFifty times he opened the book of photographs of the Chicago World\xe2\x80\x99s\nFair of 1893, fifty times he looked at the picture of the Court of\nHonor.\n\nHe was startled to find Zilla in the room. She wore a black streaky gown\nwhich she had tried to brighten with a girdle of crimson ribbon. The\nribbon had been torn and patiently mended. He noted this carefully,\nbecause he did not wish to look at her shoulders. One shoulder was lower\nthan the other; one arm she carried in contorted fashion, as though it\nwere paralyzed; and behind a high collar of cheap lace there was a gouge\nin the anemic neck which had once been shining and softly plump.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes?\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, well, old Zilla! By golly, it\xe2\x80\x99s good to see you again!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe can send his messages through a lawyer.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, rats, Zilla, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t come just because of him. Came as an old\nfriend.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou waited long enough!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you know how it is. Figured you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t want to see a friend of\nhis for quite some time and-- Sit down, honey! Let\xe2\x80\x99s be sensible. We\xe2\x80\x99ve\nall of us done a bunch of things that we hadn\xe2\x80\x99t ought to, but maybe we\ncan sort of start over again. Honest, Zilla, I\xe2\x80\x99d like to do something to\nmake you both happy. Know what I thought to-day? Mind you, Paul doesn\xe2\x80\x99t\nknow a thing about this--doesn\xe2\x80\x99t know I was going to come see you. I got\nto thinking: Zilla\xe2\x80\x99s a fine, big-hearted woman, and she\xe2\x80\x99ll understand\nthat, uh, Paul\xe2\x80\x99s had his lesson now. Why wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t it be a fine idea if\nyou asked the governor to pardon him? Believe he would, if it came from\nyou. No! Wait! Just think how good you\xe2\x80\x99d feel if you were generous.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I wish to be generous.\xe2\x80\x9d She was sitting primly, speaking icily.\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor that reason I wish to keep him in prison, as an example to\nevil-doers. I\xe2\x80\x99ve gotten religion, George, since the terrible thing that\nman did to me. Sometimes I used to be unkind, and I wished for worldly\npleasures, for dancing and the theater. But when I was in the hospital\nthe pastor of the Pentecostal Communion Faith used to come to see me,\nand he showed me, right from the prophecies written in the Word of God,\nthat the Day of Judgment is coming and all the members of the older\nchurches are going straight to eternal damnation, because they only do\nlip-service and swallow the world, the flesh, and the devil--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nFor fifteen wild minutes she talked, pouring out admonitions to flee the\nwrath to come, and her face flushed, her dead voice recaptured something\nof the shrill energy of the old Zilla. She wound up with a furious:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the blessing of God himself that Paul should be in prison now, and\ntorn and humbled by punishment, so that he may yet save his soul, and so\nother wicked men, these horrible chasers after women and lust, may have\nan example.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBabbitt had itched and twisted. As in church he dared not move during\nthe sermon so now he felt that he must seem attentive, though her\nscreeching denunciations flew past him like carrion birds.\n\nHe sought to be calm and brotherly:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I know, Zilla. But gosh, it certainly is the essence of religion\nto be charitable, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it? Let me tell you how I figure it: What we\nneed in the world is liberalism, liberality, if we\xe2\x80\x99re going to get\nanywhere. I\xe2\x80\x99ve always believed in being broad-minded and liberal--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou? Liberal?\xe2\x80\x9d It was very much the old Zilla. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, George Babbitt,\nyou\xe2\x80\x99re about as broad-minded and liberal as a razor-blade!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I am, am I! Well, just let me tell you, just--let--me--tell--you,\nI\xe2\x80\x99m as by golly liberal as you are religious, anyway! _You religious!_\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am so! Our pastor says I sustain him in the faith!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll bet you do! With Paul\xe2\x80\x99s money! But just to show you how liberal I\nam, I\xe2\x80\x99m going to send a check for ten bucks to this Beecher Ingram,\nbecause a lot of fellows are saying the poor cuss preaches sedition and\nfree love, and they\xe2\x80\x99re trying to run him out of town.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd they\xe2\x80\x99re right! They ought to run him out of town! Why, he\npreaches--if you can call it preaching--in a theater, in the House of\nSatan! You don\xe2\x80\x99t know what it is to find God, to find peace, to behold\nthe snares that the devil spreads out for our feet. Oh, I\xe2\x80\x99m so glad to\nsee the mysterious purposes of God in having Paul harm me and stop my\nwickedness--and Paul\xe2\x80\x99s getting his, good and plenty, for the cruel\nthings he did to me, and I hope he _dies_ in prison!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBabbitt was up, hat in hand, growling, \xe2\x80\x9cWell, if that\xe2\x80\x99s what you call\nbeing at peace, for heaven\xe2\x80\x99s sake just warn me before you go to war,\nwill you?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\nIII\n\nVast is the power of cities to reclaim the wanderer. More than mountains\nor the shore-devouring sea, a city retains its character, imperturbable,\ncynical, holding behind apparent changes its essential purpose. Though\nBabbitt had deserted his family and dwelt with Joe Paradise in the\nwilderness, though he had become a liberal, though he had been quite\nsure, on the night before he reached Zenith, that neither he nor the\ncity would be the same again, ten days after his return he could not\nbelieve that he had ever been away. Nor was it at all evident to his\nacquaintances that there was a new George F. Babbitt, save that he was\nmore irritable under the incessant chaffing at the Athletic Club, and\nonce, when Vergil Gunch observed that Seneca Doane ought to be hanged,\nBabbitt snorted, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, rats, he\xe2\x80\x99s not so bad.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAt home he grunted \xe2\x80\x9cEh?\xe2\x80\x9d across the newspaper to his commentatory wife,\nand was delighted by Tinka\xe2\x80\x99s new red tam o\xe2\x80\x99shanter, and announced, \xe2\x80\x9cNo\nclass to that corrugated iron garage. Have to build me a nice frame\none.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nVerona and Kenneth Escott appeared really to be engaged. In his\nnewspaper Escott had conducted a pure-food crusade against\ncommission-houses. As a result he had been given an excellent job in a\ncommission-house, and he was making a salary on which he could marry,\nand denouncing irresponsible reporters who wrote stories criticizing\ncommission-houses without knowing what they were talking about.\n\nThis September Ted had entered the State University as a freshman in the\nCollege of Arts and Sciences. The university was at Mohalis, only\nfifteen miles from Zenith, and Ted often came down for the week-end.\nBabbitt was worried. Ted was \xe2\x80\x9cgoing in for\xe2\x80\x9d everything but books. He had\ntried to \xe2\x80\x9cmake\xe2\x80\x9d the football team as a light half-back, he was looking\nforward to the basket-ball season, he was on the committee for the\nFreshman Hop, and (as a Zenithite, an aristocrat among the yokels) he\nwas being \xe2\x80\x9crushed\xe2\x80\x9d by two fraternities. But of his studies Babbitt could\nlearn nothing save a mumbled, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, gosh, these old stiffs of teachers\njust give you a lot of junk about literature and economics.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nOne week-end Ted proposed, \xe2\x80\x9cSay, Dad, why can\xe2\x80\x99t I transfer over from the\nCollege to the School of Engineering and take mechanical engineering?\nYou always holler that I never study, but honest, I would study there.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, the Engineering School hasn\xe2\x80\x99t got the standing the College has,\xe2\x80\x9d\nfretted Babbitt.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d like to know how it hasn\xe2\x80\x99t! The Engineers can play on any of the\nteams!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThere was much explanation of the \xe2\x80\x9cdollars-and-cents value of being\nknown as a college man when you go into the law,\xe2\x80\x9d and a truly oratorical\naccount of the lawyer\xe2\x80\x99s life. Before he was through with it, Babbitt had\nTed a United States Senator.\n\nAmong the great lawyers whom he mentioned was Seneca Doane.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, gee whiz,\xe2\x80\x9d Ted marveled, \xe2\x80\x9cI thought you always said this Doane was\na reg\xe2\x80\x99lar nut!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s no way to speak of a great man! Doane\xe2\x80\x99s always been a good\nfriend of mine--fact I helped him in college-- I started him out and you\nmight say inspired him. Just because he\xe2\x80\x99s sympathetic with the aims of\nLabor, a lot of chumps that lack liberality and broad-mindedness think\nhe\xe2\x80\x99s a crank, but let me tell you there\xe2\x80\x99s mighty few of \xe2\x80\x99em that rake in\nthe fees he does, and he\xe2\x80\x99s a friend of some of the strongest, most\nconservative men in the world--like Lord Wycombe, this, uh, this big\nEnglish nobleman that\xe2\x80\x99s so well known. And you now, which would you\nrather do: be in with a lot of greasy mechanics and laboring-men, or\nchum up to a real fellow like Lord Wycombe, and get invited to his house\nfor parties?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell--gosh,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Ted.\n\nThe next week-end he came in joyously with, \xe2\x80\x9cSay, Dad, why couldn\xe2\x80\x99t I\ntake mining engineering instead of the academic course? You talk about\nstanding--maybe there isn\xe2\x80\x99t much in mechanical engineering, but the\nMiners, gee, they got seven out of eleven in the new elections to Nu Tau\nTau!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\n\nCHAPTER XXVII\n\n\nI\n\nThe strike which turned Zenith into two belligerent camps, white and\nred, began late in September with a walk-out of telephone girls and\nlinemen, in protest against a reduction of wages. The newly formed union\nof dairy-products workers went out, partly in sympathy and partly in\ndemand for a forty-four hour week. They were followed by the\ntruck-drivers\xe2\x80\x99 union. Industry was tied up, and the whole city was\nnervous with talk of a trolley strike, a printers\xe2\x80\x99 strike, a general\nstrike. Furious citizens, trying to get telephone calls through\nstrike-breaking girls, danced helplessly. Every truck that made its way\nfrom the factories to the freight-stations was guarded by a policeman,\ntrying to look stoical beside the scab driver. A line of fifty trucks\nfrom the Zenith Steel and Machinery Company was attacked by\nstrikers--rushing out from the sidewalk, pulling drivers from the seats,\nsmashing carburetors and commutators, while telephone girls cheered from\nthe walk, and small boys heaved bricks.\n\nThe National Guard was ordered out. Colonel Nixon, who in private life\nwas Mr. Caleb Nixon, secretary of the Pullmore Tractor Company, put on a\nlong khaki coat and stalked through crowds, a .44 automatic in hand.\nEven Babbitt\xe2\x80\x99s friend, Clarence Drum the shoe merchant--a round and\nmerry man who told stories at the Athletic Club, and who strangely\nresembled a Victorian pug-dog--was to be seen as a waddling but\nferocious captain, with his belt tight about his comfortable little\nbelly, and his round little mouth petulant as he piped to chattering\ngroups on corners. \xe2\x80\x9cMove on there now! I can\xe2\x80\x99t have any of this\nloitering!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nEvery newspaper in the city, save one, was against the strikers. When\nmobs raided the news-stands, at each was stationed a militiaman, a\nyoung, embarrassed citizen-soldier with eye-glasses, bookkeeper or\ngrocery-clerk in private life, trying to look dangerous while small boys\nyelped, \xe2\x80\x9cGet onto de tin soldier!\xe2\x80\x9d and striking truck-drivers inquired\ntenderly, \xe2\x80\x9cSay, Joe, when I was fighting in France, was you in camp in\nthe States or was you doing Swede exercises in the Y. M. C. A.? Be\ncareful of that bayonet, now, or you\xe2\x80\x99ll cut yourself!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThere was no one in Zenith who talked of anything but the strike, and no\none who did not take sides. You were either a courageous friend of\nLabor, or you were a fearless supporter of the Rights of Property; and\nin either case you were belligerent, and ready to disown any friend who\ndid not hate the enemy.\n\nA condensed-milk plant was set afire--each side charged it to the\nother--and the city was hysterical.\n\nAnd Babbitt chose this time to be publicly liberal.\n\nHe belonged to the sound, sane, right-thinking wing, and at first he\nagreed that the Crooked Agitators ought to be shot. He was sorry when\nhis friend, Seneca Doane, defended arrested strikers, and he thought of\ngoing to Doane and explaining about these agitators, but when he read a\nbroadside alleging that even on their former wages the telephone girls\nhad been hungry, he was troubled. \xe2\x80\x9cAll lies and fake figures,\xe2\x80\x9d he said,\nbut in a doubtful croak.\n\nFor the Sunday after, the Chatham Road Presbyterian Church announced a\nsermon by Dr. John Jennison Drew on \xe2\x80\x9cHow the Saviour Would End Strikes.\xe2\x80\x9d\nBabbitt had been negligent about church-going lately, but he went to the\nservice, hopeful that Dr. Drew really did have the information as to\nwhat the divine powers thought about strikes. Beside Babbitt in the\nlarge, curving, glossy, velvet-upholstered pew was Chum Frink.\n\nFrink whispered, \xe2\x80\x9cHope the doc gives the strikers hell! Ordinarily, I\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t believe in a preacher butting into political matters--let him\nstick to straight religion and save souls, and not stir up a lot of\ndiscussion--but at a time like this, I do think he ought to stand right\nup and bawl out those plug-uglies to a fare-you-well!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes--well--\xe2\x80\x9d said Babbitt.\n\nThe Rev. Dr. Drew, his rustic bang flopping with the intensity of his\npoetic and sociologic ardor, trumpeted:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDuring the untoward series of industrial dislocations which have--let\nus be courageous and admit it boldly--throttled the business life of our\nfair city these past days, there has been a great deal of loose talk\nabout scientific prevention of scientific--_scientific!_ Now, let me\ntell you that the most unscientific thing in the world is science! Take\nthe attacks on the established fundamentals of the Christian creed which\nwere so popular with the \xe2\x80\x98scientists\xe2\x80\x99 a generation ago. Oh, yes, they\nwere mighty fellows, and great poo-bahs of criticism! They were going to\ndestroy the church; they were going to prove the world was created and\nhas been brought to its extraordinary level of morality and civilization\nby blind chance. Yet the church stands just as firmly to-day as ever,\nand the only answer a Christian pastor needs make to the long-haired\nopponents of his simple faith is just a pitying smile!\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now these same \xe2\x80\x98scientists\xe2\x80\x99 want to replace the natural condition\nof free competition by crazy systems which, no matter by what\nhigh-sounding names they are called, are nothing but a despotic\npaternalism. Naturally, I\xe2\x80\x99m not criticizing labor courts, injunctions\nagainst men proven to be striking unjustly, or those excellent unions in\nwhich the men and the boss get together. But I certainly am criticizing\nthe systems in which the free and fluid motivation of independent labor\nis to be replaced by cooked-up wage-scales and minimum salaries and\ngovernment commissions and labor federations and all that poppycock.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is not generally understood is that this whole industrial matter\nisn\xe2\x80\x99t a question of economics. It\xe2\x80\x99s essentially and only a matter of\nLove, and of the practical application of the Christian religion!\nImagine a factory--instead of committees of workmen alienating the boss,\nthe boss goes among them smiling, and they smile back, the elder brother\nand the younger. Brothers, that\xe2\x80\x99s what they must be, loving brothers,\nand then strikes would be as inconceivable as hatred in the home!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nIt was at this point that Babbitt muttered, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, rot!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuh?\xe2\x80\x9d said Chum Frink.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe doesn\xe2\x80\x99t know what he\xe2\x80\x99s talking about. It\xe2\x80\x99s just as clear as mud. It\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t mean a darn thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaybe, but--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nFrink looked at him doubtfully, through all the service kept glancing at\nhim doubtfully, till Babbitt was nervous.\n\n\nII\n\nThe strikers had announced a parade for Tuesday morning, but Colonel\nNixon had forbidden it, the newspapers said. When Babbitt drove west\nfrom his office at ten that morning, he saw a drove of shabby men\nheading toward the tangled, dirty district beyond Court House Square. He\nhated them, because they were poor, because they made him feel insecure.\n\xe2\x80\x9cDamn loafers! Wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be common workmen if they had any pep,\xe2\x80\x9d he\ncomplained. He wondered if there was going to be a riot. He drove toward\nthe starting-point of the parade, a triangle of limp and faded grass\nknown as Moore Street Park, and halted his car.\n\nThe park and streets were buzzing with strikers, young men in blue denim\nshirts, old men with caps. Through them, keeping them stirred like a\nboiling pot, moved the militiamen. Babbitt could hear the soldiers\xe2\x80\x99\nmonotonous orders: \xe2\x80\x9cKeep moving--move on, \xe2\x80\x99bo--keep your feet warm!\xe2\x80\x9d\nBabbitt admired their stolid good temper. The crowd shouted, \xe2\x80\x9cTin\nsoldiers,\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cDirty dogs--servants of the capitalists!\xe2\x80\x9d but the\nmilitiamen grinned and answered only, \xe2\x80\x9cSure, that\xe2\x80\x99s right. Keep moving,\nBilly!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBabbitt thrilled over the citizen-soldiers, hated the scoundrels who\nwere obstructing the pleasant ways of prosperity, admired Colonel\nNixon\xe2\x80\x99s striding contempt for the crowd; and as Captain Clarence Drum,\nthat rather puffing shoe-dealer, came raging by, Babbitt respectfully\nclamored, \xe2\x80\x9cGreat work, Captain! Don\xe2\x80\x99t let \xe2\x80\x99em march!\xe2\x80\x9d He watched the\nstrikers filing from the park. Many of them bore posters with \xe2\x80\x9cThey\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t stop our peacefully walking.\xe2\x80\x9d The militiamen tore away the\nposters, but the strikers fell in behind their leaders and straggled\noff, a thin unimpressive trickle between steel-glinting lines of\nsoldiers. Babbitt saw with disappointment that there wasn\xe2\x80\x99t going to be\nany violence, nothing interesting at all. Then he gasped.\n\nAmong the marchers, beside a bulky young workman, was Seneca Doane,\nsmiling, content. In front of him was Professor Brockbank, head of the\nhistory department in the State University, an old man and\nwhite-bearded, known to come from a distinguished Massachusetts family.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, gosh,\xe2\x80\x9d Babbitt marveled, \xe2\x80\x9ca swell like him in with the strikers?\nAnd good ole Senny Doane! They\xe2\x80\x99re fools to get mixed up with this bunch.\nThey\xe2\x80\x99re parlor socialists! But they have got nerve. And nothing in it\nfor them, not a cent! And-- I don\xe2\x80\x99t know \xe2\x80\x99s _all_ the strikers look like\nsuch tough nuts. Look just about like anybody else to me!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThe militiamen were turning the parade down a side street.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey got just as much right to march as anybody else! They own the\nstreets as much as Clarence Drum or the American Legion does!\xe2\x80\x9d Babbitt\ngrumbled. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course, they\xe2\x80\x99re--they\xe2\x80\x99re a bad element, but-- Oh, rats!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAt the Athletic Club, Babbitt was silent during lunch, while the others\nfretted, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what the world\xe2\x80\x99s coming to,\xe2\x80\x9d or solaced their\nspirits with \xe2\x80\x9ckidding.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nCaptain Clarence Drum came swinging by, splendid in khaki.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow\xe2\x80\x99s it going, Captain?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Vergil Gunch.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, we got \xe2\x80\x99em stopped. We worked \xe2\x80\x99em off on side streets and separated\n\xe2\x80\x99em and they got discouraged and went home.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cFine work. No violence.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cFine work nothing!\xe2\x80\x9d groaned Mr. Drum. \xe2\x80\x9cIf I had my way, there\xe2\x80\x99d be a\nwhole lot of violence, and I\xe2\x80\x99d start it, and then the whole thing would\nbe over. I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe in standing back and wet-nursing these fellows\nand letting the disturbances drag on. I tell you these strikers are\nnothing in God\xe2\x80\x99s world but a lot of bomb-throwing socialists and thugs,\nand the only way to handle \xe2\x80\x99em is with a club! That\xe2\x80\x99s what I\xe2\x80\x99d do; beat\nup the whole lot of \xe2\x80\x99em!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBabbitt heard himself saying, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, rats, Clarence, they look just about\nlike you and me, and I certainly didn\xe2\x80\x99t notice any bombs.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nDrum complained, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, you didn\xe2\x80\x99t, eh? Well, maybe you\xe2\x80\x99d like to take\ncharge of the strike! Just tell Colonel Nixon what innocents the\nstrikers are! He\xe2\x80\x99d be glad to hear about it!\xe2\x80\x9d Drum strode on, while all\nthe table stared at Babbitt.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the idea? Do you want us to give those hell-hounds love and\nkisses, or what?\xe2\x80\x9d said Orville Jones.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you defend a lot of hoodlums that are trying to take the bread and\nbutter away from our families?\xe2\x80\x9d raged Professor Pumphrey.\n\nVergil Gunch intimidatingly said nothing. He put on sternness like a\nmask; his jaw was hard, his bristly short hair seemed cruel, his silence\nwas a ferocious thunder. While the others assured Babbitt that they must\nhave misunderstood him, Gunch looked as though he had understood only\ntoo well. Like a robed judge he listened to Babbitt\xe2\x80\x99s stammering:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sure; course they\xe2\x80\x99re a bunch of toughs. But I just mean-- Strikes me\nit\xe2\x80\x99s bad policy to talk about clubbing \xe2\x80\x99em. Cabe Nixon doesn\xe2\x80\x99t. He\xe2\x80\x99s got\nthe fine Italian hand. And that\xe2\x80\x99s why he\xe2\x80\x99s colonel. Clarence Drum is\njealous of him.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Professor Pumphrey, \xe2\x80\x9cyou hurt Clarence\xe2\x80\x99s feelings, George.\nHe\xe2\x80\x99s been out there all morning getting hot and dusty, and no wonder he\nwants to beat the tar out of those sons of guns!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nGunch said nothing, and watched; and Babbitt knew that he was being\nwatched.\n\n\nIII\n\nAs he was leaving the club Babbitt heard Chum Frink protesting to Gunch,\n\xe2\x80\x9c--don\xe2\x80\x99t know what\xe2\x80\x99s got into him. Last Sunday Doc Drew preached a\ncorking sermon about decency in business and Babbitt kicked about that,\ntoo. Near\xe2\x80\x99s I can figure out--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBabbitt was vaguely frightened.\n\n\nIV\n\nHe saw a crowd listening to a man who was talking from the rostrum of a\nkitchen-chair. He stopped his car. From newspaper pictures he knew that\nthe speaker must be the notorious freelance preacher, Beecher Ingram, of\nwhom Seneca Doane had spoken. Ingram was a gaunt man with flamboyant\nhair, weather-beaten cheeks, and worried eyes. He was pleading:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9c--if those telephone girls can hold out, living on one meal a day,\ndoing their own washing, starving and smiling, you big hulking men ought\nto be able--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBabbitt saw that from the sidewalk Vergil Gunch was watching him. In\nvague disquiet he started the car and mechanically drove on, while\nGunch\xe2\x80\x99s hostile eyes seemed to follow him all the way.\n\n\nV\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s a lot of these fellows,\xe2\x80\x9d Babbitt was complaining to his wife,\n\xe2\x80\x9cthat think if workmen go on strike they\xe2\x80\x99re a regular bunch of fiends.\nNow, of course, it\xe2\x80\x99s a fight between sound business and the destructive\nelement, and we got to lick the stuffin\xe2\x80\x99s out of \xe2\x80\x99em when they challenge\nus, but doggoned if I see why we can\xe2\x80\x99t fight like gentlemen and not go\ncalling \xe2\x80\x99em dirty dogs and saying they ought to be shot down.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, George,\xe2\x80\x9d she said placidly, \xe2\x80\x9cI thought you always insisted that\nall strikers ought to be put in jail.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never did! Well, I mean-- Some of \xe2\x80\x99em, of course. Irresponsible\nleaders. Woman never can understand the different definitions of a word.\nDepends on how you mean it. And it don\xe2\x80\x99t pay to be too cocksure about\nanything. If people were to hear you talk like that--of course I _know_\nyou; I remember what a wild crazy boy you were; I know you don\xe2\x80\x99t mean a\nword you say--but if people that didn\xe2\x80\x99t understand you were to hear you\ntalking, they\xe2\x80\x99d think you were a regular socialist!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do I care what anybody thinks? Way Verg looked at me--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\n\nCHAPTER XXVIII\n\n\nMiss McGoun came into his private office at three in the afternoon with\n\xe2\x80\x9cLissen, Mr. Babbitt; there\xe2\x80\x99s a Mrs. Judique on the \xe2\x80\x99phone--wants to see\nabout some repairs, and the salesmen are all out. Except that Vergil Gunch\nseemed less cordial, there were no visible effects of Babbitt\xe2\x80\x99s\ntreachery to the clan. The oppressive fear of criticism was gone, but a\ndiffident loneliness remained. Now he was so exhilarated that, to prove\nhe wasn\xe2\x80\x99t, he droned about the office for fifteen minutes, looking at\nblue-prints, explaining to Miss McGoun that this Mrs. Scott wanted more\nmoney for her house--had raised the asking-price--raised it from seven\nthousand to eighty-five hundred--would Miss McGoun be sure and put it\ndown on the card--Mrs. Scott\xe2\x80\x99s house--raise. He took a particularly long time to start\nhis car; he kicked the tires, dusted the glass of the speedometer, and\ntightened the screws holding the wind-shield spot-light.\n\nHe drove happily off toward the Bellevue district, conscious of the\npresence of Mrs. Judique as of a brilliant light on the horizon. Babbitt was aware of the meditative day, and of the\nbarrenness of Bellevue--blocks of wooden houses, garages, little shops,\nweedy lots. \xe2\x80\x9cNeeds pepping up; needs the touch that people like Mrs.\nJudique could give a place,\xe2\x80\x9d he ruminated, as he rattled through the\nlong, crude, airy streets.
