His manner of bringing the\nash-tray had just the right note of easy friendliness, he felt. \xe2\x80\x9cA boob\nlike Verg Gunch would try to get funny about seeing her bedroom, but I\ntake it casually.\xe2\x80\x9d He was not casual afterward. The contentment of\ncompanionship was gone, and he was restless with desire to touch her\nhand. But whenever he turned toward her, the cigarette was in his way.\nIt was a shield between them. He waited till she should have finished,\nbut as he rejoiced at her quick crushing of its light on the ash-tray\nshe said, \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you want to give me another cigarette?\xe2\x80\x9d and hopelessly\nhe saw the screen of pale smoke and her graceful tilted hand again\nbetween them. He was not merely curious now to find out whether she\nwould let him hold her hand (all in the purest friendship, naturally),\nbut agonized with need of it.\n\nOn the surface appeared none of all this fretful drama. They were\ntalking cheerfully of motors, of trips to California, of Chum Frink.\nOnce he said delicately, \xe2\x80\x9cI do hate these guys-- I hate these people that\ninvite themselves to meals, but I seem to have a feeling I\xe2\x80\x99m going to\nhave supper with the lovely Mrs. Tanis Judique to-night. But I suppose\nyou probably have seven dates already.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I was thinking some of going to the movies. Yes, I really think I\nought to get out and get some fresh air.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nShe did not encourage him to stay, but never did she discourage him. He\nconsidered, \xe2\x80\x9cI better take a sneak! She _will_ let me stay--there _is_\nsomething doing--and I mustn\xe2\x80\x99t get mixed up with-- I mustn\xe2\x80\x99t-- I\xe2\x80\x99ve got to\nbeat it.\xe2\x80\x9d Then, \xe2\x80\x9cNo, it\xe2\x80\x99s too late now.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nSuddenly, at seven, brushing her cigarette away, brusquely taking her\nhand:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cTanis! Stop teasing me! You know we-- Here we are, a couple of lonely\nbirds, and we\xe2\x80\x99re awful happy together. Anyway I am! Never been so happy!\nDo let me stay! I\xe2\x80\x99ll gallop down to the delicatessen and buy some\nstuff--cold chicken maybe--or cold turkey--and we can have a nice little\nsupper, and afterwards, if you want to chase me out, I\xe2\x80\x99ll be good and go\nlike a lamb.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell--yes--it would be nice,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\n\nNor did she withdraw her hand. He squeezed it, trembling, and blundered\ntoward his coat. At the delicatessen he bought preposterous stores of\nfood, chosen on the principle of expensiveness. From the drug store\nacross the street he telephoned to his wife, \xe2\x80\x9cGot to get a fellow to\nsign a lease before he leaves town on the midnight. Won\xe2\x80\x99t be home till\nlate. Don\xe2\x80\x99t wait up for me. Kiss Tinka good-night.\xe2\x80\x9d He expectantly\nlumbered back to the flat.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you bad thing, to buy so much food!\xe2\x80\x9d was her greeting, and her\nvoice was gay, her smile acceptant.\n\nHe helped her in the tiny white kitchen; he washed the lettuce, he\nopened the olive bottle. She ordered him to set the table, and as he\ntrotted into the living-room, as he hunted through the buffet for knives\nand forks, he felt utterly at home.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow the only other thing,\xe2\x80\x9d he announced, \xe2\x80\x9cis what you\xe2\x80\x99re going to wear.\nI can\xe2\x80\x99t decide whether you\xe2\x80\x99re to put on your swellest evening gown, or\nlet your hair down and put on short skirts and make-believe you\xe2\x80\x99re a\nlittle girl.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m going to dine just as I am, in this old chiffon rag, and if you\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t stand poor Tanis that way, you can go to the club for dinner!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cStand you!\xe2\x80\x9d He patted her shoulder. \xe2\x80\x9cChild, you\xe2\x80\x99re the brainiest and\nthe loveliest and finest woman I\xe2\x80\x99ve ever met! Come now, Lady Wycombe, if\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ll take the Duke of Zenith\xe2\x80\x99s arm, we will proambulate in to the\nmagnolious feed!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you do say the funniest, nicest things!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nWhen they had finished the picnic supper he thrust his head out of the\nwindow and reported, \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s turned awful chilly, and I think it\xe2\x80\x99s going\nto rain. You don\xe2\x80\x99t want to go to the movies.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish we had a fireplace! I wish it was raining like all get-out\nto-night, and we were in a funny little old-fashioned cottage, and the\ntrees thrashing like everything outside, and a great big log fire\nand-- I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you! Let\xe2\x80\x99s draw this couch up to the radiator, and\nstretch our feet out, and pretend it\xe2\x80\x99s a wood-fire.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I think that\xe2\x80\x99s pathetic! You big child!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBut they did draw up to the radiator, and propped their feet against\nit--his clumsy black shoes, her patent-leather slippers. In the dimness\nthey talked of themselves; of how lonely she was, how bewildered he, and\nhow wonderful that they had found each other. As they fell silent the\nroom was stiller than a country lane. There was no sound from the street\nsave the whir of motor-tires, the rumble of a distant freight-train.\nSelf-contained was the room, warm, secure, insulated from the harassing\nworld.\n\nHe was absorbed by a rapture in which all fear and doubting were\nsmoothed away; and when he reached home, at dawn, the rapture had\nmellowed to contentment serene and full of memories.\n\n\n\n\nCHAPTER XXIX\n\n\nI\n\nThe assurance of Tanis Judique\xe2\x80\x99s friendship fortified Babbitt\xe2\x80\x99s\nself-approval. At the Athletic Club he became experimental. Though\nVergil Gunch was silent, the others at the Roughnecks\xe2\x80\x99 Table came to\naccept Babbitt as having, for no visible reason, \xe2\x80\x9cturned crank.\xe2\x80\x9d They\nargued windily with him, and he was cocky, and enjoyed the spectacle of\nhis interesting martyrdom. He even praised Seneca Doane. Professor\nPumphrey said that was carrying a joke too far; but Babbitt argued, \xe2\x80\x9cNo!\nFact! I tell you he\xe2\x80\x99s got one of the keenest intellects in the country.\nWhy, Lord Wycombe said that--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, who the hell is Lord Wycombe? What you always lugging him in for?\nYou been touting him for the last six weeks!\xe2\x80\x9d protested Orville Jones.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGeorge ordered him from Sears-Roebuck. You can get those English\nhigh-muckamucks by mail for two bucks apiece,\xe2\x80\x9d suggested Sidney\nFinkelstein.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s all right now! Lord Wycombe, he\xe2\x80\x99s one of the biggest intellects\nin English political life. As I was saying: Of course I\xe2\x80\x99m conservative\nmyself, but I appreciate a guy like Senny Doane because--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nVergil Gunch interrupted harshly, \xe2\x80\x9cI wonder if you are so conservative?\nI find I can manage to run my own business without any skunks and reds\nlike Doane in it!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThe grimness of Gunch\xe2\x80\x99s voice, the hardness of his jaw, disconcerted\nBabbitt, but he recovered and went on till they looked bored, then\nirritated, then as doubtful as Gunch.\n\n\nII\n\nHe thought of Tanis always. With a stir he remembered her every aspect.\nHis arms yearned for her. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve found her! I\xe2\x80\x99ve dreamed of her all these\nyears and now I\xe2\x80\x99ve found her!\xe2\x80\x9d he exulted. He met her at the movies in\nthe morning; he drove out to her flat in the late afternoon or on\nevenings when he was believed to be at the Elks. He knew her financial\naffairs and advised her about them, while she lamented her feminine\nignorance, and praised his masterfulness, and proved to know much more\nabout bonds than he did. They had remembrances, and laughter over old\ntimes. Once they quarreled, and he raged that she was as \xe2\x80\x9cbossy\xe2\x80\x9d as his\nwife and far more whining when he was inattentive. But that passed\nsafely.\n\nTheir high hour was a tramp on a ringing December afternoon, through\nsnow-drifted meadows down to the icy Chaloosa River. She was exotic in\nan astrachan cap and a short beaver coat; she slid on the ice and\nshouted, and he panted after her, rotund with laughter.... Myra Babbitt\nnever slid on the ice.\n\nHe was afraid that they would be seen together. In Zenith it is\nimpossible to lunch with a neighbor\xe2\x80\x99s wife without the fact being known,\nbefore nightfall, in every house in your circle. But Tanis was\nbeautifully discreet. However appealingly she might turn to him when\nthey were alone, she was gravely detached when they were abroad, and he\nhoped that she would be taken for a client. Orville Jones once saw them\nemerging from a movie theater, and Babbitt bumbled, \xe2\x80\x9cLet me make you\n\xe2\x80\x99quainted with Mrs. Judique. Now here\xe2\x80\x99s a lady who knows the right\nbroker to come to, Orvy!\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Jones, though he was a man censorious of\nmorals and of laundry machinery, seemed satisfied.\n\nHis predominant fear--not from any especial fondness for her but from\nthe habit of propriety--was that his wife would learn of the affair. He\nwas certain that she knew nothing specific about Tanis, but he was also\ncertain that she suspected something indefinite. For years she had been\nbored by anything more affectionate than a farewell kiss, yet she was\nhurt by any slackening in his irritable periodic interest, and now he\nhad no interest; rather, a revulsion. He was completely faithful--to\nTanis. He was distressed by the sight of his wife\xe2\x80\x99s slack plumpness, by\nher puffs and billows of flesh, by the tattered petticoat which she was\nalways meaning and always forgetting to throw away. But he was aware\nthat she, so long attuned to him, caught all his repulsions. He\nelaborately, heavily, jocularly tried to check them. He couldn\xe2\x80\x99t.\n\nThey had a tolerable Christmas. Kenneth Escott was there, admittedly\nengaged to Verona. Mrs. Babbitt was tearful and called Kenneth her new\nson. Babbitt was worried about Ted, because he had ceased complaining of\nthe State University and become suspiciously acquiescent. He wondered\nwhat the boy was planning, and was too shy to ask. Himself, Babbitt\nslipped away on Christmas afternoon to take his present, a silver\ncigarette-box, to Tanis. When he returned Mrs. Babbitt asked, much too\ninnocently, \xe2\x80\x9cDid you go out for a little fresh air?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, just lil drive,\xe2\x80\x9d he mumbled.\n\nAfter New Year\xe2\x80\x99s his wife proposed, \xe2\x80\x9cI heard from my sister to-day,\nGeorge. She isn\xe2\x80\x99t well. I think perhaps I ought to go stay with her for\na few weeks.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nNow, Mrs. Babbitt was not accustomed to leave home during the winter\nexcept on violently demanding occasions, and only the summer before, she\nhad been gone for weeks. Nor was Babbitt one of the detachable husbands\nwho take separations casually. He liked to have her there; she looked\nafter his clothes; she knew how his steak ought to be cooked; and her\nclucking made him feel secure. But he could not drum up even a dutiful\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, she doesn\xe2\x80\x99t really need you, does she?\xe2\x80\x9d While he tried to look\nregretful, while he felt that his wife was watching him, he was filled\nwith exultant visions of Tanis.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think I\xe2\x80\x99d better go?\xe2\x80\x9d she said sharply.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve got to decide, honey; I can\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nShe turned away, sighing, and his forehead was damp.\n\nTill she went, four days later, she was curiously still, he cumbrously\naffectionate. Her train left at noon. As he saw it grow small beyond the\ntrain-shed he longed to hurry to Tanis.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, by golly, I won\xe2\x80\x99t do that!\xe2\x80\x9d he vowed. \xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t go near her for a\nweek!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBut he was at her flat at four.\n\n\nIII\n\nHe who had once controlled or seemed to control his life in a progress\nunimpassioned but diligent and sane was for that fortnight borne on a\ncurrent of desire and very bad whisky and all the complications of new\nacquaintances, those furious new intimates who demand so much more\nattention than old friends. Each morning he gloomily recognized his\nidiocies of the evening before. With his head throbbing, his tongue and\nlips stinging from cigarettes, he incredulously counted the number of\ndrinks he had taken, and groaned, \xe2\x80\x9cI got to quit!\xe2\x80\x9d He had ceased saying,\n\xe2\x80\x9cI _will_ quit!\xe2\x80\x9d for however resolute he might be at dawn, he could not,\nfor a single evening, check his drift.\n\nHe had met Tanis\xe2\x80\x99s friends; he had, with the ardent haste of the\nMidnight People, who drink and dance and rattle and are ever afraid to\nbe silent, been adopted as a member of her group, which they called \xe2\x80\x9cThe\nBunch.\xe2\x80\x9d He first met them after a day when he had worked particularly\nhard and when he hoped to be quiet with Tanis and slowly sip her\nadmiration.\n\nFrom down the hall he could hear shrieks and the grind of a phonograph.\nAs Tanis opened the door he saw fantastic figures dancing in a haze of\ncigarette smoke. The tables and chairs were against the wall.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, isn\xe2\x80\x99t this dandy!\xe2\x80\x9d she gabbled at him. \xe2\x80\x9cCarrie Nork had the\nloveliest idea. She decided it was time for a party, and she \xe2\x80\x99phoned the\nBunch and told \xe2\x80\x99em to gather round.... George, this is Carrie.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cCarrie\xe2\x80\x9d was, in the less desirable aspects of both, at once matronly\nand spinsterish. She was perhaps forty; her hair was an unconvincing\nash-blond; and if her chest was flat, her hips were ponderous. She\ngreeted Babbitt with a giggling \xe2\x80\x9cWelcome to our little midst! Tanis says\nyou\xe2\x80\x99re a real sport.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe was apparently expected to dance, to be boyish and gay with Carrie,\nand he did his unforgiving best. He towed her about the room, bumping\ninto other couples, into the radiator, into chair-legs cunningly\nambushed. As he danced he surveyed the rest of the Bunch: A thin young\nwoman who looked capable, conceited, and sarcastic. Another woman whom\nhe could never quite remember. Three overdressed and slightly effeminate\nyoung men--soda-fountain clerks, or at least born for that profession. A\nman of his own age, immovable, self-satisfied, resentful of Babbitt\xe2\x80\x99s\npresence.\n\nWhen he had finished his dutiful dance Tanis took him aside and begged,\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear, wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t you like to do something for me? I\xe2\x80\x99m all out of booze,\nand the Bunch want to celebrate. Couldn\xe2\x80\x99t you just skip down to Healey\nHanson\xe2\x80\x99s and get some?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSure,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, trying not to sound sullen.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you: I\xe2\x80\x99ll get Minnie Sonntag to drive down with you.\xe2\x80\x9d Tanis\nwas pointing to the thin, sarcastic young woman.\n\nMiss Sonntag greeted him with an astringent \xe2\x80\x9cHow d\xe2\x80\x99you do, Mr. Babbitt.\nTanis tells me you\xe2\x80\x99re a very prominent man, and I\xe2\x80\x99m honored by being\nallowed to drive with you. Of course I\xe2\x80\x99m not accustomed to associating\nwith society people like you, so I don\xe2\x80\x99t know how to act in such exalted\ncircles!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThus Miss Sonntag talked all the way down to Healey Hanson\xe2\x80\x99s. To her\njibes he wanted to reply \xe2\x80\x9cOh, go to the devil!\xe2\x80\x9d but he never quite\nnerved himself to that reasonable comment. He was resenting the\nexistence of the whole Bunch. He had heard Tanis speak of \xe2\x80\x9cdarling\nCarrie\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cMin Sonntag--she\xe2\x80\x99s so clever--you\xe2\x80\x99ll adore her,\xe2\x80\x9d but they\nhad never been real to him. He had pictured Tanis as living in a\nrose-tinted vacuum, waiting for him, free of all the complications of a\nFloral Heights.\n\nWhen they returned he had to endure the patronage of the young\nsoda-clerks. They were as damply friendly as Miss Sonntag was dryly\nhostile. They called him \xe2\x80\x9cOld Georgie\xe2\x80\x9d and shouted, \xe2\x80\x9cCome on now, sport;\nshake a leg\xe2\x80\x9d ... boys in belted coats, pimply boys, as young as Ted and\nas flabby as chorus-men, but powerful to dance and to mind the\nphonograph and smoke cigarettes and patronize Tanis. He tried to be one\nof them; he cried \xe2\x80\x9cGood work, Pete!\xe2\x80\x9d but his voice creaked.\n\nTanis apparently enjoyed the companionship of the dancing darlings; she\nbridled to their bland flirtation and casually kissed them at the end of\neach dance. Babbitt hated her, for the moment. He saw her as\nmiddle-aged. He studied the wrinkles in the softness of her throat, the\nslack flesh beneath her chin. The taut muscles of her youth were loose\nand drooping. Between dances she sat in the largest chair, waving her\ncigarette, summoning her callow admirers to come and talk to her. (\xe2\x80\x9cShe\nthinks she\xe2\x80\x99s a blooming queen!\xe2\x80\x9d growled Babbitt.) She chanted to Miss\nSonntag, \xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t my little studio sweet?\xe2\x80\x9d (\xe2\x80\x9cStudio, rats! It\xe2\x80\x99s a plain\nold-maid-and-chow-dog flat! Oh, God, I wish I was home! I wonder if I\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t make a getaway now?\xe2\x80\x9d)\n\nHis vision grew blurred, however, as he applied himself to Healey\nHanson\xe2\x80\x99s raw but vigorous whisky. He blended with the Bunch. He began to\nrejoice that Carrie Nork and Pete, the most nearly intelligent of the\nnimble youths, seemed to like him; and it was enormously important to\nwin over the surly older man, who proved to be a railway clerk named\nFulton Bemis.\n\nThe conversation of the Bunch was exclamatory, high-colored, full of\nreferences to people whom Babbitt did not know. Apparently they thought\nvery comfortably of themselves. They were the Bunch, wise and beautiful\nand amusing; they were Bohemians and urbanites, accustomed to all the\nluxuries of Zenith: dance-halls, movie-theaters, and roadhouses; and in\na cynical superiority to people who were \xe2\x80\x9cslow\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9ctightwad\xe2\x80\x9d they\ncackled:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Pete, did I tell you what that dub of a cashier said when I came in\nlate yesterday? Oh, it was per-fect-ly priceless!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, but wasn\xe2\x80\x99t T. D. stewed! Say, he was simply ossified! What did\nGladys say to him?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThink of the nerve of Bob Bickerstaff trying to get us to come to his\nhouse! Say, the nerve of him! Can you beat it for nerve? Some nerve I\ncall it!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you notice how Dotty was dancing? Gee, wasn\xe2\x80\x99t she the limit!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBabbitt was to be heard sonorously agreeing with the once-hated Miss\nMinnie Sonntag that persons who let a night go by without dancing to\njazz music were crabs, pikers, and poor fish; and he roared \xe2\x80\x9cYou bet!\xe2\x80\x9d\nwhen Mrs. Carrie Nork gurgled, \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you love to sit on the floor? It\xe2\x80\x99s\nso Bohemian!\xe2\x80\x9d He began to think extremely well of the Bunch. When he\nmentioned his friends Sir Gerald Doak, Lord Wycombe, William Washington\nEathorne, and Chum Frink, he was proud of their condescending interest.\nHe got so thoroughly into the jocund spirit that he didn\xe2\x80\x99t much mind\nseeing Tanis drooping against the shoulder of the youngest and milkiest\nof the young men, and he himself desired to hold Carrie Nork\xe2\x80\x99s pulpy\nhand, and dropped it only because Tanis looked angry.\n\nWhen he went home, at two, he was fully a member of the Bunch, and all\nthe week thereafter he was bound by the exceedingly straitened\nconventions, the exceedingly wearing demands, of their life of pleasure\nand freedom. He had to go to their parties; he was involved in the\nagitation when everybody telephoned to everybody else that she hadn\xe2\x80\x99t\nmeant what she\xe2\x80\x99d said when she\xe2\x80\x99d said that, and anyway, why was Pete\ngoing around saying she\xe2\x80\x99d said it?\n\nNever was a Family more insistent on learning one another\xe2\x80\x99s movements\nthan were the Bunch. All of them volubly knew, or indignantly desired to\nknow, where all the others had been every minute of the week. Babbitt\nfound himself explaining to Carrie or Fulton Bemis just what he had been\ndoing that he should not have joined them till ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock, and\napologizing for having gone to dinner with a business acquaintance.\n\nEvery member of the Bunch was expected to telephone to every other\nmember at least once a week. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy haven\xe2\x80\x99t you called me up?\xe2\x80\x9d Babbitt was\nasked accusingly, not only by Tanis and Carrie but presently by new\nancient friends, Jennie and Capitolina and Toots.\n\nIf for a moment he had seen Tanis as withering and sentimental, he lost\nthat impression at Carrie Nork\xe2\x80\x99s dance. Mrs. Nork had a large house and\na small husband. To her party came all of the Bunch, perhaps thirty-five\nof them when they were completely mobilized. Babbitt, under the name of\n\xe2\x80\x9cOld Georgie,\xe2\x80\x9d was now a pioneer of the Bunch, since each month it\nchanged half its membership and he who could recall the prehistoric days\nof a fortnight ago, before Mrs. Absolom, the food-demonstrator, had gone\nto Indianapolis, and Mac had \xe2\x80\x9cgot sore at\xe2\x80\x9d Minnie, was a venerable\nleader and able to condescend to new Petes and Minnies and Gladyses.\n\nAt Carrie\xe2\x80\x99s, Tanis did not have to work at being hostess. She was\ndignified and sure, a clear fine figure in the black chiffon frock he\nhad always loved; and in the wider spaces of that ugly house Babbitt was\nable to sit quietly with her. He repented of his first revulsion, mooned\nat her feet, and happily drove her home. Next day he bought a violent\nyellow tie, to make himself young for her. He knew, a little sadly, that\nhe could not make himself beautiful; he beheld himself as heavy, hinting\nof fatness, but he danced, he dressed, he chattered, to be as young as\nshe was... as young as she seemed to be.\n\n\nIV\n\nAs all converts, whether to a religion, love, or gardening, find as by\nmagic that though hitherto these hobbies have not seemed to exist, now\nthe whole world is filled with their fury, so, once he was converted to\ndissipation, Babbitt discovered agreeable opportunities for it\neverywhere.\n\nHe had a new view of his sporting neighbor, Sam Doppelbrau. The\nDoppelbraus were respectable people, industrious people, prosperous\npeople, whose ideal of happiness was an eternal cabaret. Their life was\ndominated by suburban bacchanalia of alcohol, nicotine, gasoline, and\nkisses. They and their set worked capably all the week, and all week\nlooked forward to Saturday night, when they would, as they expressed it,\n\xe2\x80\x9cthrow a party;\xe2\x80\x9d and the thrown party grew noisier and noisier up to\nSunday dawn, and usually included an extremely rapid motor expedition to\nnowhere in particular.\n\nOne evening when Tanis was at the theater, Babbitt found himself being\nlively with the Doppelbraus, pledging friendship with men whom he had\nfor years privily denounced to Mrs. Babbitt as a \xe2\x80\x9crotten bunch of\ntin-horns that I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t go out with, not if they were the last people\non earth.\xe2\x80\x9d That evening he had sulkily come home and poked about in\nfront of the house, chipping off the walk the ice-clots, like fossil\nfootprints, made by the steps of passers-by during the recent snow.\nHoward Littlefield came up snuffling.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cStill a widower, George?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYump. Cold again to-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you hear from the wife?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s feeling fine, but her sister is still pretty sick.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay, better come in and have dinner with us to-night, George.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh--oh, thanks. Have to go out.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nSuddenly he could not endure Littlefield\xe2\x80\x99s recitals of the more\ninteresting statistics about totally uninteresting problems. He scraped\nat the walk and grunted.\n\nSam Doppelbrau appeared.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cEvenin\xe2\x80\x99, Babbitt. Working hard?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYuh, lil exercise.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cCold enough for you to-night?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, just about.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cStill a widower?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cUh-huh.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay, Babbitt, while she\xe2\x80\x99s away-- I know you don\xe2\x80\x99t care much for\nbooze-fights, but the Missus and I\xe2\x80\x99d be awfully glad if you could come\nin some night. Think you could stand a good cocktail for once?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cStand it? Young fella, I bet old Uncle George can mix the best cocktail\nin these United States!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHurray! That\xe2\x80\x99s the way to talk! Look here: There\xe2\x80\x99s some folks coming to\nthe house to-night, Louetta Swanson and some other live ones, and I\xe2\x80\x99m\ngoing to open up a bottle of pre-war gin, and maybe we\xe2\x80\x99ll dance a while.\nWhy don\xe2\x80\x99t you drop in and jazz it up a little, just for a change?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell-- What time they coming?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe was at Sam Doppelbrau\xe2\x80\x99s at nine. It was the third time he had entered\nthe house. By ten he was calling Mr. Doppelbrau \xe2\x80\x9cSam, old hoss.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAt eleven they all drove out to the Old Farm Inn. Babbitt sat in the\nback of Doppelbrau\xe2\x80\x99s car with Louetta Swanson. Once he had timorously\ntried to make love to her. Now he did not try; he merely made love; and\nLouetta dropped her head on his shoulder, told him what a nagger Eddie\nwas, and accepted Babbitt as a decent and well-trained libertine.\n\nWith the assistance of Tanis\xe2\x80\x99s Bunch, the Doppelbraus, and other\ncompanions in forgetfulness, there was not an evening for two weeks when\nhe did not return home late and shaky. With his other faculties blurred\nhe yet had the motorist\xe2\x80\x99s gift of being able to drive when he could\nscarce walk; of slowing down at corners and allowing for approaching\ncars. He came wambling into the house. If Verona and Kenneth Escott were\nabout, he got past them with a hasty greeting, horribly aware of their\nlevel young glances, and hid himself up-stairs. He found when he came\ninto the warm house that he was hazier than he had believed. His head\nwhirled. He dared not lie down. He tried to soak out the alcohol in a\nhot bath. For the moment his head was clearer but when he moved about\nthe bathroom his calculations of distance were wrong, so that he dragged\ndown the towels, and knocked over the soap-dish with a clatter which, he\nfeared, would betray him to the children. Chilly in his dressing-gown he\ntried to read the evening paper. He could follow every word; he seemed\nto take in the sense of things; but a minute afterward he could not have\ntold what he had been reading. When he went to bed his brain flew in\ncircles, and he hastily sat up, struggling for self-control. At last he\nwas able to lie still, feeling only a little sick and dizzy--and\nenormously ashamed. To hide his \xe2\x80\x9ccondition\xe2\x80\x9d from his own children! To\nhave danced and shouted with people whom he despised! To have said\nfoolish things, sung idiotic songs, tried to kiss silly girls!\nIncredulously he remembered that he had by his roaring familiarity with\nthem laid himself open to the patronizing of youths whom he would have\nkicked out of his office; that by dancing too ardently he had exposed\nhimself to rebukes from the rattiest of withering women. As it came\nrelentlessly back to him he snarled, \xe2\x80\x9cI hate myself! God how I hate\nmyself!\xe2\x80\x9d But, he raged, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m through! No more! Had enough, plenty!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe was even surer about it the morning after, when he was trying to be\ngrave and paternal with his daughters at breakfast. At noontime he was\nless sure. He did not deny that he had been a fool; he saw it almost as\nclearly as at midnight; but anything, he struggled, was better than\ngoing back to a life of barren heartiness. At four he wanted a drink. He\nkept a whisky flask in his desk now, and after two minutes of battle he\nhad his drink. Three drinks later he began to see the Bunch as tender\nand amusing friends, and by six he was with them... and the tale was to\nbe told all over.\n\nEach morning his head ached a little less. A bad head for drinks had\nbeen his safeguard, but the safeguard was crumbling. Presently he could\nbe drunk at dawn, yet not feel particularly wretched in his\nconscience--or in his stomach--when he awoke at eight. No regret, no\ndesire to escape the toil of keeping up with the arduous merriment of\nthe Bunch, was so great as his feeling of social inferiority when he\nfailed to keep up. To be the \xe2\x80\x9clivest\xe2\x80\x9d of them was as much his ambition\nnow as it had been to excel at making money, at playing golf, at\nmotor-driving, at oratory, at climbing to the McKelvey set. But\noccasionally he failed.\n\nHe found that Pete and the other young men considered the Bunch too\nausterely polite and the Carrie who merely kissed behind doors too\nembarrassingly monogamic. As Babbitt sneaked from Floral Heights down to\nthe Bunch, so the young gallants sneaked from the proprieties of the\nBunch off to \xe2\x80\x9ctimes\xe2\x80\x9d with bouncing young women whom they picked up in\ndepartment stores and at hotel coatrooms. Once Babbitt tried to\naccompany them. There was a motor car, a bottle of whisky, and for him a\ngrubby shrieking cash-girl from Parcher and Stein\xe2\x80\x99s. He sat beside her\nand worried. He was apparently expected to \xe2\x80\x9cjolly her along,\xe2\x80\x9d but when\nshe sang out, \xe2\x80\x9cHey, leggo, quit crushing me cootie-garage,\xe2\x80\x9d he did not\nquite know how to go on. They sat in the back room of a saloon, and\nBabbitt had a headache, was confused by their new slang, looked at them\nbenevolently, wanted to go home, and had a drink--a good many drinks.\n\nTwo evenings after, Fulton Bemis, the surly older man of the Bunch, took\nBabbitt aside and grunted, \xe2\x80\x9cLook here, it\xe2\x80\x99s none of my business, and God\nknows I always lap up my share of the hootch, but don\xe2\x80\x99t you think you\nbetter watch yourself? You\xe2\x80\x99re one of these enthusiastic chumps that\nalways overdo things. D\xe2\x80\x99you realize you\xe2\x80\x99re throwing in the booze as fast\nas you can, and you eat one cigarette right after another? Better cut it\nout for a while.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBabbitt tearfully said that good old Fult was a prince, and yes, he\ncertainly would cut it out, and thereafter he lighted a cigarette and\ntook a drink and had a terrific quarrel with Tanis when she caught him\nbeing affectionate with Carrie Nork.\n\nNext morning he hated himself that he should have sunk into a position\nwhere a fifteenth-rater like Fulton Bemis could rebuke him. He perceived\nthat, since he was making love to every woman possible, Tanis was no\nlonger his one pure star, and he wondered whether she had ever been\nanything more to him than A Woman. And if Bemis had spoken to him, were\nother people talking about him? He suspiciously watched the men at the\nAthletic Club that noon. It seemed to him that they were uneasy. They\nhad been talking about him then? He was angry. He became belligerent. He\nnot only defended Seneca Doane but even made fun of the Y. M. C. A.\nVergil Gunch was rather brief in his answers.\n\nAfterward Babbitt was not angry. He was afraid. He did not go to the\nnext lunch of the Boosters\xe2\x80\x99 Club but hid in a cheap restaurant, and,\nwhile he munched a ham-and-egg sandwich and sipped coffee from a cup on\nthe arm of his chair, he worried.\n\nFour days later, when the Bunch were having one of their best parties,\nBabbitt drove them to the skating-rink which had been laid out on the\nChaloosa River. After a thaw the streets had frozen in smooth ice. Down\nthose wide endless streets the wind rattled between the rows of wooden\nhouses, and the whole Bellevue district seemed a frontier town. Even\nwith skid chains on all four wheels, Babbitt was afraid of sliding, and\nwhen he came to the long slide of a hill he crawled down, both brakes\non. Slewing round a corner came a less cautious car. It skidded, it\nalmost raked them with its rear fenders. In relief at their escape the\nBunch--Tanis, Minnie Sonntag, Pete, Fulton Bemis--shouted \xe2\x80\x9cOh, baby,\xe2\x80\x9d\nand waved their hands to the agitated other driver. Then Babbitt saw\nProfessor Pumphrey laboriously crawling up hill, afoot, staring owlishly\nat the revelers. He was sure that Pumphrey recognized him and saw Tanis\nkiss him as she crowed, \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re such a good driver!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAt lunch next day he probed Pumphrey with \xe2\x80\x9cOut last night with my\nbrother and some friends of his. Gosh, what driving! Slippery\xe2\x80\x99s glass.\nThought I saw you hiking up the Bellevue Avenue Hill.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t-- I didn\xe2\x80\x99t see you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Pumphrey, hastily, rather\nguiltily.\n\nPerhaps two days afterward Babbitt took Tanis to lunch at the Hotel\nThornleigh. She who had seemed well content to wait for him at her flat\nhad begun to hint with melancholy smiles that he must think but little\nof her if he never introduced her to his friends, if he was unwilling to\nbe seen with her except at the movies. He thought of taking her to the\n\xe2\x80\x9cladies\xe2\x80\x99 annex\xe2\x80\x9d of the Athletic Club, but that was too dangerous. He\nwould have to introduce her and, oh, people might misunderstand and-- He\ncompromised on the Thornleigh.\n\nShe was unusually smart, all in black: small black tricorne hat, short\nblack caracul coat, loose and swinging, and austere high-necked black\nvelvet frock at a time when most street costumes were like evening\ngowns. Perhaps she was too smart. Every one in the gold and oak\nrestaurant of the Thornleigh was staring at her as Babbitt followed her\nto a table. He uneasily hoped that the head-waiter would give them a\ndiscreet place behind a pillar, but they were stationed on the center\naisle. Tanis seemed not to notice her admirers; she smiled at Babbitt\nwith a lavish \xe2\x80\x9cOh, isn\xe2\x80\x99t this nice! What a peppy-looking orchestra!\xe2\x80\x9d\nBabbitt had difficulty in being lavish in return, for two tables away he\nsaw Vergil Gunch. All through the meal Gunch watched them, while Babbitt\nwatched himself being watched and lugubriously tried to keep from\nspoiling Tanis\xe2\x80\x99s gaiety. \xe2\x80\x9cI felt like a spree to-day,\xe2\x80\x9d she rippled. \xe2\x80\x9cI\nlove the Thornleigh, don\xe2\x80\x99t you? It\xe2\x80\x99s so live and yet so--so refined.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe made talk about the Thornleigh, the service, the food, the people he\nrecognized in the restaurant, all but Vergil Gunch. There did not seem\nto be anything else to talk of. He smiled conscientiously at her\nfluttering jests; he agreed with her that Minnie Sonntag was \xe2\x80\x9cso hard to\nget along with,\xe2\x80\x9d and young Pete \xe2\x80\x9csuch a silly lazy kid, really just no\ngood at all.\xe2\x80\x9d But he himself had nothing to say. He considered telling\nher his worries about Gunch, but--\xe2\x80\x9coh, gosh, it was too much work to go\ninto the whole thing and explain about Verg and everything.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe was relieved when he put Tanis on a trolley; he was cheerful in the\nfamiliar simplicities of his office.\n\nAt four o\xe2\x80\x99clock Vergil Gunch called on him.\n\nBabbitt was agitated, but Gunch began in a friendly way:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow\xe2\x80\x99s the boy? Say, some of us are getting up a scheme we\xe2\x80\x99d kind of\nlike to have you come in on.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cFine, Verg. Shoot.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know during the war we had the Undesirable Element, the Reds and\nwalking delegates and just the plain common grouches, dead to rights,\nand so did we for quite a while after the war, but folks forget about\nthe danger and that gives these cranks a chance to begin working\nunderground again, especially a lot of these parlor socialists. Well,\nit\xe2\x80\x99s up to the folks that do a little sound thinking to make a conscious\neffort to keep bucking these fellows. Some guy back East has organized a\nsociety called the Good Citizens\xe2\x80\x99 League for just that purpose. Of\ncourse the Chamber of Commerce and the American Legion and so on do a\nfine work in keeping the decent people in the saddle, but they\xe2\x80\x99re\ndevoted to so many other causes that they can\xe2\x80\x99t attend to this one\nproblem properly. But the Good Citizens\xe2\x80\x99 League, the G. C. L., they\nstick right to it. Oh, the G. C. L. has to have some other ostensible\npurposes--frinstance here in Zenith I think it ought to support the\npark-extension project and the City Planning Committee--and then, too,\nit should have a social aspect, being made up of the best people--have\ndances and so on, especially as one of the best ways it can put the\nkibosh on cranks is to apply this social boycott business to folks big\nenough so you can\xe2\x80\x99t reach \xe2\x80\x99em otherwise. Then if that don\xe2\x80\x99t work, the G.\nC. L. can finally send a little delegation around to inform folks that\nget too flip that they got to conform to decent standards and quit\nshooting off their mouths so free. Don\xe2\x80\x99t it sound like the organization\ncould do a great work? We\xe2\x80\x99ve already got some of the strongest men in\ntown, and of course we want you in. How about it?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBabbitt was uncomfortable. He felt a compulsion back to all the\nstandards he had so vaguely yet so desperately been fleeing. He fumbled:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you\xe2\x80\x99d especially light on fellows like Seneca Doane and try\nto make \xe2\x80\x99em--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou bet your sweet life we would! Look here, old Georgie: I\xe2\x80\x99ve never\nfor one moment believed you meant it when you\xe2\x80\x99ve defended Doane, and the\nstrikers and so on, at the Club. I knew you were simply kidding those\npoor galoots like Sid Finkelstein.... At least I certainly hope you were\nkidding!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, well--sure-- Course you might say--\xe2\x80\x9d Babbitt was conscious of how\nfeeble he sounded, conscious of Gunch\xe2\x80\x99s mature and relentless eye.\n\xe2\x80\x9cGosh, you know where I stand! I\xe2\x80\x99m no labor agitator! I\xe2\x80\x99m a business\nman, first, last, and all the time! But--but honestly, I don\xe2\x80\x99t think\nDoane means so badly, and you got to remember he\xe2\x80\x99s an old friend of\nmine.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGeorge, when it comes right down to a struggle between decency and the\nsecurity of our homes on the one hand, and red ruin and those lazy dogs\nplotting for free beer on the other, you got to give up even old\nfriendships. \xe2\x80\x98He that is not with me is against me.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYe-es, I suppose--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow about it? Going to join us in the Good Citizens\xe2\x80\x99 League?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll have to think it over, Verg.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, just as you say.\xe2\x80\x9d Babbitt was relieved to be let off so\neasily, but Gunch went on: \xe2\x80\x9cGeorge, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what\xe2\x80\x99s come over you;\nnone of us do; and we\xe2\x80\x99ve talked a lot about you. For a while we figured\nout you\xe2\x80\x99d been upset by what happened to poor Riesling, and we forgave\nyou for any fool things you said, but that\xe2\x80\x99s old stuff now, George, and\nwe can\xe2\x80\x99t make out what\xe2\x80\x99s got into you. Personally, I\xe2\x80\x99ve always defended\nyou, but I must say it\xe2\x80\x99s getting too much for me. All the boys at the\nAthletic Club and the Boosters\xe2\x80\x99 are sore, the way you go on deliberately\ntouting Doane and his bunch of hell-hounds, and talking about being\nliberal--which means being wishy-washy--and even saying this preacher\nguy Ingram isn\xe2\x80\x99t a professional free-love artist. And then the way you\nbeen carrying on personally! Joe Pumphrey says he saw you out the other\nnight with a gang of totties, all stewed to the gills, and here to-day\ncoming right into the Thornleigh with a--well, she may be all right and\na perfect lady, but she certainly did look like a pretty gay skirt for a\nfellow with his wife out of town to be taking to lunch. Didn\xe2\x80\x99t look\nwell. What the devil has come over you, George?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cStrikes me there\xe2\x80\x99s a lot of fellows that know more about my personal\nbusiness than I do myself!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow don\xe2\x80\x99t go getting sore at me because I come out flatfooted like a\nfriend and say what I think instead of tattling behind your back, the\nway a whole lot of \xe2\x80\x99em do. I tell you, George, you got a position in the\ncommunity, and the community expects you to live up to it. And-- Better\nthink over joining the Good Citizens\xe2\x80\x99 League. See you about it later.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe was gone.\n\nThat evening Babbitt dined alone. He saw all the Clan of Good Fellows\npeering through the restaurant window, spying on him. Fear sat beside\nhim, and he told himself that to-night he would not go to Tanis\xe2\x80\x99s flat;\nand he did not go... till late.\n\n\n\n\nCHAPTER XXX\n\n\nI\n\nThe summer before, Mrs. Babbitt\xe2\x80\x99s letters had crackled with desire to\nreturn to Zenith. Now they said nothing of returning, but a wistful \xe2\x80\x9cI\nsuppose everything is going on all right without me\xe2\x80\x9d among her dry\nchronicles of weather and sicknesses hinted to Babbitt that he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t\nbeen very urgent about her coming. He worried it:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf she were here, and I went on raising cain like I been doing, she\xe2\x80\x99d\nhave a fit. I got to get hold of myself. I got to learn to play around\nand yet not make a fool of myself. I can do it, too, if folks like Verg\nGunch\xe2\x80\x99ll let me alone, and Myra\xe2\x80\x99ll stay away. But--poor kid, she sounds\nlonely. Lord, I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to hurt her!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nImpulsively he wrote that they missed her, and her next letter said\nhappily that she was coming home.\n\nHe persuaded himself that he was eager to see her. He bought roses for\nthe house, he ordered squab for dinner, he had the car cleaned and\npolished. All the way home from the station with her he was adequate in\nhis accounts of Ted\xe2\x80\x99s success in basket-ball at the university, but\nbefore they reached Floral Heights there was nothing more to say, and\nalready he felt the force of her stolidity, wondered whether he could\nremain a good husband and still sneak out of the house this evening for\nhalf an hour with the Bunch. When he had housed the car he blundered\nupstairs, into the familiar talcum-scented warmth of her presence,\nblaring, \xe2\x80\x9cHelp you unpack your bag?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I can do it.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nSlowly she turned, holding up a small box, and slowly she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI\nbrought you a present, just a new cigar-case. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know if you\xe2\x80\x99d care\nto have it--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nShe was the lonely girl, the brown appealing Myra Thompson, whom he had\nmarried, and he almost wept for pity as he kissed her and besought, \xe2\x80\x9cOh,\nhoney, honey, _care_ to have it? Of course I do! I\xe2\x80\x99m awful proud you\nbrought it to me. And I needed a new case badly.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe wondered how he would get rid of the case he had bought the week\nbefore.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you really are glad to see me back?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you poor kiddy, what you been worrying about?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you didn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to miss me very much.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBy the time he had finished his stint of lying they were firmly bound\nagain. By ten that evening it seemed improbable that she had ever been\naway. There was but one difference: the problem of remaining a\nrespectable husband, a Floral Heights husband, yet seeing Tanis and the\nBunch with frequency. He had promised to telephone to Tanis that\nevening, and now it was melodramatically impossible. He prowled about\nthe telephone, impulsively thrusting out a hand to lift the receiver,\nbut never quite daring to risk it. Nor could he find a reason for\nslipping down to the drug store on Smith Street, with its\ntelephone-booth. He was laden with responsibility till he threw it off\nwith the speculation: \xe2\x80\x9cWhy the deuce should I fret so about not being\nable to \xe2\x80\x99phone Tanis? She can get along without me. I don\xe2\x80\x99t owe her\nanything. She\xe2\x80\x99s a fine girl, but I\xe2\x80\x99ve given her just as much as she has\nme.... Oh, damn these women and the way they get you all tied up in\ncomplications!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\nII\n\nFor a week he was attentive to his wife, took her to the theater, to\ndinner at the Littlefields\xe2\x80\x99; then the old weary dodging and shifting\nbegan, and at least two evenings a week he spent with the Bunch. He\nstill made pretense of going to the Elks and to committee-meetings but\nless and less did he trouble to have his excuses interesting, less and\nless did she affect to believe them. He was certain that she knew he was\nassociating with what Floral Heights called \xe2\x80\x9ca sporty crowd,\xe2\x80\x9d yet\nneither of them acknowledged it. In matrimonial geography the distance\nbetween the first mute recognition of a break and the admission thereof\nis as great as the distance between the first na\xc3\xafve faith and the first\ndoubting.\n\nAs he began to drift away he also began to see her as a human being, to\nlike and dislike her instead of accepting her as a comparatively movable\npart of the furniture, and he compassionated that husband-and-wife\nrelation which, in twenty-five years of married life, had become a\nseparate and real entity. He recalled their high lights: the summer\nvacation in Virginia meadows under the blue wall of the mountains; their\nmotor tour through Ohio, and the exploration of Cleveland, Cincinnati,\nand Columbus; the birth of Verona; their building of this new house,\nplanned to comfort them through a happy old age--chokingly they had said\nthat it might be the last home either of them would ever have. Yet his\nmost softening remembrance of these dear moments did not keep him from\nbarking at dinner, \xe2\x80\x9cYep, going out f\xe2\x80\x99 few hours. Don\xe2\x80\x99t sit up for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe did not dare now to come home drunk, and though he rejoiced in his\nreturn to high morality and spoke with gravity to Pete and Fulton Bemis\nabout their drinking, he prickled at Myra\xe2\x80\x99s unexpressed criticisms and\nsulkily meditated that a \xe2\x80\x9cfellow couldn\xe2\x80\x99t ever learn to handle himself\nif he was always bossed by a lot of women.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe no longer wondered if Tanis wasn\xe2\x80\x99t a bit worn and sentimental. In\ncontrast to the complacent Myra he saw her as swift and air-borne and\nradiant, a fire-spirit tenderly stooping to the hearth, and however\npitifully he brooded on his wife, he longed to be with Tanis.\n\nThen Mrs. Babbitt tore the decent cloak from her unhappiness and the\nastounded male discovered that she was having a small determined\nrebellion of her own.\n\n\nIII\n\nThey were beside the fireless fire-place, in the evening.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGeorgie,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cyou haven\xe2\x80\x99t given me the list of your household\nexpenses while I was away.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I-- Haven\xe2\x80\x99t made it out yet.\xe2\x80\x9d Very affably: \xe2\x80\x9cGosh, we must try to\nkeep down expenses this year.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s so. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know where all the money goes to. I try to\neconomize, but it just seems to evaporate.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose I oughtn\xe2\x80\x99t to spend so much on cigars. Don\xe2\x80\x99t know but what\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll cut down my smoking, maybe cut it out entirely. I was thinking of a\ngood way to do it, the other day: start on these cubeb cigarettes, and\nthey\xe2\x80\x99d kind of disgust me with smoking.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I do wish you would! They always exaggerate so.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGeorge, I don\xe2\x80\x99t think you ought to talk that way when I\xe2\x80\x99m just speaking\nfor your own good.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know, but gosh all fishhooks, that\xe2\x80\x99s the trouble with women! Just talking over the same\nold things with the same old crowd, while you have all sorts of\ninteresting people coming in to see you at the office.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cInteresting! Cranky old dames that want to know why I haven\xe2\x80\x99t rented\ntheir dear precious homes for about seven times their value, and bunch\nof old crabs panning the everlasting daylights out of me because they\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t receive every cent of their rentals by three G.M. on the second of\nthe month! Sure! Interesting! Just as interesting as the small pox!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, George, I will not have you shouting at me that way!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it gets my goat the way women figure out that a man doesn\xe2\x80\x99t do a\ndarn thing but sit on his chair and have lovey-dovey conferences with a\nlot of classy dames and give \xe2\x80\x99em the glad eye!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI guess you manage to give them a glad enough eye when they do come\nin.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean? Fixes the\nfurnace when the furnace-man doesn\xe2\x80\x99t show up, and pays the bills, but\ndull, awful dull! They think he\xe2\x80\x99s\nnot so bad-looking, not so bad that it hurts anyway, and he\xe2\x80\x99s got a\npretty good line of guff, and some even think he shakes a darn wicked\nWalkover at dancing!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d She spoke slowly. \xe2\x80\x9cI haven\xe2\x80\x99t much doubt that when I\xe2\x80\x99m away you\nmanage to find people who properly appreciate you.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I just mean--\xe2\x80\x9d he protested, with a sound of denial. Then he was\nangered into semi-honesty. \xe2\x80\x9cYou bet I do! I find plenty of folks, and\ndoggone nice ones, that don\xe2\x80\x99t think I\xe2\x80\x99m a weak-stomached baby!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s exactly what I was saying! You can run around with anybody you\nplease, but I\xe2\x80\x99m supposed to sit here and wait for you. I\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know what\xe2\x80\x99s come over you. Will you help me?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSure. Anything I can do to help you in the culture-grabbing line--yours\nto oblige, G. F. Babbitt.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well then, I want you to go to Mrs. Mudge\xe2\x80\x99s New Thought meeting\nwith me, next Sunday afternoon.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Who\xe2\x80\x99s which?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Opal Emerson Mudge. The field-lecturer for the American New\nThought League. She\xe2\x80\x99s going to speak on \xe2\x80\x98Cultivating the Sun Spirit\xe2\x80\x99\nbefore the League of the Higher Illumination, at the Thornleigh.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, punk! New Thought! Hashed thought with a poached egg! \xe2\x80\x98Cultivating\nthe--\xe2\x80\x99 It sounds like \xe2\x80\x98Why is a mouse when it spins?\xe2\x80\x99 That\xe2\x80\x99s a fine\nspiel for a good Presbyterian to be going to, when you can hear Doc\nDrew!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cReverend Drew is a scholar and a pulpit orator and all that, but he\nhasn\xe2\x80\x99t got the Inner Ferment, as Mrs. Mudge calls it; he hasn\xe2\x80\x99t any\ninspiration for the New Era. Women need inspiration now. So I want you\nto come, as you promised.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\nIV\n\nThe Zenith branch of the League of the Higher Illumination met in the\nsmaller ballroom at the Hotel Thornleigh, a refined apartment with pale\ngreen walls and plaster wreaths of roses, refined parquet flooring, and\nultra-refined frail gilt chairs. Here were gathered sixty-five women and\nten men. Most of the men slouched in their chairs and wriggled, while\ntheir wives sat rigidly at attention, but two of them--red-necked, meaty\nmen--were as respectably devout as their wives. They were newly rich\ncontractors who, having bought houses, motors, hand-painted pictures,\nand gentlemanliness, were now buying a refined ready-made philosophy. It\nhad been a toss-up with them whether to buy New Thought, Christian\nScience, or a good standard high-church model of Episcopalianism.\n\nIn the flesh, Mrs. Opal Emerson Mudge fell somewhat short of a prophetic\naspect. She was pony-built and plump, with the face of a haughty\nPekingese, a button of a nose, and arms so short that, despite her most\nindignant endeavors, she could not clasp her hands in front of her as\nshe sat on the platform waiting. Her frock of taffeta and green velvet,\nwith three strings of glass beads, and large folding eye-glasses\ndangling from a black ribbon, was a triumph of refinement.\n\nMrs. Mudge was introduced by the president of the League of the Higher\nIllumination, an oldish young woman with a yearning voice, white spats,\nand a mustache. She said that Mrs. Mudge would now make it plain to the\nsimplest intellect how the Sun Spirit could be cultivated, and they who\nhad been thinking about cultivating one would do well to treasure Mrs.\nMudge\xe2\x80\x99s words, because even Zenith (and everybody knew that Zenith stood\nin the van of spiritual and New Thought progress) didn\xe2\x80\x99t often have the\nopportunity to sit at the feet of such an inspiring Optimist and\nMetaphysical Seer as Mrs. Opal Emerson Mudge, who had lived the Life of\nWider Usefulness through Concentration, and in the Silence found those\nSecrets of Mental Control and the Inner Key which were immediately going\nto transform and bring Peace, Power, and Prosperity to the unhappy\nnations; and so, friends, would they for this precious gem-studded hour\nforget the Illusions of the Seeming Real, and in the actualization of\nthe deep-lying Veritas pass, along with Mrs. Opal Emerson Mudge, to the\nRealm Beautiful.\n\nIf Mrs. Mudge was rather pudgier than one would like one\xe2\x80\x99s swamis,\nyogis, seers, and initiates, yet her voice had the real professional\nnote. It was refined and optimistic; it was overpoweringly calm; it\nflowed on relentlessly, without one comma, till Babbitt was hypnotized.\nHer favorite word was \xe2\x80\x9calways,\xe2\x80\x9d which she pronounced olllllle-ways. Her\nprincipal gesture was a pontifical but thoroughly ladylike blessing with\ntwo stubby fingers.\n\nShe explained about this matter of Spiritual Saturation:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are those--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nOf \xe2\x80\x9cthose\xe2\x80\x9d she made a linked sweetness long drawn out; a far-off\ndelicate call in a twilight minor. It chastely rebuked the restless\nhusbands, yet brought them a message of healing.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are those who have seen the rim and outer seeming of the Logos\nthere are those who have glimpsed and in enthusiasm possessed themselves\nof some segment and portion of the Logos there are those who thus\nflicked but not penetrated and radioactivated by the Dynamis go always\nto and fro assertative that they possess and are possessed of the Logos\nand the Metaphysikos but this word I bring you this concept I enlarge\nthat those that are not utter are not even inceptive and that holiness\nis in its definitive essence always always always whole-iness and--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nIt proved that the Essence of the Sun Spirit was Truth, but its Aura and\nEffluxion were Cheerfulness:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cFace always the day with the dawn-laugh with the enthusiasm of the\ninitiate who perceives that all works together in the revolutions of the\nWheel and who answers the strictures of the Soured Souls of the\nDestructionists with a Glad Affirmation--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nIt went on for about an hour and seven minutes.\n\nAt the end Mrs. Mudge spoke with more vigor and punctuation:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow let me suggest to all of you the advantages of the Theosophical and\nPantheistic Oriental Reading Circle, which I represent. Our object is to\nunite all the manifestations of the New Era into one cohesive whole--New\nThought, Christian Science, Theosophy, Vedanta, Bahaism, and the other\nsparks from the one New Light. The subscription is but ten dollars a\nyear, and for this mere pittance the members receive not only the\nmonthly magazine, _Pearls of Healing_, but the privilege of sending\nright to the president, our revered Mother Dobbs, any questions\nregarding spiritual progress, matrimonial problems, health and\nwell-being questions, financial difficulties, and--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThey listened to her with adoring attention. They looked genteel. They\nlooked ironed-out. They coughed politely, and crossed their legs with\nquietness, and in expensive linen handkerchiefs they blew their noses\nwith a delicacy altogether optimistic and refined.\n\nAs for Babbitt, he sat and suffered.\n\nWhen they were blessedly out in the air again, when they drove home\nthrough a wind smelling of snow and honest sun, he dared not speak. They\nhad been too near to quarreling, these days. Mrs. Babbitt forced it:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you enjoy Mrs. Mudge\xe2\x80\x99s talk?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell I-- What did you get out of it?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it starts a person thinking. It gets you out of a routine of\nordinary thoughts.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99ll hand it to Opal she isn\xe2\x80\x99t ordinary, but gosh-- Honest, did\nthat stuff mean anything to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course I\xe2\x80\x99m not trained in metaphysics, and there was lots I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\nquite grasp, but I did feel it was inspiring. And she speaks so readily.\nI do think you ought to have got something out of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t! I swear, I was simply astonished, the way those women\nlapped it up! Why the dickens they want to put in their time listening\nto all that blaa when they--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s certainly better for them than going to roadhouses and smoking and\ndrinking!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know whether it is or not! Personally I don\xe2\x80\x99t see a whole lot\nof difference. In both cases they\xe2\x80\x99re trying to get away from\nthemselves--most everybody is, these days, I guess. And I\xe2\x80\x99d certainly\nget a whole lot more out of hoofing it in a good lively dance, even in\nsome dive, than sitting looking as if my collar was too tight, and\nfeeling too scared to spit, and listening to Opal chewing her words.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure you do! You\xe2\x80\x99re very fond of dives. No doubt you saw a lot of\nthem while I was away!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here! With amazement he found that he wasn\xe2\x80\x99t particularly\nsorry. It was only with an effort that he made himself more agreeable:\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, gosh, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean to get sore.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGeorge, do you realize that we can\xe2\x80\x99t go on like this, getting farther\nand farther apart, and you ruder and ruder to me? I just don\xe2\x80\x99t know\nwhat\xe2\x80\x99s going to happen.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe had a moment\xe2\x80\x99s pity for her bewilderment; he thought of how many deep\nand tender things would be hurt if they really \xe2\x80\x9ccouldn\xe2\x80\x99t go on like\nthis.\xe2\x80\x9d But his pity was impersonal, and he was wondering, \xe2\x80\x9cWouldn\xe2\x80\x99t it\nmaybe be a good thing if-- Not a divorce and all that, o\xe2\x80\x99 course, but\nkind of a little more independence?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nWhile she looked at him pleadingly he drove on in a dreadful silence.\n\n\n\n\nCHAPTER XXXI\n\n\nI\n\nWhen he was away from her, while he kicked about the garage and swept\nthe snow off the running-board and examined a cracked hose-connection,\nhe repented, he was alarmed and astonished that he could have flared out\nat his wife, and thought fondly how much more lasting she was than the\nflighty Bunch. He went in to mumble that he was \xe2\x80\x9csorry, didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean to\nbe grouchy,\xe2\x80\x9d and to inquire as to her interest in movies. But in the\ndarkness of the movie theater he brooded that he\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x9cgone and tied\nhimself up to Myra all over again.\xe2\x80\x9d He had some satisfaction in taking\nit out on Tanis Judique. \xe2\x80\x9cHang Tanis anyway! Why\xe2\x80\x99d she gone and got him\ninto these mix-ups and made him all jumpy and nervous and cranky? Too\nmany complications! Cut \xe2\x80\x99em out!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe wanted peace. For ten days he did not see Tanis nor telephone to her,\nand instantly she put upon him the compulsion which he hated. When he\nhad stayed away from her for five days, hourly taking pride in his\nresoluteness and hourly picturing how greatly Tanis must miss him, Miss\nMcGoun reported, \xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Judique on the \xe2\x80\x99phone. Like t\xe2\x80\x99 speak t\xe2\x80\x99 you \xe2\x80\x99bout\nsome repairs.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nTanis was quick and quiet:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Babbitt? Oh, George, this is Tanis. I haven\xe2\x80\x99t seen you for\nweeks--days, anyway. You aren\xe2\x80\x99t sick, are you?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, just been terribly rushed. I, uh, I think there\xe2\x80\x99ll be a big revival\nof building this year. Got to, uh, got to work hard.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course, my man! I want you to. You know I\xe2\x80\x99m terribly ambitious for\nyou; much more than I am for myself. I just don\xe2\x80\x99t want you to forget\npoor Tanis. Will you call me up soon?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSure! Sure! You bet!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease do. I sha\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t call you again.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe meditated, \xe2\x80\x9cPoor kid!... But gosh, she oughtn\xe2\x80\x99t to \xe2\x80\x99phone me at the\noffice.... She\xe2\x80\x99s a wonder--sympathy--\xe2\x80\x98ambitious for me.\xe2\x80\x99... But gosh, I\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t be made and compelled to call her up till I get ready. Darn these\nwomen, the way they make demands! It\xe2\x80\x99ll be one long old time before I\nsee her!... But gosh, I\xe2\x80\x99d like to see her to-night--sweet little\nthing.... Oh, cut that, son! Now you\xe2\x80\x99ve broken away, be wise!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nShe did not telephone again, nor he, but after five more days she wrote\nto him:\n\n     Have I offended you? You must know, dear, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean to. I\xe2\x80\x99m so\n     lonely and I need somebody to cheer me up. Why didn\xe2\x80\x99t you come to\n     the nice party we had at Carrie\xe2\x80\x99s last evening I remember she\n     invited you. Can\xe2\x80\x99t you come around here to-morrow Thur evening? I\n     shall be alone and hope to see you.\n\nHis reflections were numerous:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoggone it, why can\xe2\x80\x99t she let me alone? Why can\xe2\x80\x99t women ever learn a\nfellow hates to be bulldozed? And they always take advantage of you by\nyelling how lonely they are.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow that isn\xe2\x80\x99t nice of you, young fella. She\xe2\x80\x99s a fine, square, straight\ngirl, and she does get lonely. She writes a swell hand. Nice-looking\nstationery. Plain. Refined. I guess I\xe2\x80\x99ll have to go see her. Well, thank\nGod, I got till to-morrow night free of her, anyway.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s nice but-- Hang it, I won\xe2\x80\x99t be _made_ to do things! I\xe2\x80\x99m not\nmarried to her. No, nor by golly going to be!\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, rats, I suppose I better go see her.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\nII\n\nThursday, the to-morrow of Tanis\xe2\x80\x99s note, was full of emotional crises.\nAt the Roughnecks\xe2\x80\x99 Table at the club, Verg Gunch talked of the Good\nCitizens\xe2\x80\x99 League and (it seemed to Babbitt) deliberately left him out of\nthe invitations to join. Old Mat Penniman, the general utility man at\nBabbitt\xe2\x80\x99s office, had Troubles, and came in to groan about them: his\noldest boy was \xe2\x80\x9cno good,\xe2\x80\x9d his wife was sick, and he had quarreled with\nhis brother-in-law. Conrad Lyte also had Troubles, and since Lyte was\none of his best clients, Babbitt had to listen to them. Mr. Lyte, it\nappeared, was suffering from a peculiarly interesting neuralgia, and the\ngarage had overcharged him. When Babbitt came home, everybody had\nTroubles: his wife was simultaneously thinking about discharging the\nimpudent new maid, and worried lest the maid leave; and Tinka desired to\ndenounce her teacher.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, quit fussing!\xe2\x80\x9d Babbitt fussed. \xe2\x80\x9cYou never hear me whining about my\nTroubles, and yet if you had to run a real-estate office-- Why, to-day I\nfound Miss Bannigan was two days behind with her accounts, and I pinched\nmy finger in my desk, and Lyte was in and just as unreasonable as ever.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe was so vexed that after dinner, when it was time for a tactful escape\nto Tanis, he merely grumped to his wife, \xe2\x80\x9cGot to go out. Be back by\neleven, should think.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! You\xe2\x80\x99re going out again?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAgain! What do you mean \xe2\x80\x98again\xe2\x80\x99! Haven\xe2\x80\x99t hardly been out of the house\nfor a week!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you--are you going to the Elks?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNope. Got to see some people.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThough this time he heard his own voice and knew that it was curt,\nthough she was looking at him with wide-eyed reproach, he stumped into\nthe hall, jerked on his ulster and fur-lined gloves, and went out to\nstart the car.\n\nHe was relieved to find Tanis cheerful, unreproachful, and brilliant in\na frock of brown net over gold tissue. \xe2\x80\x9cYou poor man, having to come out\non a night like this! It\xe2\x80\x99s terribly cold. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you think a small\nhighball would be nice?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, by golly, there\xe2\x80\x99s a woman with savvy! I think we could more or\nless stand a highball if it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t too long a one--not over a foot\ntall!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe kissed her with careless heartiness, he forgot the compulsion of her\ndemands, he stretched in a large chair and felt that he had beautifully\ncome home. He was suddenly loquacious; he told her what a noble and\nmisunderstood man he was, and how superior to Pete, Fulton Bemis, and\nthe other men of their acquaintance; and she, bending forward, chin in\ncharming hand, brightly agreed. But when he forced himself to ask,\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, honey, how\xe2\x80\x99s things with _you_,\xe2\x80\x9d she took his duty-question\nseriously, and he discovered that she too had Troubles:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, all right but-- I did get so angry with Carrie. She told Minnie that\nI told her that Minnie was an awful tightwad, and Minnie told me Carrie\nhad told her, and of course I told her I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t said anything of the\nkind, and then Carrie found Minnie had told me, and she was simply\nfurious because Minnie had told me, and of course I was just boiling\nbecause Carrie had told her I\xe2\x80\x99d told her, and then we all met up at\nFulton\xe2\x80\x99s--his wife is away--thank heavens!--oh, there\xe2\x80\x99s the dandiest\nfloor in his house to dance on--and we were all of us simply furious at\neach other and-- Oh, I do hate that kind of a mix-up, don\xe2\x80\x99t you? I\nmean--it\xe2\x80\x99s so lacking in refinement, but-- And Mother wants to come and\nstay with me for a whole month, and of course I do love her, I suppose I\ndo, but honestly, she\xe2\x80\x99ll cramp my style something dreadful--she never\ncan learn not to comment, and she always wants to know where I\xe2\x80\x99m going\nwhen I go out evenings, and if I lie to her she always spies around and\nferrets around and finds out where I\xe2\x80\x99ve been, and then she looks like\nPatience on a Monument till I could just scream. And oh, I _must_ tell\nyou-- You know I _never_ talk about myself; I just hate people who do,\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t you? But-- I feel so stupid to-night, and I know I must be boring\nyou with all this but-- What would you do about Mother?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe gave her facile masculine advice. She was to put off her mother\xe2\x80\x99s\nstay. She was to tell Carrie to go to the deuce. For these valuable\nrevelations she thanked him, and they ambled into the familiar gossip of\nthe Bunch. Of what a sentimental fool was Carrie. Of what a lazy brat\nwas Pete. Of how nice Fulton Bemis could be--\xe2\x80\x9ccourse lots of people\nthink he\xe2\x80\x99s a regular old grouch when they meet him because he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t\ngive \xe2\x80\x99em the glad hand the first crack out of the box, but when they get\nto know him, he\xe2\x80\x99s a corker.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBut as they had gone conscientiously through each of these analyses\nbefore, the conversation staggered. Babbitt tried to be intellectual and\ndeal with General Topics. He said some thoroughly sound things about\nDisarmament, and broad-mindedness and liberalism; but it seemed to him\nthat General Topics interested Tanis only when she could apply them to\nPete, Carrie, or themselves. He was distressingly conscious of their\nsilence. He tried to stir her into chattering again, but silence rose\nlike a gray presence and hovered between them.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI, uh--\xe2\x80\x9d he labored. \xe2\x80\x9cIt strikes me--it strikes me that unemployment is\nlessening.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaybe Pete will get a decent job, then.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nSilence.\n\nDesperately he essayed, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the trouble, old honey? You seem kind of\nquiet to-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAm I? Oh, I\xe2\x80\x99m not. But--do you really care whether I am or not?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cCare? Sure! Course I do!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you really?\xe2\x80\x9d She swooped on him, sat on the arm of his chair.\n\nHe hated the emotional drain of having to appear fond of her. He\nstroked her hand, smiled up at her dutifully, and sank back.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGeorge, I wonder if you really like me at all?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cCourse I do, silly.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you really, precious? Do you care a bit?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy certainly! You don\xe2\x80\x99t suppose I\xe2\x80\x99d be here if I didn\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow see here, young man, I won\xe2\x80\x99t have you speaking to me in that huffy\nway!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean to sound huffy. I just--\xe2\x80\x9d In injured and rather childish\ntones: \xe2\x80\x9cGosh almighty, it makes me tired the way everybody says I sound\nhuffy when I just talk natural! Do they expect me to sing it or\nsomething?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho do you mean by \xe2\x80\x98everybody\xe2\x80\x99? How many other ladies have you been\nconsoling?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here now, I won\xe2\x80\x99t have this hinting!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHumbly: \xe2\x80\x9cI know, dear. I was only teasing. I know it didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean to talk\nhuffy--it was just tired. Forgive bad Tanis. But say you love me, say\nit!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI love you.... Course I do.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, you do!\xe2\x80\x9d cynically. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, darling, I don\xe2\x80\x99t mean to be rude but-- I\nget so lonely. I feel so useless. Nobody needs me, nothing I can do for\nanybody. And you know, dear, I\xe2\x80\x99m so active-- I could be if there was\nsomething to do. And I _am_ young, aren\xe2\x80\x99t I! I\xe2\x80\x99m not an old thing! I\xe2\x80\x99m\nnot old and stupid, am I?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe had to assure her. She stroked his hair, and he had to look pleased\nunder that touch, the more demanding in its beguiling softness. He was\nimpatient. He wanted to flee out to a hard, sure, unemotional man-world.\nThrough her delicate and caressing fingers she may have caught something\nof his shrugging distaste. She left him--he was for the moment buoyantly\nrelieved--she dragged a footstool to his feet and sat looking\nbeseechingly up at him. But as in many men the cringing of a dog, the\nflinching of a frightened child, rouse not pity but a surprised and\njerky cruelty, so her humility only annoyed him. And he saw her now as\nmiddle-aged, as beginning to be old. Even while he detested his own\nthoughts, they rode him. She was old, he winced. Old! He noted how the\nsoft flesh was creasing into webby folds beneath her chin, below her\neyes, at the base of her wrists. A patch of her throat had a minute\nroughness like the crumbs from a rubber eraser. Old! She was younger in\nyears than himself, yet it was sickening to have her yearning up at him\nwith rolling great eyes--as if, he shuddered, his own aunt were making\nlove to him.\n\nHe fretted inwardly, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m through with this asinine fooling around. I\xe2\x80\x99m\ngoing to cut her out. She\xe2\x80\x99s a darn decent nice woman, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t want\nto hurt her, but it\xe2\x80\x99ll hurt a lot less to cut her right out, like a good\nclean surgical operation.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe was on his feet. He was speaking urgently. By every rule of\nself-esteem, he had to prove to her, and to himself, that it was her\nfault.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose maybe I\xe2\x80\x99m kind of out of sorts to-night, but honest, honey,\nwhen I stayed away for a while to catch up on work and everything and\nfigure out where I was at, you ought to have been cannier and waited\ntill I came back. Can\xe2\x80\x99t you see, dear, when you _made_ me come, I--being\nabout an average bull-headed chump--my tendency was to resist? Listen,\ndear, I\xe2\x80\x99m going now--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot for a while, precious! No!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYep. Right now. And then sometime we\xe2\x80\x99ll see about the future.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean, dear, \xe2\x80\x98about the future\xe2\x80\x99? Have I done something I\noughtn\xe2\x80\x99t to? Oh, I\xe2\x80\x99m so dreadfully sorry!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe resolutely put his hands behind him. \xe2\x80\x9cNot a thing, God bless you, not\na thing. You\xe2\x80\x99re as good as they make \xe2\x80\x99em. But it\xe2\x80\x99s just-- Good Lord, do\nyou realize I\xe2\x80\x99ve got things to do in the world? I\xe2\x80\x99ve got a business to\nattend to and, you might not believe it, but I\xe2\x80\x99ve got a wife and kids\nthat I\xe2\x80\x99m awful fond of!\xe2\x80\x9d Then only during the murder he was committing\nwas he able to feel nobly virtuous. \xe2\x80\x9cI want us to be friends but, gosh,\nI can\xe2\x80\x99t go on this way feeling I _got_ to come up here every so often--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, darling, darling, and I\xe2\x80\x99ve always told you, so carefully, that you\nwere absolutely free. I just wanted you to come around when you were\ntired and wanted to talk to me, or when you could enjoy our parties--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nShe was so reasonable, she was so gently right! It took him an hour to\nmake his escape, with nothing settled and everything horribly settled.\nIn a barren freedom of icy Northern wind he sighed, \xe2\x80\x9cThank God that\xe2\x80\x99s\nover! Poor Tanis, poor darling decent Tanis! But it is over. Absolute!\nI\xe2\x80\x99m free!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\n\nCHAPTER XXXII\n\n\nI\n\nHis wife was up when he came in. \xe2\x80\x9cDid you have a good time?\xe2\x80\x9d she\nsniffed.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did not. I had a rotten time! Anything else I got to explain?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGeorge, how can you speak like-- Oh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what\xe2\x80\x99s come over you!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood Lord, there\xe2\x80\x99s nothing come over me! Why do you look for trouble\nall the time?\xe2\x80\x9d He was warning himself, \xe2\x80\x9cCareful! Stop being so\ndisagreeable. Course she feels it, being left alone here all evening.\xe2\x80\x9d\nBut he forgot his warning as she went on:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you go out and see all sorts of strange people? I suppose you\xe2\x80\x99ll\nsay you\xe2\x80\x99ve been to another committee-meeting this evening!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNope. I\xe2\x80\x99ve been calling on a woman. We sat by the fire and kidded each\nother and had a whale of a good time, if you want to know!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell-- From the way you say it, I suppose it\xe2\x80\x99s my fault you went there!\nI probably sent you!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou did!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, upon my word--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou hate \xe2\x80\x98strange people\xe2\x80\x99 as you call \xe2\x80\x99em. If you had your way, I\xe2\x80\x99d be\nas much of an old stick-in-the-mud as Howard Littlefield. You never want\nto have anybody with any git to \xe2\x80\x99em at the house; you want a bunch of\nold stiffs that sit around and gas about the weather. You\xe2\x80\x99re doing your\nlevel best to make me old. Well, let me tell you, I\xe2\x80\x99m not going to\nhave--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nOverwhelmed she bent to his unprecedented tirade, and in answer she\nmourned:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, dearest, I don\xe2\x80\x99t think that\xe2\x80\x99s true. I don\xe2\x80\x99t mean to make you old, I\nknow. Perhaps you\xe2\x80\x99re partly right. Perhaps I am slow about getting\nacquainted with new people.
