He went up to bed well pleased,\nnot only the master but the martyr of the household. For a distasteful\nmoment after he had lain down he wondered if he had been altogether\njust. \xe2\x80\x9cOught to be ashamed, bullying her. Maybe there is her side to\nthings. Maybe she hasn\xe2\x80\x99t had such a bloomin\xe2\x80\x99 hectic time herself. But I\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t care! Good for her to get waked up a little. And I\xe2\x80\x99m going to keep\nfree. Of her and Tanis and the fellows at the club and everybody. I\xe2\x80\x99m\ngoing to run my own life!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\nII\n\nIn this mood he was particularly objectionable at the Boosters\xe2\x80\x99 Club\nlunch next day. They were addressed by a congressman who had just\nreturned from an exhaustive three-months study of the finances,\nethnology, political systems, linguistic divisions, mineral resources,\nand agriculture of Germany, France, Great Britain, Italy, Austria,\nCzechoslovakia, Jugoslavia, and Bulgaria. He told them all about those\nsubjects, together with three funny stories about European\nmisconceptions of America and some spirited words on the necessity of\nkeeping ignorant foreigners out of America.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay, that was a mighty informative talk. Real he-stuff,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sidney\nFinkelstein.\n\nBut the disaffected Babbitt grumbled, \xe2\x80\x9cFour-flusher! Bunch of hot air!\nAnd what\xe2\x80\x99s the matter with the immigrants? Gosh, they aren\xe2\x80\x99t all\nignorant, and I got a hunch we\xe2\x80\x99re all descended from immigrants\nourselves.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you make me tired!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Finkelstein.\n\nBabbitt was aware that Dr. A. I. Dilling was sternly listening from\nacross the table. Dr. Dilling was one of the most important men in the\nBoosters\xe2\x80\x99. He was not a physician but a surgeon, a more romantic and\nsounding occupation. He was an intense large man with a boiling of black\nhair and a thick black mustache. The newspapers often chronicled his\noperations; he was professor of surgery in the State University; he went\nto dinner at the very best houses on Royal Ridge; and he was said to be\nworth several hundred thousand dollars. It was dismaying to Babbitt to\nhave such a person glower at him. He hastily praised the congressman\xe2\x80\x99s\nwit, to Sidney Finkelstein, but for Dr. Dilling\xe2\x80\x99s benefit.\n\n\nIII\n\nThat afternoon three men shouldered into Babbitt\xe2\x80\x99s office with the air\nof a Vigilante committee in frontier days. They were large, resolute,\nbig-jawed men, and they were all high lords in the land of Zenith--Dr.\nDilling the surgeon, Charles McKelvey the contractor, and, most\ndismaying of all, the white-bearded Colonel Rutherford Snow, owner of\nthe _Advocate-Times_. In their whelming presence Babbitt felt small and\ninsignificant.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, well, great pleasure, have chairs, what c\xe2\x80\x99n I do for you?\xe2\x80\x9d he\nbabbled.\n\nThey neither sat nor offered observations on the weather.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBabbitt,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colonel Snow, \xe2\x80\x9cwe\xe2\x80\x99ve come from the Good Citizens\xe2\x80\x99\nLeague. We\xe2\x80\x99ve decided we want you to join. Vergil Gunch says you don\xe2\x80\x99t\ncare to, but I think we can show you a new light. The League is going to\ncombine with the Chamber of Commerce in a campaign for the Open Shop,\nso it\xe2\x80\x99s time for you to put your name down.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nIn his embarrassment Babbitt could not recall his reasons for not\nwishing to join the League, if indeed he had ever definitely known them,\nbut he was passionately certain that he did not wish to join, and at the\nthought of their forcing him he felt a stirring of anger against even\nthese princes of commerce.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSorry, Colonel, have to think it over a little,\xe2\x80\x9d he mumbled.\n\nMcKelvey snarled, \xe2\x80\x9cThat means you\xe2\x80\x99re not going to join, George?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nSomething black and unfamiliar and ferocious spoke from Babbitt: \xe2\x80\x9cNow,\nyou look here, Charley! I\xe2\x80\x99m damned if I\xe2\x80\x99m going to be bullied into\njoining anything, not even by you plutes!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re not bullying anybody,\xe2\x80\x9d Dr. Billing began, but Colonel Snow thrust\nhim aside with, \xe2\x80\x9cCertainly we are! We don\xe2\x80\x99t mind a little bullying, if\nit\xe2\x80\x99s necessary. Babbitt, the G.C.L. has been talking about you a good\ndeal. You\xe2\x80\x99re supposed to be a sensible, clean, responsible man; you\nalways have been; but here lately, for God knows what reason, I hear\nfrom all sorts of sources that you\xe2\x80\x99re running around with a loose crowd,\nand what\xe2\x80\x99s a whole lot worse, you\xe2\x80\x99ve actually been advocating and\nsupporting some of the most dangerous elements in town, like this fellow\nDoane.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cColonel, that strikes me as my private business.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cPossibly, but we want to have an understanding. You\xe2\x80\x99ve stood in, you\nand your father-in-law, with some of the most substantial and\nforward-looking interests in town, like my friends of the Street\nTraction Company, and my papers have given you a lot of boosts. Well,\nyou can\xe2\x80\x99t expect the decent citizens to go on aiding you if you intend\nto side with precisely the people who are trying to undermine us.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBabbitt was frightened, but he had an agonized instinct that if he\nyielded in this he would yield in everything. He protested:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re exaggerating, Colonel. I believe in being broad-minded and\nliberal, but, of course, I\xe2\x80\x99m just as much agin the cranks and\nblatherskites and labor unions and so on as you are. But fact is, I\nbelong to so many organizations now that I can\xe2\x80\x99t do \xe2\x80\x99em justice, and I\nwant to think it over before I decide about coming into the G.C.L.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nColonel Snow condescended, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, no, I\xe2\x80\x99m not exaggerating! Why the doctor\nhere heard you cussing out and defaming one of the finest types of\nRepublican congressmen, just this noon! And you have entirely the wrong\nidea about \xe2\x80\x98thinking over joining.\xe2\x80\x99 We\xe2\x80\x99re not begging you to join the\nG.C.L.--we\xe2\x80\x99re permitting you to join. I\xe2\x80\x99m not sure, my boy, but what if\nyou put it off it\xe2\x80\x99ll be too late. I\xe2\x80\x99m not sure we\xe2\x80\x99ll want you then.\nBetter think quick--better think quick!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThe three Vigilantes, formidable in their righteousness, stared at him\nin a taut silence. Babbitt waited through. He thought nothing at all, he\nmerely waited, while in his echoing head buzzed, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want to\njoin-- I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to join-- I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right. Sorry for you!\xe2\x80\x9d said Colonel Snow, and the three men\nabruptly turned their beefy backs.\n\n\nIV\n\nAs Babbitt went out to his car that evening he saw Vergil Gunch coming\ndown the block. He raised his hand in salutation, but Gunch ignored it\nand crossed the street. He was certain that Gunch had seen him. He drove\nhome in sharp discomfort.\n\nHis wife attacked at once: \xe2\x80\x9cGeorgie dear, Muriel Frink was in this\nafternoon, and she says that Chum says the committee of this Good\nCitizens\xe2\x80\x99 League especially asked you to join and you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t. Don\xe2\x80\x99t\nyou think it would be better? You know all the nicest people belong, and\nthe League stands for--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know what the League stands for! It stands for the suppression of\nfree speech and free thought and everything else! I don\xe2\x80\x99t propose to be\nbullied and rushed into joining anything, and it isn\xe2\x80\x99t a question of\nwhether it\xe2\x80\x99s a good league or a bad league or what the hell kind of a\nleague it is; it\xe2\x80\x99s just a question of my refusing to be told I got to--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut dear, if you don\xe2\x80\x99t join, people might criticize you.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet \xe2\x80\x99em criticize!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I mean _nice_ people!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cRats, I-- Matter of fact, this whole League is just a fad. It\xe2\x80\x99s like all\nthese other organizations that start off with such a rush and let on\nthey\xe2\x80\x99re going to change the whole works, and pretty soon they peter out\nand everybody forgets all about \xe2\x80\x99em!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut if it\xe2\x80\x99s _the_ fad now, don\xe2\x80\x99t you think you--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I don\xe2\x80\x99t! Oh, Myra, please quit nagging me about it. I\xe2\x80\x99m sick of\nhearing about the confounded G.C.L. I almost wish I\xe2\x80\x99d joined it when\nVerg first came around, and got it over. And maybe I\xe2\x80\x99d\xe2\x80\x99ve come in to-day\nif the committee hadn\xe2\x80\x99t tried to bullyrag me, but, by God, as long as\nI\xe2\x80\x99m a free-born independent American cit--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, George, you\xe2\x80\x99re talking exactly like the German furnace-man.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I am, am I! Then, I won\xe2\x80\x99t talk at all!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe longed, that evening, to see Tanis Judique, to be strengthened by her\nsympathy. When all the family were up-stairs he got as far as\ntelephoning to her apartment-house, but he was agitated about it and\nwhen the janitor answered he blurted, \xe2\x80\x9cNev\xe2\x80\x99 mind-- I\xe2\x80\x99ll call later,\xe2\x80\x9d and\nhung up the receiver.\n\n\nV\n\nIf Babbitt had not been certain about Vergil Gunch\xe2\x80\x99s avoiding him, there\ncould be little doubt about William Washington Eathorne, next morning.\nWhen Babbitt was driving down to the office he overtook Eathorne\xe2\x80\x99s car,\nwith the great banker sitting in anemic solemnity behind his chauffeur.\nBabbitt waved and cried, \xe2\x80\x9cMornin\xe2\x80\x99!\xe2\x80\x9d Eathorne looked at him deliberately,\nhesitated, and gave him a nod more contemptuous than a direct cut.\n\nBabbitt\xe2\x80\x99s partner and father-in-law came in at ten:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGeorge, what\xe2\x80\x99s this I hear about some song and dance you gave Colonel\nSnow about not wanting to join the G.C.L.? What the dickens you trying\nto do? Wreck the firm? You don\xe2\x80\x99t suppose these Big Guns will stand your\nbucking them and springing all this \xe2\x80\x98liberal\xe2\x80\x99 poppycock you been getting\noff lately, do you?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, rats, Henry T., you been reading bum fiction. There ain\xe2\x80\x99t any such\na thing as these plots to keep folks from being liberal. This is a free\ncountry. A man can do anything he wants to.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cCourse th\xe2\x80\x99 ain\xe2\x80\x99t any plots. Who said they was? Only if folks get an\nidea you\xe2\x80\x99re scatter-brained and unstable, you don\xe2\x80\x99t suppose they\xe2\x80\x99ll want\nto do business with you, do you? One little rumor about your being a\ncrank would do more to ruin this business than all the plots and stuff\nthat these fool story-writers could think up in a month of Sundays.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThat afternoon, when the old reliable Conrad Lyte, the merry miser,\nConrad Lyte, appeared, and Babbitt suggested his buying a parcel of land\nin the new residential section of Dorchester, Lyte said hastily, too\nhastily, \xe2\x80\x9cNo, no, don\xe2\x80\x99t want to go into anything new just now.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nA week later Babbitt learned, through Henry Thompson, that the officials\nof the Street Traction Company were planning another real-estate coup,\nand that Sanders, Torrey and Wing, not the Babbitt-Thompson Company,\nwere to handle it for them.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI figure that Jake Offutt is kind of leery about the way folks are\ntalking about you. Of course Jake is a rock-ribbed old die-hard, and he\nprobably advised the Traction fellows to get some other broker. George,\nyou got to do something!\xe2\x80\x9d trembled Thompson.\n\nAnd, in a rush, Babbitt agreed. All nonsense the way people misjudged\nhim, but still-- He determined to join the Good Citizens\xe2\x80\x99 League the next\ntime he was asked, and in furious resignation he waited. He wasn\xe2\x80\x99t\nasked. They ignored him. He did not have the courage to go to the League\nand beg in, and he took refuge in a shaky boast that he had \xe2\x80\x9cgotten away\nwith bucking the whole city. Nobody could dictate to _him_ how he was\ngoing to think and act!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe was jarred as by nothing else when the paragon of stenographers, Miss\nMcGoun, suddenly left him, though her reasons were excellent--she needed\na rest, her sister was sick, she might not do any more work for six\nmonths. He was uncomfortable with her successor, Miss Havstad. What Miss\nHavstad\xe2\x80\x99s given name was, no one in the office ever knew. It seemed\nimprobable that she had a given name, a lover, a powder-puff, or a\ndigestion. She was so impersonal, this slight, pale, industrious Swede,\nthat it was vulgar to think of her as going to an ordinary home to eat\nhash. She was a perfectly oiled and enameled machine, and she ought,\neach evening, to have been dusted off and shut in her desk beside her\ntoo-slim, too-frail pencil points. She took dictation swiftly, her\ntyping was perfect, but Babbitt became jumpy when he tried to work with\nher. She made him feel puffy, and at his best-beloved daily jokes she\nlooked gently inquiring. He longed for Miss McGoun\xe2\x80\x99s return, and thought\nof writing to her.\n\nThen he heard that Miss McGoun had, a week after leaving him, gone over\nto his dangerous competitors, Sanders, Torrey and Wing.\n\nHe was not merely annoyed; he was frightened. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy did she quit, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\nhe worried. \xe2\x80\x9cDid she have a hunch my business is going on the rocks? And\nit was Sanders got the Street Traction deal. Rats--sinking ship!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nGray fear loomed always by him now. He watched Fritz Weilinger, the\nyoung salesman, and wondered if he too would leave. Daily he fancied\nslights. He noted that he was not asked to speak at the annual Chamber\nof Commerce dinner. When Orville Jones gave a large poker party and he\nwas not invited, he was certain that he had been snubbed. He was afraid\nto go to lunch at the Athletic Club, and afraid not to go. He believed\nthat he was spied on; that when he left the table they whispered about\nhim. Everywhere he heard the rustling whispers: in the offices of\nclients, in the bank when he made a deposit, in his own office, in his\nown home. Interminably he wondered what They were saying of him. All day\nlong in imaginary conversations he caught them marveling, \xe2\x80\x9cBabbitt? Why,\nsay, he\xe2\x80\x99s a regular anarchist! You got to admire the fellow for his\nnerve, the way he turned liberal and, by golly, just absolutely runs his\nlife to suit himself, but say, he\xe2\x80\x99s dangerous, that\xe2\x80\x99s what he is, and\nhe\xe2\x80\x99s got to be shown up.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe was so twitchy that when he rounded a corner and chanced on two\nacquaintances talking--whispering--his heart leaped, and he stalked by\nlike an embarrassed schoolboy. When he saw his neighbors Howard\nLittlefield and Orville Jones together, he peered at them, went indoors\nto escape their spying, and was miserably certain that they had been\nwhispering--plotting--whispering.\n\nThrough all his fear ran defiance. He felt stubborn. Sometimes he\ndecided that he had been a very devil of a fellow, as bold as Seneca\nDoane; sometimes he planned to call on Doane and tell him what a\nrevolutionist he was, and never got beyond the planning. But just as\noften, when he heard the soft whispers enveloping him he wailed, \xe2\x80\x9cGood\nLord, what have I done? Just played with the Bunch, and called down\nClarence Drum about being such a high-and-mighty sodger. Never catch\n_me_ criticizing people and trying to make them accept _my_ ideas!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe could not stand the strain. Before long he admitted that he would\nlike to flee back to the security of conformity, provided there was a\ndecent and creditable way to return. But, stubbornly, he would not be\nforced back; he would not, he swore, \xe2\x80\x9ceat dirt.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nOnly in spirited engagements with his wife did these turbulent fears\nrise to the surface. She complained that he seemed nervous, that she\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t understand why he did not want to \xe2\x80\x9cdrop in at the\nLittlefields\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d for the evening. He tried, but he could not express to\nher the nebulous facts of his rebellion and punishment. And, with Paul\nand Tanis lost, he had no one to whom he could talk. \xe2\x80\x9cGood Lord, Tinka\nis the only real friend I have, these days,\xe2\x80\x9d he sighed, and he clung to\nthe child, played floor-games with her all evening.\n\nHe considered going to see Paul in prison, but, though he had a pale\ncurt note from him every week, he thought of Paul as dead. It was Tanis\nfor whom he was longing.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought I was so smart and independent, cutting Tanis out, and I need\nher, Lord how I need her!\xe2\x80\x9d he raged. \xe2\x80\x9cMyra simply can\xe2\x80\x99t understand. All\nshe sees in life is getting along by being just like other folks. But\nTanis, she\xe2\x80\x99d tell me I was all right.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThen he broke, and one evening, late, he did run to Tanis. He had not\ndared to hope for it, but she was in, and alone. Only she wasn\xe2\x80\x99t Tanis.\nShe was a courteous, brow-lifting, ice-armored woman who looked like\nTanis. She said, \xe2\x80\x9cYes, George, what is it?\xe2\x80\x9d in even and uninterested\ntones, and he crept away, whipped.\n\nHis first comfort was from Ted and Eunice Littlefield.\n\nThey danced in one evening when Ted was home from the university, and\nTed chuckled, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s this I hear from Euny, dad? She says her dad says\nyou raised Cain by boosting old Seneca Doane. Hot dog! Give \xe2\x80\x99em fits!\nStir \xe2\x80\x99em up! This old burg is asleep!\xe2\x80\x9d Eunice plumped down on Babbitt\xe2\x80\x99s\nlap, kissed him, nestled her bobbed hair against his chin, and crowed,\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you\xe2\x80\x99re lots nicer than Howard. Why is it,\xe2\x80\x9d confidentially,\n\xe2\x80\x9cthat Howard is such an old grouch? The man has a good heart, and\nhonestly, he\xe2\x80\x99s awfully bright, but he never will learn to step on the\ngas, after all the training I\xe2\x80\x99ve given him. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you think we could do\nsomething with him, dearest?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Eunice, that isn\xe2\x80\x99t a nice way to speak of your papa,\xe2\x80\x9d Babbitt\nobserved, in the best Floral Heights manner, but he was happy for the\nfirst time in weeks. He pictured himself as the veteran liberal\nstrengthened by the loyalty of the young generation. They went out to\nrifle the ice-box. Babbitt gloated, \xe2\x80\x9cIf your mother caught us at this,\nwe\xe2\x80\x99d certainly get our come-uppance!\xe2\x80\x9d and Eunice became maternal,\nscrambled a terrifying number of eggs for them, kissed Babbitt on the\near, and in the voice of a brooding abbess marveled, \xe2\x80\x9cIt beats the devil\nwhy feminists like me still go on nursing these men!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThus stimulated, Babbitt was reckless when he encountered Sheldon\nSmeeth, educational director of the Y.M.C.A. and choir-leader of the\nChatham Road Church. With one of his damp hands Smeeth imprisoned\nBabbitt\xe2\x80\x99s thick paw while he chanted, \xe2\x80\x9cBrother Babbitt, we haven\xe2\x80\x99t seen\nyou at church very often lately. I know you\xe2\x80\x99re busy with a multitude of\ndetails, but you mustn\xe2\x80\x99t forget your dear friends at the old church\nhome.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBabbitt shook off the affectionate clasp--Sheldy liked to hold hands for\na long time--and snarled, \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I guess you fellows can run the show\nwithout me. Sorry, Smeeth; got to beat it. G\xe2\x80\x99day.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBut afterward he winced, \xe2\x80\x9cIf that white worm had the nerve to try to\ndrag me back to the Old Church Home, then the holy outfit must have been\ndoing a lot of talking about me, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe heard them whispering--whispering--Dr. John Jennison Drew,\nCholmondeley Frink, even William Washington Eathorne. The independence\nseeped out of him and he walked the streets alone, afraid of men\xe2\x80\x99s\ncynical eyes and the incessant hiss of whispering.\n\n\n\n\nCHAPTER XXXIII\n\n\nI\n\nHe tried to explain to his wife, as they prepared for bed, how\nobjectionable was Sheldon Smeeth, but all her answer was, \xe2\x80\x9cHe has such a\nbeautiful voice--so spiritual. I don\xe2\x80\x99t think you ought to speak of him\nlike that just because you can\xe2\x80\x99t appreciate music!\xe2\x80\x9d He saw her then as a\nstranger; he stared bleakly at this plump and fussy woman with the broad\nbare arms, and wondered how she had ever come here.\n\nIn his chilly cot, turning from aching side to side, he pondered of\nTanis. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99d been a fool to lose her. He had to have somebody he could\nreally talk to. He\xe2\x80\x99d--oh, he\xe2\x80\x99d _bust_ if he went on stewing about things\nby himself. And Myra, useless to expect her to understand. Well, rats,\nno use dodging the issue. Darn shame for two married people to drift\napart after all these years; darn rotten shame; but nothing could bring\nthem together now, as long as he refused to let Zenith bully him into\ntaking orders--and he was by golly not going to let anybody bully him\ninto anything, or wheedle him or coax him either!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe woke at three, roused by a passing motor, and struggled out of bed\nfor a drink of water. As he passed through the bedroom he heard his wife\ngroan. His resentment was night-blurred; he was solicitous in inquiring,\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the trouble, hon?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve got--such a pain down here in my side--oh, it\xe2\x80\x99s just--it tears at\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBad indigestion? Shall I get you some bicarb?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t think--that would help. I felt funny last evening and yesterday,\nand then--oh!--it passed away and I got to sleep and-- That auto woke me\nup.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHer voice was laboring like a ship in a storm. He was alarmed.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI better call the doctor.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no! It\xe2\x80\x99ll go away. But maybe you might get me an ice-bag.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe stalked to the bathroom for the ice-bag, down to the kitchen for ice.\nHe felt dramatic in this late-night expedition, but as he gouged the\nchunk of ice with the dagger-like pick he was cool, steady, mature; and\nthe old friendliness was in his voice as he patted the ice-bag into\nplace on her groin, rumbling, \xe2\x80\x9cThere, there, that\xe2\x80\x99ll be better now.\xe2\x80\x9d He\nretired to bed, but he did not sleep. He heard her groan again.\nInstantly he was up, soothing her, \xe2\x80\x9cStill pretty bad, honey?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it just gripes me, and I can\xe2\x80\x99t get to sleep.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHer voice was faint. He knew her dread of doctors\xe2\x80\x99 verdicts and he did\nnot inform her, but he creaked down-stairs, telephoned to Dr. Earl\nPatten, and waited, shivering, trying with fuzzy eyes to read a\nmagazine, till he heard the doctor\xe2\x80\x99s car.\n\nThe doctor was youngish and professionally breezy. He came in as though\nit were sunny noontime. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, George, little trouble, eh? How is she\nnow?\xe2\x80\x9d he said busily as, with tremendous and rather irritating\ncheerfulness, he tossed his coat on a chair and warmed his hands at a\nradiator. He took charge of the house. Babbitt felt ousted and\nunimportant as he followed the doctor up to the bedroom, and it was the\ndoctor who chuckled, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, just little stomach-ache\xe2\x80\x9d when Verona peeped\nthrough her door, begging, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it, Dad, what is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nTo Mrs. Babbitt the doctor said with amiable belligerence, after his\nexamination, \xe2\x80\x9cKind of a bad old pain, eh? I\xe2\x80\x99ll give you something to\nmake you sleep, and I think you\xe2\x80\x99ll feel better in the morning. I\xe2\x80\x99ll come\nin right after breakfast.\xe2\x80\x9d But to Babbitt, lying in wait in the lower\nhall, the doctor sighed, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like the feeling there in her belly.\nThere\xe2\x80\x99s some rigidity and some inflammation. She\xe2\x80\x99s never had her\nappendix out, has she? Um. Well, no use worrying. I\xe2\x80\x99ll be here first\nthing in the morning, and meantime she\xe2\x80\x99ll get some rest. I\xe2\x80\x99ve given her\na hypo. Good night.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThen was Babbitt caught up in the black tempest.\n\nInstantly all the indignations which had been dominating him and the\nspiritual dramas through which he had struggled became pallid and absurd\nbefore the ancient and overwhelming realities, the standard and\ntraditional realities, of sickness and menacing death, the long night,\nand the thousand steadfast implications of married life. He crept back\nto her. As she drowsed away in the tropic languor of morphia, he sat on\nthe edge of her bed, holding her hand, and for the first time in many\nweeks her hand abode trustfully in his.\n\nHe draped himself grotesquely in his toweling bathrobe and a pink and\nwhite couch-cover, and sat lumpishly in a wing-chair. The bedroom was\nuncanny in its half-light, which turned the curtains to lurking robbers,\nthe dressing-table to a turreted castle. It smelled of cosmetics, of\nlinen, of sleep. He napped and woke, napped and woke, a hundred times.\nHe heard her move and sigh in slumber; he wondered if there wasn\xe2\x80\x99t some\nofficious brisk thing he could do for her, and before he could quite\nform the thought he was asleep, racked and aching. The night was\ninfinite. When dawn came and the waiting seemed at an end, he fell\nasleep, and was vexed to have been caught off his guard, to have been\naroused by Verona\xe2\x80\x99s entrance and her agitated \xe2\x80\x9cOh, what _is_ it, Dad?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHis wife was awake, her face sallow and lifeless in the morning light,\nbut now he did not compare her with Tanis; she was not merely A Woman,\nto be contrasted with other women, but his own self, and though he might\ncriticize her and nag her, it was only as he might criticize and nag\nhimself, interestedly, unpatronizingly, without the expectation of\nchanging--or any real desire to change--the eternal essence.\n\nWith Verona he sounded fatherly again, and firm. He consoled Tinka, who\nsatisfactorily pointed the excitement of the hour by wailing. He ordered\nearly breakfast, and wanted to look at the newspaper, and felt somehow\nheroic and useful in not looking at it. But there were still crawling\nand totally unheroic hours of waiting before Dr. Patten returned.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t see much change,\xe2\x80\x9d said Patten. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll be back about eleven, and if\nyou don\xe2\x80\x99t mind, I think I\xe2\x80\x99ll bring in some other world-famous\npill-pedler for consultation, just to be on the safe side. Now George,\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s nothing you can do. I\xe2\x80\x99ll have Verona keep the ice-bag\nfilled--might as well leave that on, I guess--and you, you better beat\nit to the office instead of standing around her looking as if you were\nthe patient. The nerve of husbands! Lot more neurotic than the women!\nThey always have to horn in and get all the credit for feeling bad when\ntheir wives are ailing. Now have another nice cup of coffee and git!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nUnder this derision Babbitt became more matter-of-fact. He drove to the\noffice, tried to dictate letters, tried to telephone and, before the\ncall was answered, forgot to whom he was telephoning. At a quarter after\nten he returned home. As he left the down-town traffic and sped up the\ncar, his face was as grimly creased as the mask of tragedy.\n\nHis wife greeted him with surprise. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy did you come back, dear? I\nthink I feel a little better. I told Verona to skip off to her office.\nWas it wicked of me to go and get sick?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe knew that she wanted petting, and she got it, joyously. They were\ncuriously happy when he heard Dr. Patten\xe2\x80\x99s car in front. He looked out\nof the window. He was frightened. With Patten was an impatient man with\nturbulent black hair and a hussar mustache--Dr. A. I. Dilling, the\nsurgeon. Babbitt sputtered with anxiety, tried to conceal it, and\nhurried down to the door.\n\nDr. Patten was profusely casual: \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t want to worry you, old man, but\nI thought it might be a good stunt to have Dr. Dilling examine her.\xe2\x80\x9d He\ngestured toward Dilling as toward a master.\n\nBilling nodded in his curtest manner and strode up-stairs. Babbitt\ntramped the living-room in agony. Except for his wife\xe2\x80\x99s confinements\nthere had never been a major operation in the family, and to him surgery\nwas at once a miracle and an abomination of fear. But when Dilling and\nPatten came down again he knew that everything was all right, and he\nwanted to laugh, for the two doctors were exactly like the bearded\nphysicians in a musical comedy, both of them rubbing their hands and\nlooking foolishly sagacious.\n\nDr. Dilling spoke:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sorry, old man, but it\xe2\x80\x99s acute appendicitis. We ought to operate.\nOf course you must decide, but there\xe2\x80\x99s no question as to what has to be\ndone.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBabbitt did not get all the force of it. He mumbled, \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I suppose we\ncould get her ready in a couple o\xe2\x80\x99 days. Probably Ted ought to come down\nfrom the university, just in case anything happened.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nDr. Dilling growled, \xe2\x80\x9cNope. If you don\xe2\x80\x99t want peritonitis to set in,\nwe\xe2\x80\x99ll have to operate right away. I must advise it strongly. If you say\ngo ahead, I\xe2\x80\x99ll \xe2\x80\x99phone for the St. Mary\xe2\x80\x99s ambulance at once, and we\xe2\x80\x99ll\nhave her on the table in three-quarters of an hour.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI--I-- Of course, I suppose you know what-- But great God, man, I can\xe2\x80\x99t\nget her clothes ready and everything in two seconds, you know! And in\nher state, so wrought-up and weak--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust throw her hair-brush and comb and tooth-brush in a bag; that\xe2\x80\x99s all\nshe\xe2\x80\x99ll need for a day or two,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr. Dilling, and went to the\ntelephone.\n\nBabbitt galloped desperately up-stairs. He sent the frightened Tinka out\nof the room. He said gaily to his wife, \xe2\x80\x9cWell, old thing, the doc thinks\nmaybe we better have a little operation and get it over. Just take a\nfew minutes--not half as serious as a confinement--and you\xe2\x80\x99ll be all\nright in a jiffy.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nShe gripped his hand till the fingers ached. She said patiently, like a\ncowed child, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m afraid--to go into the dark, all alone!\xe2\x80\x9d Maturity was\nwiped from her eyes; they were pleading and terrified. \xe2\x80\x9cWill you stay\nwith me? Darling, you don\xe2\x80\x99t have to go to the office now, do you? Could\nyou just go down to the hospital with me? Could you come see me this\nevening--if everything\xe2\x80\x99s all right? You won\xe2\x80\x99t have to go out this\nevening, will you?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe was on his knees by the bed. While she feebly ruffled his hair, he\nsobbed, he kissed the lawn of her sleeve, and swore, \xe2\x80\x9cOld honey, I love\nyou more than anything in the world! I\xe2\x80\x99ve kind of been worried by\nbusiness and everything, but that\xe2\x80\x99s all over now, and I\xe2\x80\x99m back again.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you really? George, I was thinking, lying here, maybe it would be a\ngood thing if I just _went_. I was wondering if anybody really needed\nme. Or wanted me. I was wondering what was the use of my living. I\xe2\x80\x99ve\nbeen getting so stupid and ugly--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you old humbug! Fishing for compliments when I ought to be packing\nyour bag! Me, sure, I\xe2\x80\x99m young and handsome and a regular village cut-up\nand--\xe2\x80\x9d He could not go on. He sobbed again; and in muttered\nincoherencies they found each other.\n\nAs he packed, his brain was curiously clear and swift. He\xe2\x80\x99d have no more\nwild evenings, he realized. He admitted that he would regret them. A\nlittle grimly he perceived that this had been his last despairing fling\nbefore the paralyzed contentment of middle-age. Well, and he grinned\nimpishly, \xe2\x80\x9cit was one doggone good party while it lasted!\xe2\x80\x9d And--how much\nwas the operation going to cost? \xe2\x80\x9cI ought to have fought that out with\nDilling. But no, damn it, I don\xe2\x80\x99t care how much it costs!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThe motor ambulance was at the door. Even in his grief the Babbitt who\nadmired all technical excellences was interested in the kindly skill\nwith which the attendants slid Mrs. Babbitt upon a stretcher and carried\nher down-stairs. The ambulance was a huge, suave, varnished, white\nthing. Mrs. Babbitt moaned, \xe2\x80\x9cIt frightens me. It\xe2\x80\x99s just like a hearse,\njust like being put in a hearse. I want you to stay with me.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll be right up front with the driver,\xe2\x80\x9d Babbitt promised.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I want you to stay inside with me.\xe2\x80\x9d To the attendants: \xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t he be\ninside?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSure, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, you bet. There\xe2\x80\x99s a fine little camp-stool in there,\xe2\x80\x9d the\nolder attendant said, with professional pride.\n\nHe sat beside her in that traveling cabin with its cot, its stool, its\nactive little electric radiator, and its quite unexplained calendar,\ndisplaying a girl eating cherries, and the name of an enterprising\ngrocer. But as he flung out his hand in hopeless cheerfulness it touched\nthe radiator, and he squealed:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOuch! Jesus!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, George Babbitt, I won\xe2\x80\x99t have you cursing and swearing and\nblaspheming!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know, awful sorry but-- Gosh all fish-hooks, look how I burned my\nhand! Gee whiz, it hurts! It hurts like the mischief! Why, that damn\nradiator is hot as--it\xe2\x80\x99s hot as--it\xe2\x80\x99s hotter \xe2\x80\x99n the hinges of Hades!\nLook! You can see the mark!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nSo, as they drove up to St. Mary\xe2\x80\x99s Hospital, with the nurses already\nlaying out the instruments for an operation to save her life, it was she\nwho consoled him and kissed the place to make it well, and though he\ntried to be gruff and mature, he yielded to her and was glad to be\nbabied.\n\nThe ambulance whirled under the hooded carriage-entrance of the\nhospital, and instantly he was reduced to a zero in the nightmare\nsuccession of cork-floored halls, endless doors open on old women\nsitting up in bed, an elevator, the anesthetizing room, a young interne\ncontemptuous of husbands. He was permitted to kiss his wife; he saw a\nthin dark nurse fit the cone over her mouth and nose; he stiffened at a\nsweet and treacherous odor; then he was driven out, and on a high stool\nin a laboratory he sat dazed, longing to see her once again, to insist\nthat he had always loved her, had never for a second loved anybody else\nor looked at anybody else. In the laboratory he was conscious only of a\ndecayed object preserved in a bottle of yellowing alcohol. It made him\nvery sick, but he could not take his eyes from it. He was more aware of\nit than of waiting. His mind floated in abeyance, coming back always to\nthat horrible bottle. To escape it he opened the door to the right,\nhoping to find a sane and business-like office. He realized that he was\nlooking into the operating-room; in one glance he took in Dr. Dilling,\nstrange in white gown and bandaged head, bending over the steel table\nwith its screws and wheels, then nurses holding basins and cotton\nsponges, and a swathed thing, just a lifeless chin and a mound of white\nin the midst of which was a square of sallow flesh with a gash a little\nbloody at the edges, protruding from the gash a cluster of forceps like\nclinging parasites.\n\nHe shut the door with haste. It may be that his frightened repentance of\nthe night and morning had not eaten in, but this dehumanizing interment\nof her who had been so pathetically human shook him utterly, and as he\ncrouched again on the high stool in the laboratory he swore faith to his\nwife... to Zenith... to business efficiency... to the Boosters\xe2\x80\x99 Club...\nto every faith of the Clan of Good Fellows.\n\nThen a nurse was soothing, \xe2\x80\x9cAll over! Perfect success! She\xe2\x80\x99ll come out\nfine! She\xe2\x80\x99ll be out from under the anesthetic soon, and you can see\nher.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe found her on a curious tilted bed, her face an unwholesome yellow but\nher purple lips moving slightly. Then only did he really believe that\nshe was alive. She was muttering. He bent, and heard her sighing, \xe2\x80\x9cHard\nget real maple syrup for pancakes.\xe2\x80\x9d He laughed inexhaustibly; he beamed\non the nurse and proudly confided, \xe2\x80\x9cThink of her talking about maple\nsyrup! By golly, I\xe2\x80\x99m going to go and order a hundred gallons of it,\nright from Vermont!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\nII\n\nShe was out of the hospital in seventeen days. He went to see her each\nafternoon, and in their long talks they drifted back to intimacy. Once\nhe hinted something of his relations to Tanis and the Bunch, and she was\ninflated by the view that a Wicked Woman had captivated her poor George.\n\nIf once he had doubted his neighbors and the supreme charm of the Good\nFellows, he was convinced now. You didn\xe2\x80\x99t, he noted, \xe2\x80\x9csee Seneca Doane\ncoming around with any flowers or dropping in to chat with the Missus,\xe2\x80\x9d\nbut Mrs. Howard Littlefield brought to the hospital her priceless wine\njelly (flavored with real wine); Orville Jones spent hours in picking\nout the kind of novels Mrs. Babbitt liked--nice love stories about New\nYork millionaries and Wyoming cowpunchers; Louetta Swanson knitted a\npink bed-jacket; Sidney Finkelstein and his merry brown-eyed flapper of\na wife selected the prettiest nightgown in all the stock of Parcher and\nStein.\n\nAll his friends ceased whispering about him, suspecting him. At the\nAthletic Club they asked after her daily. Club members whose names he\ndid not know stopped him to inquire, \xe2\x80\x9cHow\xe2\x80\x99s your good lady getting on?\xe2\x80\x9d\nBabbitt felt that he was swinging from bleak uplands down into the rich\nwarm air of a valley pleasant with cottages.\n\nOne noon Vergil Gunch suggested, \xe2\x80\x9cYou planning to be at the hospital\nabout six? The wife and I thought we\xe2\x80\x99d drop in.\xe2\x80\x9d They did drop in. Gunch\nwas so humorous that Mrs. Babbitt said he must \xe2\x80\x9cstop making her laugh\nbecause honestly it was hurting her incision.\xe2\x80\x9d As they passed down the\nhall Gunch demanded amiably, \xe2\x80\x9cGeorge, old scout, you were soreheaded\nabout something, here a while back. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know why, and it\xe2\x80\x99s none of\nmy business. But you seem to be feeling all hunky-dory again, and why\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t you come join us in the Good Citizens\xe2\x80\x99 League, old man? We have\nsome corking times together, and we need your advice.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThen did Babbitt, almost tearful with joy at being coaxed instead of\nbullied, at being permitted to stop fighting, at being able to desert\nwithout injuring his opinion of himself, cease utterly to be a domestic\nrevolutionist. He patted Gunch\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder, and next day he became a\nmember of the Good Citizens\xe2\x80\x99 League.\n\nWithin two weeks no one in the League was more violent regarding the\nwickedness of Seneca Doane, the crimes of labor unions, the perils of\nimmigration, and the delights of golf, morality, and bank-accounts than\nwas George F. Babbitt.\n\n\n\n\nCHAPTER XXXIV\n\n\nI\n\nThe Good Citizens\xe2\x80\x99 League had spread through the country, but nowhere\nwas it so effective and well esteemed as in cities of the type of\nZenith, commercial cities of a few hundred thousand inhabitants, most of\nwhich--though not all--lay inland, against a background of cornfields\nand mines and of small towns which depended upon them for\nmortgage-loans, table-manners, art, social philosophy and millinery.\n\nTo the League belonged most of the prosperous citizens of Zenith. They\nwere not all of the kind who called themselves \xe2\x80\x9cRegular Guys.\xe2\x80\x9d Besides\nthese hearty fellows, these salesmen of prosperity, there were the\naristocrats, that is, the men who were richer or had been rich for more\ngenerations: the presidents of banks and of factories, the land-owners,\nthe corporation lawyers, the fashionable doctors, and the few young-old\nmen who worked not at all but, reluctantly remaining in Zenith,\ncollected luster-ware and first editions as though they were back in\nParis. All of them agreed that the working-classes must be kept in their\nplace; and all of them perceived that American Democracy did not imply\nany equality of wealth, but did demand a wholesome sameness of thought,\ndress, painting, morals, and vocabulary.\n\nIn this they were like the ruling-class of any other country,\nparticularly of Great Britain, but they differed in being more vigorous\nand in actually trying to produce the accepted standards which all\nclasses, everywhere, desire, but usually despair of realizing.\n\nThe longest struggle of the Good Citizens\xe2\x80\x99 League was against the Open\nShop--which was secretly a struggle against all union labor.\nAccompanying it was an Americanization Movement, with evening classes in\nEnglish and history and economics, and daily articles in the newspapers,\nso that newly arrived foreigners might learn that the true-blue and one\nhundred per cent. American way of settling labor-troubles was for\nworkmen to trust and love their employers.\n\nThe League was more than generous in approving other organizations which\nagreed with its aims. It helped the Y.M.C.A. to raise a\ntwo-hundred-thousand-dollar fund for a new building. Babbitt, Vergil\nGunch, Sidney Finkelstein, and even Charles McKelvey told the spectators\nat movie theaters how great an influence for manly Christianity the\n\xe2\x80\x9cgood old Y.\xe2\x80\x9d had been in their own lives; and the hoar and mighty\nColonel Rutherford Snow, owner of the _Advocate-Times_, was photographed\nclasping the hand of Sheldon Smeeth of the Y.M.C.A. It is true that\nafterward, when Smeeth lisped, \xe2\x80\x9cYou must come to one of our\nprayer-meetings,\xe2\x80\x9d the ferocious Colonel bellowed, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat the hell would I\ndo that for? I\xe2\x80\x99ve got a bar of my own,\xe2\x80\x9d but this did not appear in the\npublic prints.\n\nThe League was of value to the American Legion at a time when certain of\nthe lesser and looser newspapers were criticizing that organization of\nveterans of the Great War. One evening a number of young men raided the\nZenith Socialist Headquarters, burned its records, beat the office\nstaff, and agreeably dumped desks out of the window. All of the\nnewspapers save the _Advocate-Times_ and the _Evening Advocate_\nattributed this valuable but perhaps hasty direct-action to the American\nLegion. Then a flying squadron from the Good Citizens\xe2\x80\x99 League called on\nthe unfair papers and explained that no ex-soldier could possibly do\nsuch a thing, and the editors saw the light, and retained their\nadvertising. When Zenith\xe2\x80\x99s lone Conscientious Objector came home from\nprison and was righteously run out of town, the newspapers referred to\nthe perpetrators as an \xe2\x80\x9cunidentified mob.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\nII\n\nIn all the activities and triumphs of the Good Citizens\xe2\x80\x99 League Babbitt\ntook part, and completely won back to self-respect, placidity, and the\naffection of his friends. But he began to protest, \xe2\x80\x9cGosh, I\xe2\x80\x99ve done my\nshare in cleaning up the city. I want to tend to business. Think I\xe2\x80\x99ll\njust kind of slacken up on this G.C.L. stuff now.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe had returned to the church as he had returned to the Boosters\xe2\x80\x99 Club.\nHe had even endured the lavish greeting which Sheldon Smeeth gave him.\nHe was worried lest during his late discontent he had imperiled his\nsalvation. He was not quite sure there was a Heaven to be attained, but\nDr. John Jennison Drew said there was, and Babbitt was not going to take\na chance.\n\nOne evening when he was walking past Dr. Drew\xe2\x80\x99s parsonage he impulsively\nwent in and found the pastor in his study.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cJus\xe2\x80\x99 minute--getting \xe2\x80\x99phone call,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr. Drew in business-like\ntones, then, aggressively, to the telephone: \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Lo--\xe2\x80\x99lo! This Berkey and\nHannis? Reverend Drew speaking. Where the dickens is the proof for next\nSunday\xe2\x80\x99s calendar? Huh? Y\xe2\x80\x99 ought to have it here. Well, I can\xe2\x80\x99t help it\nif they\xe2\x80\x99re _all_ sick! I got to have it to-night. Get an A.D.T. boy and\nshoot it up here quick.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe turned, without slackening his briskness. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, Brother Babbitt,\nwhat c\xe2\x80\x99n I do for you?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI just wanted to ask-- Tell you how it is, dominie: Here a while ago I\nguess I got kind of slack. Took a few drinks and so on. What I wanted to\nask is: How is it if a fellow cuts that all out and comes back to his\nsenses? Does it sort of, well, you might say, does it score against him\nin the long run?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThe Reverend Dr. Drew was suddenly interested. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd, uh, brother--the\nother things, too? Women?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, practically, you might say, practically not at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t hesitate to tell me, brother! That\xe2\x80\x99s what I\xe2\x80\x99m here for. Been\ngoing on joy-rides? Squeezing girls in cars?\xe2\x80\x9d The reverend eyes\nglistened.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo--no--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you. I\xe2\x80\x99ve got a deputation from the Don\xe2\x80\x99t Make\nProhibition a Joke Association coming to see me in a quarter of an hour,\nand one from the Anti-Birth-Control Union at a quarter of ten.\xe2\x80\x9d He\nbusily glanced at his watch. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I can take five minutes off and pray\nwith you. Kneel right down by your chair, brother. Don\xe2\x80\x99t be ashamed to\nseek the guidance of God.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBabbitt\xe2\x80\x99s scalp itched and he longed to flee, but Dr. Drew had already\nflopped down beside his desk-chair and his voice had changed from\nrasping efficiency to an unctuous familiarity with sin and with the\nAlmighty. Babbitt also knelt, while Drew gloated:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cO Lord, thou seest our brother here, who has been led astray by\nmanifold temptations. O Heavenly Father, make his heart to be pure, as\npure as a little child\xe2\x80\x99s. Oh, let him know again the joy of a manly\ncourage to abstain from evil--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nSheldon Smeeth came frolicking into the study. At the sight of the two\nmen he smirked, forgivingly patted Babbitt on the shoulder, and knelt\nbeside him, his arm about him, while he authorized Dr. Drew\xe2\x80\x99s\nimprecations with moans of \xe2\x80\x9cYes, Lord! Help our brother, Lord!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThough he was trying to keep his eyes closed, Babbitt squinted between\nhis fingers and saw the pastor glance at his watch as he concluded with\na triumphant, \xe2\x80\x9cAnd let him never be afraid to come to Us for counsel and\ntender care, and let him know that the church can lead him as a little\nlamb.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nDr. Drew sprang up, rolled his eyes in the general direction of Heaven,\nchucked his watch into his pocket, and demanded, \xe2\x80\x9cHas the deputation\ncome yet, Sheldy?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYep, right outside,\xe2\x80\x9d Sheldy answered, with equal liveliness; then,\ncaressingly, to Babbitt, \xe2\x80\x9cBrother, if it would help, I\xe2\x80\x99d love to go\ninto the next room and pray with you while Dr. Drew is receiving the\nbrothers from the Don\xe2\x80\x99t Make Prohibition a Joke Association.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo--no thanks--can\xe2\x80\x99t take the time!\xe2\x80\x9d yelped Babbitt, rushing toward the\ndoor.\n\nThereafter he was often seen at the Chatham Road Presbyterian Church,\nbut it is recorded that he avoided shaking hands with the pastor at the\ndoor.\n\n\nIII\n\nIf his moral fiber had been so weakened by rebellion that he was not\nquite dependable in the more rigorous campaigns of the Good Citizens\xe2\x80\x99\nLeague nor quite appreciative of the church, yet there was no doubt of\nthe joy with which Babbitt returned to the pleasures of his home and of\nthe Athletic Club, the Boosters, the Elks.\n\nVerona and Kenneth Escott were eventually and hesitatingly married. For\nthe wedding Babbitt was dressed as carefully as was Verona; he was\ncrammed into the morning-coat he wore to teas thrice a year; and with a\ncertain relief, after Verona and Kenneth had driven away in a limousine,\nhe returned to the house, removed the morning coat, sat with his aching\nfeet up on the davenport, and reflected that his wife and he could have\nthe living-room to themselves now, and not have to listen to Verona and\nKenneth worrying, in a cultured collegiate manner, about minimum wages\nand the Drama League.\n\nBut even this sinking into peace was less consoling than his return to\nbeing one of the best-loved men in the Boosters\xe2\x80\x99 Club.\n\n\nIV\n\nPresident Willis Ijams began that Boosters\xe2\x80\x99 Club luncheon by standing\nquiet and staring at them so unhappily that they feared he was about to\nannounce the death of a Brother Booster. He spoke slowly then, and\ngravely:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBoys, I have something shocking to reveal to you; something terrible\nabout one of our own members.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nSeveral Boosters, including Babbitt, looked disconcerted.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cA knight of the grip, a trusted friend of mine, recently made a trip\nup-state, and in a certain town, where a certain Booster spent his\nboyhood, he found out something which can no longer be concealed. In\nfact, he discovered the inward nature of a man whom we have accepted as\na Real Guy and as one of us. Gentlemen, I cannot trust my voice to say\nit, so I have written it down.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe uncovered a large blackboard and on it, in huge capitals, was the\nlegend:\n\n               George Follansbee Babbitt--oh you Folly!\n\nThe Boosters cheered, they laughed, they wept, they threw rolls at\nBabbitt, they cried, \xe2\x80\x9cSpeech, speech! Oh you Folly!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nPresident Ijams continued:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat, gentlemen, is the awful thing Georgie Babbitt has been concealing\nall these years, when we thought he was just plain George F. Now I want\nyou to tell us, taking it in turn, what you\xe2\x80\x99ve always supposed the F.\nstood for.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nFlivver, they suggested, and Frog-face and Flathead and Farinaceous and\nFreezone and Flapdoodle and Foghorn. By the joviality of their insults\nBabbitt knew that he had been taken back to their hearts, and happily he\nrose.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBoys, I\xe2\x80\x99ve got to admit it. I\xe2\x80\x99ve never worn a wrist-watch, or parted my\nname in the middle, but I will confess to \xe2\x80\x98Follansbee.\xe2\x80\x99 My only\njustification is that my old dad--though otherwise he was perfectly\nsane, and packed an awful wallop when it came to trimming the City\nFellers at checkers--named me after the family doc, old Dr. Ambrose\nFollansbee. I apologize, boys. In my next what-d\xe2\x80\x99you-call-it I\xe2\x80\x99ll see to\nit that I get named something really practical--something that sounds\nswell and yet is good and virile--something, in fact, like that grand\nold name so familiar to every household--that bold and almost\noverpowering name, Willis Jimjams Ijams!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe knew by the cheer that he was secure again and popular; he knew that\nhe would no more endanger his security and popularity by straying from\nthe Clan of Good Fellows.\n\n\nV\n\nHenry Thompson dashed into the office, clamoring, \xe2\x80\x9cGeorge! Big news!\nJake Offutt says the Traction Bunch are dissatisfied with the way\nSanders, Torrey and Wing handled their last deal, and they\xe2\x80\x99re willing to\ndicker with us!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBabbitt was pleased in the realization that the last scar of his\nrebellion was healed, yet as he drove home he was annoyed by such\nbackground thoughts as had never weakened him in his days of belligerent\nconformity. He discovered that he actually did not consider the Traction\ngroup quite honest. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, he\xe2\x80\x99d carry out one more deal for them, but as\nsoon as it was practicable, maybe as soon as old Henry Thompson died,\nhe\xe2\x80\x99d break away from all association from them. He was forty-eight; in\ntwelve years he\xe2\x80\x99d be sixty; he wanted to leave a clean business to his\ngrandchildren. Course there was a lot of money in negotiating for the\nTraction people, and a fellow had to look at things in a practical way,\nonly--\xe2\x80\x9d He wriggled uncomfortably. He wanted to tell the Traction group\nwhat he thought of them. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t do it, not now. If he offended\nthem this second time, they would crush him. But--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe was conscious that his line of progress seemed confused. He wondered\nwhat he would do with his future. He was still young; was he through\nwith all adventuring? He felt that he had been trapped into the very net\nfrom which he had with such fury escaped and, supremest jest of all,\nbeen made to rejoice in the trapping.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99ve licked me; licked me to a finish!\xe2\x80\x9d he whimpered.\n\nThe house was peaceful, that evening, and he enjoyed a game of pinochle\nwith his wife. He indignantly told the Tempter that he was content to\ndo things in the good old-fashioned way. The day after, he went to see\nthe purchasing-agent of the Street Traction Company and they made plans\nfor the secret purchase of lots along the Evanston Road. But as he drove\nto his office he struggled, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m going to run things and figure out\nthings to suit myself--when I retire.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\nVI\n\nTed had come down from the University for the week-end. Though he no\nlonger spoke of mechanical engineering and though he was reticent about\nhis opinion of his instructors, he seemed no more reconciled to college,\nand his chief interest was his wireless telephone set.\n\nOn Saturday evening he took Eunice Littlefield to a dance at Devon\nWoods. Babbitt had a glimpse of her, bouncing in the seat of the car,\nbrilliant in a scarlet cloak over a frock of thinnest creamy silk. They\ntwo had not returned when the Babbitts went to bed, at half-past eleven.\nAt a blurred indefinite time of late night Babbitt was awakened by the\nring of the telephone and gloomily crawled down-stairs. Howard\nLittlefield was speaking:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGeorge, Euny isn\xe2\x80\x99t back yet. Is Ted?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo--at least his door is open--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey ought to be home. Eunice said the dance would be over at midnight.\nWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the name of those people where they\xe2\x80\x99re going?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, gosh, tell the truth, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know, Howard. It\xe2\x80\x99s some classmate of\nTed\xe2\x80\x99s, out in Devon Woods. Don\xe2\x80\x99t see what we can do. Wait, I\xe2\x80\x99ll skip up\nand ask Myra if she knows their name.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBabbitt turned on the light in Ted\xe2\x80\x99s room. It was a brown boyish room;\ndisordered dresser, worn books, a high-school pennant, photographs of\nbasket-ball teams and baseball teams. Ted was decidedly not there.\n\nMrs. Babbitt, awakened, irritably observed that she certainly did not\nknow the name of Ted\xe2\x80\x99s host, that it was late, that Howard Littlefield\nwas but little better than a born fool, and that she was sleepy. But she\nremained awake and worrying while Babbitt, on the sleeping-porch,\nstruggled back into sleep through the incessant soft rain of her\nremarks. It was after dawn when he was aroused by her shaking him and\ncalling \xe2\x80\x9cGeorge! George!\xe2\x80\x9d in something like horror.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWha--wha--what is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome here quick and see. Be quiet!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nShe led him down the hall to the door of Ted\xe2\x80\x99s room and pushed it gently\nopen. On the worn brown rug he saw a froth of rose-colored chiffon\nlingerie; on the sedate Morris chair a girl\xe2\x80\x99s silver slipper. And on the\npillows were two sleepy heads--Ted\xe2\x80\x99s and Eunice\xe2\x80\x99s.\n\nTed woke to grin, and to mutter with unconvincing defiance, \xe2\x80\x9cGood\nmorning! Let me introduce my wife--Mrs. Theodore Roosevelt Eunice\nLittlefield Babbitt, Esquiress.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood God!\xe2\x80\x9d from Babbitt, and from his wife a long wailing, \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve gone\nand--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe got married last evening. Wife! Sit up and say a pretty good morning\nto mother-in-law.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBut Eunice hid her shoulders and her charming wild hair under the\npillow.\n\nBy nine o\xe2\x80\x99clock the assembly which was gathered about Ted and Eunice in\nthe living-room included Mr. and Mrs. George Babbitt, Dr. and Mrs.\nHoward Littlefield, Mr. and Mrs. Kenneth Escott, Mr. and Mrs. Henry T.\nThompson, and Tinka Babbitt, who was the only pleased member of the\ninquisition.\n\nA crackling shower of phrases filled the room:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt their age--\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cOught to be annulled--\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cNever heard of such a thing\nin--\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cFault of both of them and--\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cKeep it out of the papers--\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cOught\nto be packed off to school--\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cDo something about it at once, and what I\nsay is--\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cDamn good old-fashioned spanking--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nWorst of them all was Verona. \xe2\x80\x9c_Ted!_ Some way _must_ be found to make\nyou under_stand_ how dreadfully _serious_ this is, instead of standing\n_around_ with that silly foolish _smile_ on your face!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe began to revolt. \xe2\x80\x9cGee whittakers, Rone, you got married yourself,\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s entirely different.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou bet it is! They didn\xe2\x80\x99t have to work on Eu and me with a chain and\ntackle to get us to hold hands!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, young man, we\xe2\x80\x99ll have no more flippancy,\xe2\x80\x9d old Henry Thompson\nordered. \xe2\x80\x9cYou listen to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou listen to Grandfather!\xe2\x80\x9d said Verona.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, listen to your Grandfather!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Babbitt.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cTed, you listen to Mr. Thompson!\xe2\x80\x9d said Howard Littlefield.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, for the love o\xe2\x80\x99 Mike, I am listening!\xe2\x80\x9d Ted shouted. \xe2\x80\x9cBut you look\nhere, all of you! I\xe2\x80\x99m getting sick and tired of being the corpse in this\npost mortem! If you want to kill somebody, go kill the preacher that\nmarried us! Why, he stung me five dollars, and all the money I had in\nthe world was six dollars and two bits. I\xe2\x80\x99m getting just about enough of\nbeing hollered at!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nA new voice, booming, authoritative, dominated the room. It was Babbitt.\n\xe2\x80\x9cYuh, there\xe2\x80\x99s too darn many putting in their oar! Rone, you dry up.\nHoward and I are still pretty strong, and able to do our own cussing.\nTed, come into the dining-room and we\xe2\x80\x99ll talk this over.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nIn the dining-room, the door firmly closed, Babbitt walked to his son,\nput both hands on his shoulders. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re more or less right. They all\ntalk too much. Now what do you plan to do, old man?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGosh, dad, are you really going to be human?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I-- Remember one time you called us \xe2\x80\x98the Babbitt men\xe2\x80\x99 and said we\nought to stick together? I want to. I don\xe2\x80\x99t pretend to think this isn\xe2\x80\x99t\nserious. The way the cards are stacked against a young fellow to-day, I\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t say I approve of early marriages. But you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t have married a\nbetter girl than Eunice; and way I figure it, Littlefield is darn lucky\nto get a Babbitt for a son-in-law! But what do you plan to do? Course\nyou could go right ahead with the U., and when you\xe2\x80\x99d finished--\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDad, I can\xe2\x80\x99t stand it any more. Maybe it\xe2\x80\x99s all right for some fellows.\nMaybe I\xe2\x80\x99ll want to go back some day. But me, I want to get into\nmechanics. I think I\xe2\x80\x99d get to be a good inventor. There\xe2\x80\x99s a fellow that\nwould give me twenty dollars a week in a factory right now.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell--\xe2\x80\x9d Babbitt crossed the floor, slowly, ponderously, seeming a\nlittle old. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve always wanted you to have a college degree.\xe2\x80\x9d He\nmeditatively stamped across the floor again. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I\xe2\x80\x99ve never-- Now, for\nheaven\xe2\x80\x99s sake, don\xe2\x80\x99t repeat this to your mother, or she\xe2\x80\x99d remove what\nlittle hair I\xe2\x80\x99ve got left, but practically, I\xe2\x80\x99ve never done a single\nthing I\xe2\x80\x99ve wanted to in my whole life! I don\xe2\x80\x99t know \xe2\x80\x99s I\xe2\x80\x99ve accomplished\nanything except just get along. I figure out I\xe2\x80\x99ve made about a quarter\nof an inch out of a possible hundred rods. Well, maybe you\xe2\x80\x99ll carry\nthings on further. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know. But I do get a kind of sneaking\npleasure out of the fact that you knew what you wanted to do and did it.\nWell, those folks in there will try to bully you, and tame you down.\nTell \xe2\x80\x99em to go to the devil! I\xe2\x80\x99ll back you. Take your factory job, if\nyou want to. Don\xe2\x80\x99t be scared of the family. No, nor all of Zenith. Nor\nof yourself, the way I\xe2\x80\x99ve been. Go ahead, old man! The world is yours!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nArms about each other\xe2\x80\x99s shoulders, the Babbitt men marched into the\nliving-room and faced the swooping family.\n\n*** END OF THE PROJECT GUTENBERG EBOOK BABBITT ***\n\nUpdated editions will replace the previous one--the old editions will\nbe renamed.\n\nCreating the works from print editions not protected by U.S. copyright\nlaw means that no one owns a United States copyright in these works,\nso the Foundation (and you!) can copy and distribute it in the\nUnited States without permission and without paying copyright\nroyalties.
